==PAGE:3==
Preface
The current thesis is prepared in the spring and summer of 2025 and serves as the com-
pletion of the education Master in Fire Safety at the Technical University of Denmark -
DTU.
The content of the report is motivated by real-world challenges and experiences. The
report is written by Jacob Kvist Skiffard and Michael Tøgersen with Anne S. Dederichs,
Associate Professor at DTU, as supervisor. The report reflects a workload equivalent to
2x15 ECTS credits.
The aim of the topic in the report is to investigate the effectiveness of replacement air
in smoke ventilation by varying certain parameters and then examining the relationships
between these. CFD analysis has primarily been used as the method to investigate pa-
rameter variations. FDS has been employed as the simulation tool, and the comparison
criteria are the temperature, smoke layer height, visibility and the volume flow through the
openings.
The motivation lies in demonstrating that fire safety can be ensured with reduced material
use, while building owners benefit economically from carefully designed systems. Since
general solutions may be overly conservative for some building types, it is essential that
fire safety design is tailored to the specific building, ensuring adequate safety without
over-engineering.
The cover illustration was generated using artificial intelligence (AI).
Jacob Kvist Skiffard

vi
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
This study investigates the effectiveness of natural thermal smoke ventilation as a function
of the placement of replacement-air openings in the façade of a simple square building.
The objective was to clarify how fire size, wind speed, and the angular separation between
two openings influence ventilation performance, measured primarily by volume flow and
temperature, and secondarily by smoke layer height and visibility. Current Danish prac-
tice requires a minimum angular separation of 135° between openings, yet no scientific
evidence confirms that this ensures independence from wind effects.
A total of 27 simulations were carried out in Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS), varying fire
size (5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW), wind speed (0 m/s, 8 m/s, and 14 m/s), and angle (70°,
112°, 150°). Results show that volume flow was governed by fire size in the absence
of wind, whereas a combination of fire size and wind speed controlled the outcome at
8 m/s and 14 m/s with 150° as an exception, where wind alone dominated. Temperature
conditions were likewise primarily determined by fire size but were secondarily influenced
by wind and angle. For smoke layer height and visibility, wind speed was the primary
driver, while angle determined robustness as 112° generally provided the most stable
conditions, 150° the most critical, and 70° advantageous at moderate wind but unstable
at high wind.
Overall, no optimal angle was identified, and the hypothesis that ≥135° ensures wind inde-
pendence could not be confirmed. Instead, performance was determined by the specific
interaction between wind speed, fire size, and the placement of openings, emphasising
the need for project-specific evaluation of design and wind conditions.

Resume (Danish)
Dette studie undersøger effektiviteten af naturlig termisk brandventilation som funktion af
placeringen af erstatningsluftsåbninger i facaden på en simpel kvadratisk bygning. For-
målet var at afklare, hvordan brandstørrelse, vindhastighed og vinklen mellem to åbninger
påvirker ventilationsydelsen målt primært på volumenflow og temperatur, samt sekundært
på røglagshøjde og sigtbarhed. Ifølge dansk praksis kræves en minimumsvinkel på 135°
mellem åbninger, men der findes ingen dokumentation for, at dette sikrer uafhængighed
af vindpåvirkning.
I alt blev der gennemført 27 simuleringer i Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) med variation i
brandstørrelse (5 MW, 10 MW og 20 MW), vindhastighed (0 m/s, 8 m/s og 14 m/s) og vinkel
(70°, 112°, 150°). Resultaterne viser, at volumenflowet domineres af brandstørrelsen un-
der vindstille forhold, mens en kombination af brand og vind er styrende ved 8 m/s og
14 m/s dog med 150° som et særtilfælde, hvor vinden alene dominerer. Temperatur-
forholdene bestemmes ligeledes primært af brandstørrelsen, men påvirkes sekundært af
vind og vinkel. For røglagshøjde og sigtbarhed er vindhastigheden den primære driver,
mens vinklen bestemmer robustheden eftersom 112° generelt gav de mest stabile forhold,
150° de mest kritiske, og 70° var fordelagtig ved moderat vind men ustabil ved høj vind.
Samlet viser studiet, at der ikke findes en optimal vinkel, og hypotesen om vinduafhængig-
hed ved ≥,135° kan ikke bekræftes. Systemets effektivitet er i stedet bestemt af den
specifikke interaktion mellem vindhastighed, brandstørrelse og åbningernes placering,
hvilket understreger behovet for projektspecifik evaluering af udformning og vindforhold.

==PAGE:27==
1
Introduction
As modern buildings become larger, more complex and with greater storage capacities,
there is an increasing demand for both fire safety and operational response in the event
of an incident. A crucial element in fire suppression is the control of smoke and heat.
For this purpose, smoke ventilation is employed as a central technique aimed at limiting
the spread of smoke and heat, as well as ensuring the operational capabilities of rescue
services. Smoke ventilation improves evacuation conditions while also facilitating rescue
and extinguishing efforts in most cases.
Wind and fire spread are two factors that are continuously interconnected but are rarely
considered quantitatively, instead being viewed qualitatively based on common knowl-
edge of wind effects on buildings, as understood in the field of construction. In Denmark,
the wind is often neglected as a parameter in smoke ventilation, despite its potentially
significant influence on system performance.
In smoke ventilation, wind effects are typically only considered in qualitative analyzes,
without being based on quantitative calculations or having the possibility to perform such
calculations. To address this, the initial part of this report investigates the possibilities and
relationships between wind speed, the placement of openings, and fire sizes.
When it comes to active fire protection systems, this is always associated with greater
financial considerations and increased complexity regarding the interplay between the
various systems such as fire alarm systems, sprinkler-systems etc., which must be able
to communicate correctly without interfering with each other.
In production and storage buildings, as well as large assembly halls, natural smoke ven-
tilation is commonly applied to prevent flashover, structural collapse, and to protect evac-
uees. Since natural systems rely on pressure differences, temperature gradients, and
wind, it is crucial to understand how wind interacts with the building and the thermal con-
ditions in the fire zone when assessing system performance. Given that such buildings
often contain high fire loads concentrated in limited areas, it is particularly important that
the smoke ventilation functions effectively even under wind influence.
Smoke ventilation openings are often tested in controlled environments in a testing lab-
oratory, where the influence of the external wind through replacement air openings is
neglected or set as a fixed wind speed and direction. There are many different building
designs and locations for buildings with smoke ventilation, each of which will produce
varying efficiencies of the overall thermal smoke ventilation. Furthermore, the building
shape, surrounding buildings in the landscape and terrain also play an important role in
the influence of external wind.
In Denmark’s national regulations, the only requirement is that replacement air openings
must be wind independent. Wind independence is intended to account for the surrounding
wind environment and the unfavorable situations that may arise affecting the efficiency of
smoke ventilation inside the building. In one of the most commonly used design standards,
an angle of at least 135° degrees between two openings is described as constituting wind
independence.
Master in Fire Safety
1
==PAGE:28==
1.1
Literature review
This section presents a review of the literature in the field of smoke ventilation, including
replacement air openings and wind effects, to identify existing knowledge on the subject.
The review is based on previous master’s thesis, scientific articles, and various regulations
and guidelines from both national and international sources.
To begin with, some search terms were selected for the literature search. These search
terms include: ”Smoke ventilation”, ”Wind effects on buildings”, ”Thermal smoke ventila-
tion” and ”Replacement air openings in smoke ventilation systems”. As it is the authors’
interest that the literature maintains a sufficiently high quality, the search has primarily
focused on scientific articles with recognized academic standards.
Figure 1.1 illustrates the search process that was carried out in the literature review.
Figure 1.1: Illustration of the search process for the literature review.
To begin with, various search strategies were selected. Among these, brainstorming and
different search methods were used. The focus was on including different types of liter-
ature, such as articles, legislation, guidelines, etc. However, most of the references are
digital. The primary search methods used were Cited Reference Searching and Pearl
Growing. Both methods are based on a single relevant random article, from which rele-
vant citations and references are reviewed.
In the literature study, various search engines have been used to find scientific articles
relevant to the project. Different search terms have been used, all related to the influence
of the wind on thermal smoke ventilation in buildings.
The following search engines have been used in the literature study:
• DTU Findit
• Google Scholar
• Carrot2

as a supplementary tool to gain an overview of a large number of scientific articles. The

themes. However, the final assessment of relevance and academic content was made
exclusively by the authors.
1.1.1
Summary of the literature review
The complete literature study can be found in appendix A where a summary is presented
in the following.
Smoke ventilation constitutes a key component in building fire safety and, in some cases,
evacuation strategies. The purpose of natural smoke and heat exhaust ventilation sys-
tems (NSHEVS) is to ensure that smoke and heat are effectively discharged from the
building to maintain a usable smoke-free zone.
However, recent research has repeatedly shown that environmental factors, particularly
wind, play a crucial role in the performance of these systems. This review of theses,
scientific articles, and national and international standards has revealed both strengths
and gaps in the existing body of knowledge.
There is a broad consensus among the reviewed studies that wind has a significant im-
pact on the effectiveness of smoke ventilation. Several studies, including Svensgaard
4
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:31==
and Brøndum (2024), Brandforsk (1995), Kramer and Gerhardt (1990), and Zhou et al.
(2019), document how the direction and speed of the wind can cause turbulence, pressure
differences, and reverse flow, which directly reduce the efficiency of exhaust openings.
In particular, headwind scenarios can cause a pressure buildup at the exhaust openings,
which in some cases may lead to backflow of smoke and heat into the building.
Based on these sources, it is clear that wind has a dominant effect when wind speed
becomes sufficiently high. Therefore, thermal buoyancy is not always sufficient when the
wind effect becomes dominant.
A key finding from the review is that very few sources directly address the impact of wind
on the placement of replacement air openings. While some articles (e.g., Brandforsk
(1995) and Zhou et al. (2019)) touch on the relationship between pressure and flow di-
rection through both inlet and exhaust openings, it is rare to find a systematic treatment
of how wind affects the physical placement of intake openings on building façades.
Studies presented in Appendix A indicate, in line with Cp-based analyses of facade pres-
sure distribution (e.g. (Zhou et al., 2019); (Brandforsk, 1995)), that openings on the lee-
ward side may experience negative pressures, while windward placement can enhance
inflow but also increase the risk of bi-directional flow.
CFD-based studies (e.g. (Svensgaard and Brøndum, 2024); (Andreassen and Carstensen,
2023); (Nielsen, 2023)) further report reductions in mass flow and lower exhaust temper-
atures when openings are unfavorably located, emphasizing that placement is a critical
design parameter.
This represents a significant gap in the research, as the overall effectiveness of a smoke
ventilation system depends on the interaction between exhaust and the supply of fresh
air. If replacement air openings are placed on façades exposed to wind pressure, it can
result in excessive inflow, backpressure at the exhaust, or even jet streams that disrupt
smoke accumulation.
Both national and international standards recognize the importance of replacement air
and the need to counteract adverse wind effects. Dansk Brand- og Sikringsinstitut (2021)
sets explicit requirements for the placement and distribution of replacement air openings
— including that they must be positioned on façades with an angular separation of at least
135 degrees, and that each façade must account for at least 40% of the required area.
This design requirement aims to minimize the likelihood of negative pressure at the ex-
haust openings, which can occur under unilateral wind exposure.
International guidelines, such as Dansk Standard (2009) and The National Fire Protection
Association (2024), acknowledge the same issue but place greater responsibility on the
designer to assess the effect of wind. They typically recommend the use of monitoring
systems to close wind-exposed openings at high wind speeds, but they do not provide
specific design requirements for the placement of these openings.
A number of recent theses and articles use CFD-based simulation via Fire Dynamics
Simulator (FDS) to analyze the effects of wind and geometry of smoke ventilation opening
(Svensgaard and Brøndum (2024), Andreassen and Carstensen (2023), Nielsen (2023)).
This tool offers great flexibility in modeling complex flow patterns, but it is primarily used
to examine exhaust openings, and not necessarily the interaction between replacement
air and exhaust.
Master in Fire Safety
5
==PAGE:32==
There is a clear potential in applying these findings more specifically to the placement and
configuration of replacement air openings under varying wind directions and speeds.
Based on the reviewed literature, the following conclusions can be drawn:
• Wind affects the efficiency of smoke ventilation.
• Replacement air is essential for maintaining pressure balance and effective heat
and smoke exhaust.
• Few studies directly address how wind exposure on replacement air openings influ-
ences the overall effectiveness of smoke ventilation.
Effective smoke ventilation depends on a comprehensive understanding of airflow dynam-
ics in and around a building. It is therefore crucial that both the exhaust and replacement
air openings are designed considering realistic wind scenarios.
Failing to account for the effect of wind on replacement air openings can lead to designs
that are ineffective in practice and therefore pose an unnecessary risk to building occu-
pants and emergency responders.
1.2
Objective
The overall objective of this project is to investigate the influence of wind and the place-
ment of replacement air openings on the performance of smoke ventilation systems. The
study considers variations in fire size, wind speed, and the angle between openings, and
evaluates their impact on parameters such as volume flow, temperature, smoke layer
height and visibility.
Although current Danish practice for natural thermal smoke ventilation requires a minimum
angular separation of 135° between replacement air openings, no scientific evidence has
been found to confirm that this criterion ensures independence from wind effects. Conse-
quently, the efficiency is examined for configurations with both greater and smaller angles
than 135°.
Current knowledge does not sufficiently describe how these factors influence each other in
large enclosures, or how the angle between openings may reduce the impact of wind. This
raises the overarching question of how variations in fire size, wind speed, and opening
placement, including the angle between openings, affect airflow, temperature, and smoke
layer height during a fire.
1.3
Reading Guide
This report aims to investigate the effectiveness of smoke ventilation in a simple building
exposed to wind, with varying wind speeds, placement of replacement air openings, and
fire sizes.
The report can be used by other students wishing to conduct further research within the
same field and carry out additional studies on ventilation efficiency. It is primarily intended
for readers with technical knowledge in the areas of smoke ventilation, wind conditions,
fire dynamics and computational fluid dynamics.
The report begins with a review of the theoretical background behind the principles used
in the simulations, as well as the assumptions involved. This is followed by a description
of the methodology, including how the simulation model was built, validated, and verified.
6
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:33==
The results are then presented through a systematic review based on measurable param-
eters for ventilation efficiency, including smoke layer height, visibility, temperature, and
volume flow rate. The report concludes with a summary and a perspective.
The results section includes an ongoing discussion of the findings. Several sections are
elaborated in an appendix, with clear references indicating when a section is a summary
of a more detailed discussion.
In this report, the figures are either reproduced directly from original sources with permis-
sion or redrawn based on existing figures and cited accordingly. To ensure transparency
and proper referencing, the following practice has been applied:
• Reproduced figures: These figures are identical to the originals and are included
with the explicit permission of the copyright holder. The source is stated in the figure
caption using the formulation “Reproduced with permission from (publisher, year)”.
• Redrawn figures: These figures have been prepared by the author(s) of this report
based on existing figures. They have been graphically or linguistically adapted but
are founded on the same data or information. The source is stated in the figure
caption using the formulation “Adapted from (author, year)”.
1.4
Assumptions and limitations
This section outlines the general assumptions and limitations applied in the study. De-
tailed assumptions related to the specific building model and simulation setup are pre-
sented separately in section 4.1 on page 46.
It is assumed that smoke layer height, visibility, mass flow, and temperature provide rep-
resentative indicators of the effectiveness of smoke ventilation in the room. Furthermore,
it is assumed that all roof exhaust openings are horizontal and uniform in size and place-
ment in all simulations.
The simulations conducted in this project cannot be used as general support for the design
of thermal smoke ventilation systems with fewer or smaller replacement air openings than
those specified in the relevant design standards. In addition, the results can only be used
to provide indications on whether the placement of replacement air openings can influence
the performance of the smoke ventilation system.
The number of simulations is assumed to provide a representative insight into efficiency;
however, a complete understanding of system performance can only be achieved by con-
ducting a larger number of simulations. This would allow for the development of statistical
models capable of describing effectiveness with greater precision. Due to the time con-
straints of this project, it was not possible to perform the number of simulations required
to develop fully reliable and validated statistical models.
Wind impact on the building occurs within a defined area of the computational domain,
and it is assumed that this domain is sufficiently large to achieve quasi-steady states for
the wind profile.
Only the wind acting normal to the façade has been considered, represented as either
positive pressure on the windward side or suction on the leeward side. The impact of
the oblique wind on the replacement air openings has not been included, as direct pres-
sure and suction are expected to represent the extremes in terms of efficiency, while the
influence of the oblique wind would likely fall somewhere in between.
Master in Fire Safety
7
==PAGE:34==
Wind has a significant effect relative to building height, as wind speed increases expo-
nentially with elevation above ground level. Therefore, the results cannot be generalised
to all types of buildings.
This report focuses solely on a single room. For multiple interconnected rooms, the find-
ings are not directly applicable and specific simulations should be carried out based on
the actual building design.
The variable fire load within the room is not considered in each simulation. A constant fire
load is applied within each individual simulation; however, the fire load varies between
simulations.
In this report, the fire resistance of load bearing structures is not assessed. It is assumed
that these structures have a longer durability than the duration of the fire.
Occupant impact, e.g. evacuation simulation, is not included in the project. The occupants
are assumed to have reached safety before critical conditions occur.
In the development of this project, an AI-based assistant, ChatGPT (OpenAI, GPT-5)
has been used as a sparring partner for idea generation, conceptual development, and
methodological discussions. The assistant’s input has served as a supplement to other
academic sources and considerations, but the final responsibility for the content, analy-
ses, and conclusions of the project lies solely with the authors.

2
Theory
This chapter presents the theoretical background of the project and the mechanisms that
govern the effectiveness of natural thermal smoke ventilation. The first section introduces
Smoke and Heat Exhaust Ventilation Systems (SHEVS), with emphasis on pressure dis-
tribution, thermal buoyancy, and the stack effect. The influence of wind is then addressed
through the atmospheric boundary layer and Monin–Obukhov Similarity Theory, followed
by wind–building interactions, turbulence, and requirements for computational domain
design and grid resolution. Plume theories and correlations for smoke layer height and
temperature are subsequently discussed. Finally, Computational Fluid Dynamics, CFD,
and Fire Dynamics Simulator, FDS, are introduced as the numerical tools underpinning
the analyses in chapter 4 on page 45.
2.1
Smoke and Heat Exhaust Ventilation System (SHEVS)
Smoke ventilation is most commonly used to protect against fire and smoke spread, in-
cluding the removal of smoke and heat from the building, as well as to prevent the collapse
of load-bearing structures. Fire ventilation can also protect evacuees in the event of an in-
cident where evacuation is required and ensure the ability of local fire fighters to respond.
Smoke ventilation can be designed as both natural and mechanical. In the mechanical
part, smoke and heat are forced out of the building using mechanical components, such
as fans. In the natural part, smoke and heat are expelled from the building using buoyancy
and pressure differences at various levels. This thesis only deals with Natural Smoke and
Heat Exhaust Ventilation Systems (NSHEVS).
A smoke ventilation system typically consists of the following components:
• Exhaust openings
• Smoke/thermal detectors
• Replacement air
• Control panel
• Activation buttons
• Power supply
• Optional smoke barriers
Together, these components make up a smoke ventilation system. Both exhaust openings
and replacement air openings can be placed both vertically and horizontally, depending
on the design of the building, as well as the type of system and the protective purpose in
question.
This master’s thesis investigates exclusively the effect of replacement air openings on the
efficiency of natural thermal smoke ventilation.
2.1.1
Pressure distribution inside buildings during a fire
Natural smoke ventilation occurs as a result of the fire’s plume and the temperatures and
pressure differences around it. There will naturally be thermal buoyancy in the plume
itself, which leads smoke and heat from the low-pressure area towards the high-pressure
area. This phenomenon is illustrated in figure 2.1 on the next page.
Master in Fire Safety
9
==PAGE:36==
Figure 2.1: Pressure distribution during a fire adapted from (Svensson, 2020)
A fire in a room will cause hot smoke gases, which have a lower density than the sur-
rounding air, to rise to the ceiling. The accumulation of these hot smoke gases will, over
time, create a pressure build-up in the upper part of the room, which will cause a vacuum
in the lower part of the room. This overpressure results in smoke gases being pushed out
of the room through the ceiling under ideal conditions, where there are no external wind
influences. In addition, some replacement air (typically in the lower part) must be intro-
duced to create continuous flow in the building. Alternatively, the vacuum may eventually
become sufficiently strong to reverse the flow of smoke gases, potentially resulting in an
under-ventilated fire.
2.1.2
Thermal Buoyancy
The density of fire gases decreases as temperature increases, which causes them to rise
relative to the surrounding cooler air. This phenomenon, known as thermal buoyancy,
drives the upward movement of hot gases in a fire.
The combustion products form a plume – an upward flow of hot gases and entrained air
above the fire. Ambient air is drawn into the plume, heated, and mixed with combustion
products, further increasing the buoyant flow.
As the hot gases accumulate below the ceiling, their lower density compared to the sur-
rounding air results in the formation of a distinct smoke layer.
The thickness of this smoke layer depends on the enclosure’s volume and height, the
fire’s energy output and growth rate, as well as cooling effects. During ascent, the gases
are cooled by mixing with ambient air and by heat transfer to surrounding surfaces, which
are typically cooler than the hot gases themselves.
Thermal buoyancy or the pressure difference in a room is described by the following ex-
pression (Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022):
∆P = (ρa −ρg)g h
(2.1)
Where:
ρa
Density for ambient air
[kg/m3]
ρg
Density for hot gas layer air
[kg/m3]
g
Gravity
[m/s2]
h
The height of the hot gas layer
[m]
10
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:37==
With constant values for the density of both the surrounding air and the smoke, the equa-
tion can be rewritten as (Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022):
∆P = (353/(Ta −Tg))g h
(2.2)
Where:
Ta
Temperature for ambient air
[◦C]
Tg
Temperature for hot gas layer air
[◦C]
2.1.3
The Stack Effect
The stack effect describes how pressure differences, caused by the density difference
between hot smoke gases and the surrounding cooler air, drive the upward flow of smoke
within a building. This phenomenon creates a vertical pressure gradient and establishes
a neutral plane, below which air enters and above which smoke exits.(Svensson, 2020)
In buildings with high ceilings, the efficiency of the stack effect depends on both the fire
load and the indoor–outdoor temperature difference. On warm days, if the ambient tem-
perature approaches the smoke gas temperature, the buoyancy forces decrease, reduc-
ing the effectiveness of the stack effect. For efficient removal of smoke gases, there must
be an adequate balance between building height and fire load. When the fire load is suffi-
ciently large, thermal buoyancy and the stack effect generally dominate over wind effects
inside the building.(Svensson, 2020)
The pressure difference driving the stack effect can be expressed as a function of the
density difference, gravity, and the vertical distance between the neutral plane and the
opening (Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022):
∆Pu = hu g(pa −pg)
(2.3)
Where:
pa
Ambient pressure
[Pa]
pg
Hot gas layer pressure
[Pa]
hu
Height from neutral plane to upper opening
[m]
This pressure difference corresponds to the kinetic energy of the flow at the upper opening
(Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022):
∆Pu = (ρg v2
g)/2
(2.4)
Where:
vg
Velocity through the upper opening
[m/s]
Combining these expressions yields the velocity through the upper opening (Karlsson and
Quintiere, 2022):
vg =
√
(2 hu g(ρa −ρg))/ρg
(2.5)
By considering the ideal gas law, the density difference can be related to temperature,
which allows the equations to be solved and the pressure differences to be quantified.

2.2
Wind profiles
In the atmospheric boundary layer, extending up to approximately 500 m above the ground,
wind speed and direction vary with height due to friction with the Earth’s surface. Near the
surface, friction reduces the wind speed, which in turn weakens the Coriolis force relative
to the pressure gradient force. This imbalance causes the wind to be deflected across the
isobars, rather than flowing parallel to them as in the geostrophic balance aloft as shown
on figure 2.2. (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989)
Figure 2.2: Forces of the wind adapted from (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989).
Wind speed generally increases with height following a logarithmic or power-law profile,
approaching the geostrophic wind above the boundary layer. This produces the charac-
teristic wind shear: wind direction shifts downward through the profile, and wind speed
gradually increases with height as shown on figure 2.3. (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989)
Figure 2.3: The theoretical windprofile adapted from (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989).
The resulting wind direction near the surface is therefore turned relative to the geostrophic
wind at higher altitudes. The angular deflection is typically around 20°, but depending on
the surface roughness and the stability of the atmosphere, it can range between 10° and
45°. (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989)
12
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:39==
2.2.1
The atmospheric boundary layer
The atmospheric boundary layer (ABL) is the lowest part of the troposphere, typically
extending up to 1 km above the Earth’s surface, depending on atmospheric stability and
surface conditions. Within this layer, the wind profile is strongly influenced by surface
friction, roughness, and temperature gradients. The strongest turbulence occurs near the
ground, especially within the lowest few hundred metres. (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989) The
mean wind speed close to the surface can be described by the logarithmic wind profile
(Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989):
u(z) = u∗
κ ln
( z
z0
)
(2.6)
Where:
u∗
Friction velocity
[m/s]
κ
von Kármán constant
[-]
z
Height above earth surface
[m]
z0
Roughness length
[m]
The length of the roughness represents the effective height of surface obstacles, such as
vegetation or buildings, which influence the transfer of momentum between the surface
and the atmosphere. The logarithmic profile is applicable within the surface layer (typically
the lowest 50–200 m). Corrections exist for stability effects and higher altitudes, but for
the scales considered in this project, the standard logarithmic formulation is sufficient and,
therefore, applied to represent the vertical wind profile. (Dyrbye and Hansen, 1989)
2.2.2
Monin-Obukhov Similarity Theory
The Monin-Obukhov Similarity Theory (MOST) describes the structure of the atmospheric
surface layer, i.e. the lowest 10% of the atmospheric boundary layer, where turbulence
is strongly influenced by both wind shear and buoyancy (Breedt et al., 2018). The theory
defines the stability of the wind profile based on wind speed, temperature, and turbulence.
Three key concepts form the foundation of MOST: the dynamics of the surface layer, the
Monin-Obukhov length, and dimensionless similarity functions.
2.2.2.1
Dynamics of the Surface Layer
The surface layer is characterized by turbulent fluxes of momentum and heat. Mechanical
turbulence is generated by wind shear, whereas thermal turbulence is driven by buoyancy.
(Breedt et al., 2018)
2.2.2.2
Wind Profile
The mean wind speed in the surface layer in MOST is given by (Breedt et al., 2018):
u(z) = u∗

stable and unstable atmospheric conditions (Breedt et al., 2018).

The Monin–Obukhov length, L is a stability parameter that quantifies the relative contri-

et al., 2018)
L = −

If L > 0, conditions are stable (buoyancy suppresses turbulence). If L < 0, conditions are

2.3
Wind on buildings
Wind is generated by pressure differences in the atmosphere, typically caused by tem-
perature gradients. On a global scale, these differences drive large-scale wind systems,
while local effects, including turbulence and terrain roughness, govern the wind conditions
experienced around buildings.
When wind interacts with a building, the flow is deflected around the structure, creating
characteristic pressure zones. Positive pressure occurs on the windward façade, while
negative pressure (suction) develops along the sides and on the leeward façade. The
distribution of these pressures depends on wind speed, direction, building geometry, and
the surrounding environment.
Due to turbulence and local gusts, wind conditions can vary significantly over time and
space, making it challenging to generalize wind effects at a given location. For practical
applications, approximate values for wind speed, direction, and pressure coefficients are
used. Figure 2.4 shows a typical pressure distribution around a rectangular building.
Figure 2.4: Pressure distribution of wind on buildings adapted from (Svensson, 2020).
The wind pressure acting on a building façade is proportional to the square of the wind
speed. On the windward side, positive pressure occurs, while negative pressure (suction)
develops on the sides and leeward façade. As a rule of thumb, the suction on the leeward
side can be approximated as half the magnitude of the windward pressure, although the
exact distribution depends on building geometry, wind direction, and turbulence. Roof
pressures vary with slope and design, with flat roofs typically experiencing suction and
pitched roofs showing both suction and pressure zones. (Svensson, 2020)
Master in Fire Safety
15
==PAGE:42==
The wind pressure can be calculated using the following formula (Svensson, 2020):
∆p = 0.5 cf ρa v2
(2.13)
Where:
cf
Form factor
[-]
ρa
Air density
[kg/m3]
v
Velocity
[m/s]
2.3.1
Pressure distribution outside buildings (Aerodynamics)
When wind strikes a building, pressure and suction forces are generated on its surfaces.
These over- and underpressures are referenced against the barometric pressure and de-
pend strongly on the building’s shape and design. Typically, the windward façade is sub-
jected to overpressure, while the side façades and the leeward façade experience suction
(negative pressure). The roof pressure distribution depends on slope and geometry. For
flat roofs, suction generally occurs across the entire surface as illustrated on figure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: Turbulence around buildings (set up at left - plan at right) adapted from (Dyrbye
and Hansen, 1989).
The mean pressure on a surface can be expressed using a pressure coefficient, cp, which
relates the surface pressure to the dynamic pressure of the incident flow. The coefficient
defines the magnitude of the wind-induced surface pressure. For streamlined shapes, cp,
can vary with Reynolds number, while for sharp-edged buildings the coefficient is approx-
imately Reynolds independent. Turbulence introduces additional fluctuations, meaning
that the local pressure at a given point can deviate significantly from the mean value.
As illustrated in figure 2.5, the pressure distribution changes with the direction of the wind.
Although normal incidence produces the classical pattern of windward overpressure and
leeward suction, oblique winds or flow parallel to the building’s longitudinal axis alter the
distribution considerably.
Since wind is sensitive to surrounding roughness (e.g. terrain or other buildings), ac-
curate modelling of wind-induced pressures in CFD simulations requires careful domain
definition. If the computational domain is too small, artificial acceleration of the flow and
increased turbulence may occur near the building surfaces, leading to unrealistic results.
Figure 2.6 on the facing page illustrates how insufficient domain height can distort wind
speed and turbulence around the building. (Wegrzyński et al., 2018)
16
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:43==
Figure 2.6: Turbulence in relation to the computational domain adapted from (Wegrzyński
et al., 2018)
According to Wegrzyński et al. (2018), some guideline values for the computational do-
main are provided as shown figure 2.7.
Figure 2.7: Guideline distances in the computational domain reproduced with permission
from (Wegrzyński et al., 2018, Springer Nature).
The guidelines recommend that the computational domain should extend at least five
times the height of the building (5H) upward and edge ways, and between 10-15 times
the height of the building downstream. On the windward side, the domain should also
extend at least five times the building height upstream to allow proper development of the
wind profile. These dimensions ensure that the inflow boundary conditions remain unaf-
fected by the building and that the wake region is properly resolved, minimizing unwanted
boundary effects in CFD simulations.
Master in Fire Safety
17
==PAGE:44==
2.3.2
Near-wall grid resolution
Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) employs a Very Large Eddy Simulation (VLES) formula-
tion for the core flow and wall-modelled boundary conditions near solid surfaces. Con-
sequently, the placement of the near-wall grid is governed by the dimensionless wall dis-
tance y+ rather than by the turbulence model itself. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b,c)
The wall coordinate is defined as
y+ = uτ y
ν ,
uτ =
√
τw/ρ,
(2.14)
where y is the normal wall distance from the wall to the centre of the first non-wall cell (in
FDS, typically y ≈∆y/2), uτ is the friction velocity, and ν is the kinematic viscosity [m2/s]
(Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024c). The value of y+ indicates whether the first cell is in the
viscous sublayer, buffer layer, or logarithmic layer of a turbulent boundary layer. (Kevin
McGrattan et al., 2024b)
In FDS, smooth walls are modelled by relating wall shear stress τw to the near-wall velocity
through a law-of-the-wall formulation of Werner–Wengle type, while roughness is repre-
sented with a rough log law. The method assumes that the first off-wall cell lies in the
logarithmic region rather than explicitly resolving the viscous sublayer (Kevin McGrattan
et al., 2024c).
For wall-modelled LES, it is therefore recommended that the first cell be located within
the logarithmic region, typically corresponding to y+ values of order 30 to 102. Monitor-
ing y+ can help assess placement, but reliability should always be confirmed by mesh-
sensitivity studies, since wall functions are based on idealised equilibrium assumptions
that are rarely met in buoyancy-driven, transient fire flows. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024c)
(Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b)
If y+ is too low (below about 5), the first cell lies within the viscous sublayer, which violates
the assumptions of wall function and cannot substitute for a low-Re or DNS-type near-wall
resolution. If y+ is too high, the first cell is too far from the wall and the inferred wall shear
stress becomes unreliable. Because turbulence production depends on wall shear, such
errors indirectly affect turbulence, which means that y+ controls how wall shear is inferred
but is not itself the turbulence model, which in LES is provided by the subgrid-scale model
(Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024c). Indicative limits are y+ < 5 for the viscous sublayer,
5 < y+ < 30 for the buffer layer, and y+ ≥30 for the logarithmic layer. (Kevin McGrattan
et al., 2024b) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004) (Ferziger et al.)
During verification and post-processing, y+ should be checked on relevant wall patches,
for instance via the boundary outputs, to confirm that the first cell placement corresponds
to the intended modelling region, and then verified by mesh refinement (Kevin McGrattan
et al., 2024b).
2.4
Fire plumes
There are several analytical and semi-empirical methods for describing buoyant fire plumes
in enclosures. Many are based on laboratory data and scaling arguments and are widely
used for hand calculations and for checking key inputs to FDS (e.g., convective heat-
release rate, plume entrainment, and upper-layer temperature). The following subsec-
tions summarise the relations used later for validation.

==PAGE:46==
2.5
Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD)
Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) is the numerical analysis of flow and heat-transfer
phenomena by solving the conservation equations of mass, momentum, and energy on
a discrete grid. The continuous PDEs are converted to algebraic systems through spatial
discretisation and time integration. The fidelity of the model depends on spatial / temporal
resolution, closure of unresolved scales (turbulence treatment), material data, boundary
conditions, and numerical stability. (Ferziger et al.) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004)
The rate of change of a conserved quantity within a control volume equals the net flux
through its boundary plus sources/sinks.
In differential form, this yields the continu-
ity (mass), momentum, and energy equations, which underpin the models used here.
(Ferziger et al.) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004)
The domain is divided into finite control volumes (cells), and the solution is advanced in
discrete time steps. Finer grids and smaller time steps can improve accuracy but increase
cost; a balanced resolution is chosen to capture dominant scales without excessive run-
time. (Ferziger et al.) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004)
Turbulent flows span a spectrum of scales. Three canonical approaches are commonly
cited: DNS (all relevant scales resolved), LES (large scales resolved with subgrid effects
modelled), and RANS (the mean flow solved with the effect of all turbulent fluctuations
modelled). As RANS cannot be used in the implemented code, RANS is not part of this
thesis. (Ferziger et al.) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004)
Cell sizes (∆x, ∆y, ∆z) and the time step directly affect both accuracy and cost. Coarse
grids cause numerical diffusion and under-resolved gradients; very fine grids raise cost
without guaranteed benefit unless demanded by the physics. Grid studies are applied
where relevant to demonstrate robustness. A Cartesian coordinate system (x, y, z) is
used. (Ferziger et al.) (Pope, 2008) (Wilcox, 2004)
Numerical simulations are carried out with Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS), an open-
source CFD code for low-Mach, buoyancy-driven fire flows. FDS solves a filtered form of
the Navier–Stokes equations and models unresolved small-scale motions (subgrid) while
using wall models near solid boundaries. Current releases of FDS use VLES by default,
an LES-based configuration designed for engineering-type grids. LES or DNS can be se-
lected explicitly, whereas RANS is not available in FDS. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b)
20
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:47==
3
Method
In the following chapter, the applied methodology is presented, with particular emphasis
on the development of the CFD model that constitutes the foundation for the analyses.
The simulations are performed using the CFD software Fire Dynamics Simulator, FDS,
which applies a Large Eddy Simulation (LES) approach with a Deardorff subgrid-scale
turbulence model, widely recognised for fire-driven flow simulations (Kevin McGrattan
et al., 2024b).
The construction of the CFD model is described first, beginning with the building geom-
etry, followed by the surrounding environment, the configuration of replacement-air and
smoke ventilation openings, and finally the prescribed design fire sizes and wind speeds.
Subsequently, a validation of the fire and wind models is presented, together with a veri-
fication of the simulation tool, to ensure the reliability of the modelling approach.
Finally, 27 scenarios are defined on the basis of a systematic combination of different fire
sizes, wind speeds, and configurations of replacement-air openings. These scenarios
form the foundation for the analyses presented in chapter 4 on page 45.
3.1
CFD-model
In the following section, a detailed description is provided of the CFD model that was
constructed for the analyses, which was based on an idealised building. The presenta-
tion covered the building geometry and the surrounding environment, the configuration of
replacement-air and smoke ventilation openings, and the prescribed fire sizes and wind
speeds, including the employed wind model.
3.1.1
Design of the compartment
For the construction of the CFD model, a traditional simple building was chosen as the
reference, with a geometry comparable to the building applied in the study by Svens-
gaard and Brøndum (2024). The purpose of the present project was to investigate the
effectiveness of thermal smoke ventilation, including variation of the angle between two
replacement-air openings, making this building type particularly relevant to the study.
In the present project, the interior ceiling height was increased to 10.0 m compared with
the 7.0 m applied in Svensgaard and Brøndum (2024). This adjustment was introduced
to prevent the flame from reaching the ceiling and spreading horizontally along it, which
could potentially influence the buoyancy-driven flow generated by the fire. In this way, it
was ensured that such effects did not interfere with the analysis of the angle between the
two replacement-air openings.
To enable validation of the fire model, the enclosing walls and ceiling in the present study
were modelled as concrete with a thickness of 0.1 m. The thermal conductivity, k, specific
heat capacity, c, and density, ρ, were set to 1.8 W/mK, 1.04 kJ/kgK, and 2,280 kg/m3,
respectively.
The building was modelled with a square floor plan and a height as specified in table 3.1
on the next page for both interior and exterior dimensions, and it is illustrated in figure 3.1
on the following page. The figure shows the building from the southeast, where the place-
ment of the roof-mounted smoke ventilation openings and the two replacement-air doors
can be seen. The fire location is indicated by the red square surface in the centre of the
building.

3.1.2
Computational Domain and Mesh
The computational domain was constructed such that the building was positioned cen-
trally within the flow field, while at the same time ensuring sufficient distance to the outer
boundaries in order to avoid unintended boundary effects. The domain extended from
x = −52 m to 84.8 m, y = −107.4 m to 85.4 m, and z = 0 m to 62.4 m, as illustrated in fig-
ure 3.2 on the next page. The figure also shows the location of the building within the grey
volume. The chosen extent of the computational domain ensured sufficient free airflow
both upstream and downstream of the building’s position, thereby reducing the risk of nu-
merical recirculation, as described in subsection 2.3.1 on page 16 and subsection 3.1.6.1
on page 29. Reference is also made to subsection 3.2.1 on page 34 and subsection 3.2.2
on page 35, where the cell size was validated with respect to both fire and wind conditions.
As shown in figure 3.2 on the facing page, the computational domain was subdivided
into two primary mesh blocks with cell sizes of 0.40 m and 0.80 m, as well as a third
block with varying cell sizes ranging from 0.05 m to 0.20 m. This approach allowed for
a coarse resolution to be applied in regions where weak gradients were expected, while
a finer resolution was applied in regions where strong gradients were anticipated, such
as near the façade, along the roof surface, around the smoke ventilation openings and
replacement-air openings, and within the fire plume where velocity and temperature fields
changed more rapidly. Consequently, each block was assigned a cell size according to
the required level of detail.
22
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:49==
Figure 3.2: Computational domain with centrally placed building and mesh blocks of
0.80 m, 0.40 m, and 0.05–0.20 m.
A cell size of 0.8 m was therefore applied in the outer part of the computational domain
upstream and downstream of the building’s position, as shown in figure 3.2, since this
was considered sufficient to describe the overall flow field in relation to the total number
of computational cells. This was followed by a larger region with a cell size of 0.40 m,
the extent of which can also be seen in the figure. Near the building façade, the cell size
was gradually reduced from 0.40 m →0.20 m →0.10 m →0.05 m. A refinement ratio of
1:2 ensured that pressure and flow conditions were represented more accurately, while
numerical errors caused by abrupt changes in cell size were minimized Kevin McGrattan
et al. (2024b). A cell size of 0.05 m was validated as the acceptable resolution with respect
to the values of cp and y+, as discussed in subsection 3.2.2 on page 35. This subdivision
ensured an efficient balance between computational cost and solution accuracy.
The gradual refinement of the cell size is illustrated in figure 3.3a on the next page and fig-
ure 3.3b on the following page, which show the computational domain around the building
in the XY and XZ planes, respectively, indicating the regions where cell sizes of 0.05 m,
0.10 m, and 0.20 m were applied.
Along the façades and roof of the building, a cell size of 0.05 m was applied within a
distance of 0.40 m from the exterior boundary of the structure. Beyond this region, the
cell size was gradually increased to 0.10 m and subsequently to 0.20 m, before the mesh
continued with coarser cells in the surrounding volume.
Master in Fire Safety
23
==PAGE:50==
(a) XY-plane.
(b) XZ-plane.
Figure 3.3: Computational domain around the building shown in the XY -plane (a) and
XZ-plane (b), illustrating the regions with cell sizes of 0.05 m, 0.10 m, and 0.20 m.
Inside the building volume, a cell size of 0.10 m was predominantly applied. This resolu-
tion was based on the validation of the fire model in subsection 3.2.1 on page 34, where
it was documented to provide a grid-independent solution for temperature, smoke layer
height, and volume flows. Along the exterior surfaces, a cell size of 0.05 m was applied
within the first 0.10 m of the façade and roof thickness. A consistent 1:2 transition was
ensured towards the interior computational mesh, where cells of 0.10 m were applied.
In table 3.2, the overall mesh subdivision is summarised according to cell size. The table
specifies the number of cells, their relative share, and provides a brief description of where
the respective cell sizes were applied.

The distribution showed that the highest cell density was applied in the building volume

3.1.2.1
External Domain Boundaries
As described in subsection 3.1.6.1 on page 29, the computational domain containing the
building was constructed sufficiently large to avoid boundary effects in the flow field. The
external boundaries of the computational domain were defined as an open boundary con-
dition in FDS, which was implemented in the model through so-called vents.
For an open boundary condition, the flow could enter or leave the computational domain
depending on the local pressure gradients.
24
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:51==
In FDS, this is handled numerically by imposing a zero-gradient condition on the tangential
velocity components, such that the value outside the boundary is set equal to that in the
nearest cell. In this way, the computational domain behaved as open to the surroundings
Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b).
At ground level, an open boundary condition was not applied, as this level represented the
terrain, on which a terrain roughness of 0.03 was defined, corresponding to the landscape
characteristics discussed in subsection 3.1.6.3 on page 31.
3.1.3
Design of Smoke Ventilation Openings
In the model, both smoke ventilation openings and replacement-air openings were in-
corporated to represent the thermal smoke ventilation system. The smoke ventilation
openings were located in the roof, while the replacement-air openings were positioned
in the lower third of the façade walls. This configuration was chosen to utilise the stack
effect, whereby hot smoke and gases were discharged through the roof while cooler re-
placement air was supplied through the façade. This arrangement reflected typical design
principles for natural smoke ventilation.
In all scenarios, the size of both the smoke exhaust openings in the roof and the replacement-
air openings in the façade was kept constant. Only the location of the replacement-air
openings was varied.
3.1.3.1
Replacement-Air Openings
In the model, the replacement air was represented by two doors located in the façade,
where Door 1, D1, retained a fixed position, while Door 2, D2, was varied between three
locations, as shown in figure 3.4. As illustrated in the figure, Door 2 was positioned such
that the angle between Door 1 and Door 2 was either 70°, 112°, or 150°relative to the
centre of the building.
Figure 3.4: Smoke exhaust openings in the roof (OP1–OP6) and replacement-air open-
ings in the façade (D1, D2).
Master in Fire Safety
25
==PAGE:52==
In the simulations, the wind direction was defined as acting on the façade opposite to
Door 1, which implied that Door 1 was always located on the leeward side. When the
angle between the two doors was 112°or 150°, Door 2 was oriented parallel to the wind
direction. In this configuration, a position on the windward façade could result in adverse
pressure conditions, which gradually diminished downstream. In the third configuration,
Door 2 was placed on the leeward side of the building, where the opening was influenced
indirectly by the wind.
The two replacement-air openings were each modelled with dimensions of 2.8 m × 2.8 m,
corresponding to a total geometric opening area of 15.7 m2. The openings represented
two doors, and it was therefore considered a reasonable assumption not to model them
with further detail, as doors are normally constructed without windbreaks, deflectors, or
similar features that reduce wind effects across the opening.
3.1.3.2
Smoke Ventilation Openings
The smoke ventilation openings were modelled as square openings with dimensions of
1.6 m × 1.6 m, with a total of six openings evenly distributed across the roof, as shown in
figure 3.4 on the preceding page. This corresponded to a total geometric opening area of
15.4 m2.
In the model, no account was taken of the fact that smoke ventilation openings are nor-
mally constructed with windbreaks, deflectors, or similar features that reduce wind effects
across the opening. This choice was justified by Svensgaard and Brøndum (2024), who
demonstrated that the level of detail in the opening geometry has only a limited influence
on volume flow and temperature, as these are primarily governed by wind speed and wind
direction.
3.1.4
Monitored Varibles in CFD Simulations
To evaluate the influence of the placement of the replacement air openings, measuring
points were defined in the model to record temperature, smoke layer height, and visibility,
with their locations shown in figure 3.5, as well as volume flow through the smoke ventila-
tion openings and the replacement air openings, as shown in figure 3.4 on the preceding
page.
Figure 3.5: Placement of measuring points in the model.
26
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:53==
As shown in figure 3.5 on the preceding page, nine measurement columns were placed in
a grid, which was qualitatively assessed to represent a sufficient portion of the building to
evaluate the influence of the placement of the replacement-air openings. These columns
measured the smoke layer height and the mean temperature in the upper smoke gas layer
of the building.
3.1.4.1
Smoke Layer Height
The smoke layer height was measured in nine measurement columns, positioned as
shown in figure 3.5 on the facing page. The columns were modelled to extend across
the full interior height of the building. In FDS, the smoke layer height was defined as
the height of the interface between the upper hot smoke layer and the lower relatively
smoke-free zone. A detailed explanation of the calculation procedure is provided in Kevin
McGrattan et al. (2024b). It is further noted in Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b) that, unlike
in classical two-zone models, the interface is not sharply defined.
The smoke layer height was primarily measured to analyse whether grid-independent
results were obtained with respect to the choice of cell size, as well as to verify FDS as a
modelling tool in subsection 3.2.1 on page 34 and section 3.3 on page 39. The parameter
was not used to evaluate the influence of the placement of replacement-air openings,
since significant smoke mixing occurred in the building under wind conditions. This is
consistent with Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b), which states that the interface becomes
less well-defined under conditions of strong mixing.
3.1.4.2
Visibility
Visibility was measured 2.0 m above the floor at the nine measurement points shown in
figure 3.5 on the preceding page, as a parameter relevant to the assessment of occupant
safety.
3.1.4.3
Average Upper Smoke Layer Temperature
The temperature in the upper smoke layer was measured in nine measurement columns,
positioned as shown in figure 3.5 on the facing page. The columns were modelled to ex-
tend across the full interior height of the building, where the temperature was determined
as the mean value within the upper smoke layer. This value varied continuously over time,
as the smoke layer height fluctuated due to flow instabilities, while temperature gradients
existed between the lower and upper boundary regions of the layer.
This parameter was primarily measured to analyse whether grid-independent results were
obtained with respect to the choice of cell size, as well as to verify FDS as a modelling
tool in subsection 3.2.1 on page 34 and section 3.3 on page 39. Significant smoke mixing
occurred in the building under wind conditions, which could affect the temperature val-
ues in the smoke layer, since the interface became less well-defined, as noted in Kevin
McGrattan et al. (2024b).
3.1.4.4
Temperature at Ceiling Level
In addition, the temperature was measured 0.40 m below the ceiling at the nine measure-
ment points shown in figure 3.5 on the preceding page, as an explicit point value repre-
senting the thermal conditions near the ceiling of the building. The placement was based
on the expectation that the highest temperatures occurred at ceiling level and therefore
served as an indicator of the critical temperature in the smoke layer, both with respect to
the critical steel temperature and to flashover, if this were to be evaluated. This temper-
ature was used directly in the assessment of the effectiveness of the smoke ventilation
system.
3.1.4.5
Volume flow
In the model, the volume flow was measured at all openings serving as smoke ventila-
tion openings and replacement-air openings, with their locations shown in figure 3.4 on
Master in Fire Safety
27
==PAGE:54==
page 25. With respect to flow direction, positive, negative, and net values were measured
at each opening. The flow was determined as the integral over the geometric area of the
opening.
The volume flow was used to assess the effect of varying the angle between the two
replacement-air openings on the inflow to the building, as well as on the internal temper-
ature, in relation to increasing wind speed and fire size. This parameter was therefore
central to evaluating the overall performance of the smoke ventilation system.
3.1.5
Design Fire
A design fire is typically characterised by its heat release rate, HRR, smoke production,
and growth rate, parameters that collectively govern the thermal environment and smoke
movement within the compartment. (Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022; Hurley, 2018)
The fire size influences the gas temperature in the compartment, since a higher heat re-
lease rate gives rise to a relatively greater temperature increase compared with a smaller
fire. This is evident from equation (2.19) on page 19, where the heat release rate en-
ters directly into the determination of the temperature. It follows that the fire size also
affects the buoyancy-driven flow in the compartment. Likewise, a larger fire produces a
greater mass of combustion products that must be vented compared with a smaller fire,
as illustrated for example in equation (2.17) on page 19.
3.1.5.1
Fire Size
In the study reported by Wegrzyński et al. (2023), a design fire with a moderate heat re-
lease rate (HRR) of 8 MW was applied for a warehouse building, while in another study
reported by Svensgaard and Brøndum (2024), an HRR of 10 MW was applied as a mod-
erate fire for a warehouse building.
To investigate the influence of fire size on the effectiveness of smoke ventilation under
variation of the angle between the replacement air openings, both with and without wind
effects, the present project applied design fires with HRR’s of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW.
An HRR of 5 MW and 10 MW corresponds to stacked pallets with heights of 2 m and 4 m,
respectively, while 20 MW corresponds to two stacks of pallets, each with a height of 4 m,
placed side by side (Hurley, 2018).
Since the three design fires were based on wooden pallets, the heat of combustion and the
soot yield in the simulations were set to values corresponding to wood, namely 17.5 kJ/kg
and 0.015 g/g, respectively (Hurley, 2018).
3.1.5.2
Growth phase
It is assumed that the growth rate for the fire load, Q, follows a parabolic curve as de-
scribed by:
Q = αt2
(3.1)
where:
Q
Fire load
[kW]
α
Fire growth rate
[kW/s2]
t
time
[s]
The fire growth rate depends on the burning of the material, as solid fuels and liquid fuels
have different heat release rates. However, the arrangement of the fuel also plays a role.
For CFD fire modeling, α, is normally characterized by the heat release rate and growth
rate curves with slow, medium, fast or ultra-fast fire growth rate.
28
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:55==
In this study, the focus was on steady-state conditions rather than on the transient de-
velopment of parameters such as temperature and visibility. Specifically, the analysis
considered the stabilised temperature distribution in the room and the airflow through the
replacement-air openings and the roof exhaust openings of the building. Consequently,
the fire growth rate was set to 10 s, regardless of the fire size.
3.1.5.3

An overview of the input parameters applied for defining the fire source in FDS, depending
on the fire size, is provided in table 3.3.

3.1.6
Wind modelling
The following section describes the boundary conditions applied in the modelling of the
wind field, including the geometric extent of the computational domain, the selected wind
profile model, assumptions regarding thermal stability, and the reference wind speeds
used.
3.1.6.1
Computational Domain for Wind
To ensure a robust computational domain in which the wind profile was preserved and
unwanted boundary effects were minimised in the CFD simulations, the domain was con-
structed according to the description in section 2.3 on page 15, based on the assumptions
in Wegrzyński et al. (2018). The constructed domain is illustrated in figure 3.6 on the fol-
lowing page. It should be noted, however, that the wind profile was only preserved in the
free inflow region, as the interaction with the building naturally induced turbulence and a
reorganisation of the flow field in the building’s vicinity.
The building was modelled with an external height of 10.4 m, which formed the basis for
defining the size of the computational domain, summarised in table 3.4, following the
recommendations in Wegrzyński et al. (2018).
Table 3.4: Distances from the building in the model to the boundaries.

Figure 3.6: Computational domain in the model.
As shown in table 3.4 on the preceding page, the constructed computational domain sat-
isfied the recommended requirements in Wegrzyński et al. (2018), thereby minimising the
risk of boundary effects and errors in the flow field around the building and ensuring more
robust results.
In the construction of the computational domain, both the mesh size and the value of
y+ were taken into account, since these parameters directly affected the accuracy of the
model, as noted in Wegrzyński et al. (2018) and Ramponi and Blocken (2012). The mesh
size influenced the accuracy of the calculations throughout the domain, while the y+ value
described the resolution of the flow near the walls. The pressure coefficient, cp, was par-
ticularly sensitive to the near-wall resolution and was therefore directly influenced by the
mesh size, as noted by Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b). The two parameters were interre-
lated, as a finer mesh near the walls resulted in lower y+ values. To assess the sensitivity
of the model, a sensitivity analysis of both parameters was carried out. A sensitivity study
of different cell sizes in relation to the pressure coefficient and y+ is presented in subsec-
tion 3.2.2 on page 35.
3.1.6.2
Wind Profiles
FDS provides four methods for modelling wind conditions, which differ in complexity and
realism, and can be summarised as follows (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b):
• Wind Method 1: Specified Wind Speed and Direction (SWAD)
A simple, uniform wind field with constant speed and direction is applied through-
out the domain, without accounting for temperature effects or atmospheric stability
(Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b).
• Wind Method 2: Monin–Obukhov Similarity Theory (MOST)
An atmospheric wind profile is generated based on Monin–Obukhov Similarity The-
ory (MOST), which incorporates both terrain roughness and atmospheric stability,
resulting in a logarithmic wind-speed distribution as a function of height (Kevin Mc-
Grattan et al., 2024b).
30
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:57==
• Wind Method 3: Advanced Meteorological Concepts (AMC)
A fundamental set of meteorological parameters is employed to describe large-scale
wind fields, making the method primarily applicable to simulations of computational
domains extending over several kilometres (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b).
• Wind Method 4: The “Wall of Wind” (TWW)
A power-law wind-speed profile is imposed on a vertical boundary surface of the
computational domain to establish the wind field (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b).
The choice of method depends on the purpose and scale of the simulation, as well as the
availability of meteorological input data. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b).
The SWAD method was applied in the sensitivity analysis of cell size in subsection 3.2.2
on page 35, including an assessment of the pressure coefficient, cp, and y+ around the
façades and roof of the building, since a detailed representation of the atmospheric wind
profile was of minor importance in this context. For the main analysis of the replacement-
air opening configurations, the MOST method was applied, as it provided an atmospheric
wind profile that accounted for both roughness and stability. The AMC method was disre-
garded, as it was intended for very large domains and required extensive meteorological
input, while the TWW method was not applied because it did not reproduce the atmo-
spheric boundary layer with the same level of fidelity as MOST (Kevin McGrattan et al.,
2024b).
In the final simulations conducted to investigate the efficiency of the replacement-air open-
ings, the Monin–Obukhov Similarity Theory (MOST) was applied to define the boundary
conditions for the wind field. This ensured that the influence of atmospheric stability,
terrain roughness, and reference wind speeds was consistently represented across all
scenarios. The choice of MOST was motivated by its widespread use in atmospheric
boundary-layer modelling and its ability to capture the combined effects of wind and ther-
mal stratification on flow development around buildings.
In the present study, a reference height of 10.0 m was applied, with reference wind speeds
varied between 0 m/s, 8 m/s, and 14 m/s, as defined in subsection 3.1.6.4 on page 33. A
terrain roughness of 0.03 was applied, corresponding to open terrain without major trees,
hills, or buildings; i.e., the building was assumed to be located in open, flat surroundings.
3.1.6.3
Atmospheric Stability
Atmospheric stability is a key parameter in wind modelling, as it governs the turbulence
characteristics around the building within the computational domain. In this context, at-
mospheric stability is defined as the influence of temperature stratification on buoyancy-
driven turbulence and mixing in the atmospheric boundary layer. The solar altitude, to-
gether with the assumptions adopted in the MOST method, are central to describing the
atmospheric conditions associated with stability.(Turner, 2020)
Atmospheric stability is generally classified into three categories: unstable, neutral, and
stable conditions.
An unstable atmosphere is characterised by moderate to strong solar radiation heating
the air close to the ground, making it warmer than the air aloft. The warmer air rises,
generating large turbulent eddies, which results in strong mixing in the atmosphere and
an increased turbulence intensity (Plovsing and Dam, 2012; Turner, 2020).
In contrast, a stable atmosphere is characterised by an almost constant air temperature
across the vertical layers, typically occurring during nighttime cooling or under weak solar
radiation. Under these conditions, the air close to the surface has a reduced tendency to
Master in Fire Safety
31
==PAGE:58==
rise, and only minimal turbulence develops. The atmosphere is therefore referred to as
stable, as the degree of mixing is limited (Plovsing and Dam, 2012; Turner, 2020).
The neutral state is characterised by only a weak variation in the temperature gradient
with height above the ground, such that the atmosphere is neither unstable nor stable.
This typically occurs under overcast conditions, or on days with relatively strong winds
combined with moderate solar radiation. Under these conditions, a moderate level of
turbulence is observed (Plovsing and Dam, 2012; Turner, 2020).
In table 3.5, Pasquill’s stability classes A–G are listed, relating atmospheric stability to
different weather conditions such as solar radiation, time of day, and wind speed, and
linking these to the indicative values of the Monin–Obukhov Length, L, in accordance
with both Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b) and Kahl and Chapman (2018).
Table 3.5: Stability classes from Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b).
