In table 3.6, “Strong” corresponds to solar altitudes above 60°, “Moderate” to altitudes
between 35° - 60°, and “Slight” to altitudes below 35°, according to Kahl and Chapman
(2018). As documented in appendix C on page 131, the solar altitude in Denmark does
not exceed 60°for latitudes around 54° - 58°, and therefore the “Moderate” category rep-
resents the most critical yet realistic situation for this study.
The solar altitude has a direct influence on the surface heat flux, since a higher solar
altitude increases solar radiation and thereby enhances the heat transfer from the surface
to the atmosphere. This intensifies mixing in the lower atmospheric layer and increases
turbulence intensity around the building, referring to (Kahl and Chapman, 2018; Turner,
2020).
32
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:59==
As documented in appendix C on page 131, the solar altitude in Denmark does not exceed
60° for latitudes between 54°–58°. Consequently, only the category “Moderate” daytime
insolation is considered further, as it represents the most critical yet realistic situation for
this study. As noted in subsection 2.2.2 on page 13, the Monin–Obukhov length, L, is
directly related to the surface temperature and thus to turbulence intensity.
It was therefore concluded that for buildings in Denmark, “Moderate” daytime insolation
could be applied. Based on the given wind speeds, the stability conditions were classified
as unstable for low wind speeds (0 m/s) and neutral for higher wind speeds (8 m/s and
14 m/s).
3.1.6.4
Wind speed
The average wind speed in Denmark depends on the geographical location of the building,
with mean values of 5.1 m/s in Northern Jutland and 4.0 m/s in Western and Southern
Zealand. As noted in Wang (2013), the highest wind speeds are generally observed along
the western coastline. Since the purpose of this study was to analyse the influence of wind
speed on the placement of replacement air openings, three representative wind speeds
of 0 m/s, 8 m/s, and 14 m/s were qualitatively selected and discussed in the following. In
the present study, only one wind direction was considered.
Wind speed of 0 m/s
In the absence of wind, the flow patterns are driven solely by buoyancy induced flow
from the fire, where air is entrained through the replacement air openings to maintain the
mass balance. The no-wind scenario thus constitutes a reference case in which the flow is
unaffected by wind conditions. Analysing this situation provided insight into the functioning
of smoke ventilation under optimal conditions, where the flow is driven exclusively by
temperature differences.
Wind speed of 8 m/s
According to Cappelen and Jørgensen (1999), wind speeds in the range of 5 – 10 m/s fre-
quently occur in Denmark. The choice of 8 m/s as a reference value therefore represented
a typical and realistic wind load on the building, providing a suitable basis for evaluating
the interaction between thermal smoke ventilation and wind effects. A wind speed of 8 m/s
corresponds to a “fresh breeze” on the Beaufort scale, and therefore constitutes a mod-
erate load on the building. In this situation, the buoyancy driven flow is still expected to
be significant depending on the fire size, but the wind increasingly contributes to pres-
sure effects around the replacement air openings. This enabled analysis of the combined
influence of fire induced buoyancy and wind driven flows.
Wind speed of 14 m/s
According to Cappelen and Jørgensen (1999), wind speeds around 14 m/s occur less
frequently than moderate wind speeds in Denmark, yet they are still recorded with some
regularity. The choice of 14 m/s as a reference value therefore represented a critical wind
load on the building, allowing assessment of the robustness of thermal smoke ventilation
under extreme but realistic conditions. A wind speed of 14 m/s corresponds to a “strong
gale” on the Beaufort scale and thus constitutes a significantly higher load compared
with 8 m/s. At this level, pressure effects are expected to increasingly dominate over
buoyancy, although the relative contribution remains dependent on fire size. As a result,
the effectiveness of smoke ventilation may be substantially reduced and could potentially
lead to backflow through the replacement air openings.
Master in Fire Safety
33
==PAGE:60==
3.2
Validation of Modelling with FDS
In the following, a validation is presented for the two key elements simulated in FDS,
namely fire and wind. In this context, validation was concerned with assessing whether
the numerical results converged towards grid-independent solutions when the mesh res-
olution was refined. Since no experimental data were available for direct comparison, the
accuracy of the model was evaluated by examining the sensitivity of the results to cell
size. This approach was applied in subsection 3.2.1 and subsection 3.2.2 on the facing
page.
3.2.1
Validation of Fire Model
To ensure that the simulation results were independent of the selected mesh resolution,
a study was conducted on the influence of cell size on smoke layer height, upper smoke
layer temperature, and volume flow through the openings. For a detailed account of this
study, reference was made to appendix B on page 113, while a summary was provided
here. For this purpose, the model shown in figure 3.7 was applied, representing the
simulated building with six smoke ventilation openings and two replacement air openings.
Figure 3.7: Model of building used for mesh validation
The calculations were carried out with a fire size of 5 MW, as this case imposed the most
stringent requirements on mesh resolution. This was because the characteristic fire di-
ameter, D, was smaller than for 10 MW and 20 MW, resulting in a lower ratio of D/δx
and thereby requiring a finer grid resolution to correctly model the buoyancy driven flow.
Simulations were performed with uniform cell sizes of 0.10 m, 0.20 m, and 0.40 m across
the entire computational domain.
The results showed that smoke layer height and temperature converged at cell sizes of
0.10 m and 0.20 m, with differences limited to 3% - 7% for smoke layer height and 2% - 5%
for the upper smoke layer temperature. When the cell size was increased from 0.10 m
to 0.40 m, the differences increased to 6% - 13% for smoke layer height and 8% - 17%
for temperature. A similar trend was observed for the volume flow through the openings,
where 0.40 m yielded values that were 4% - 16% higher than those obtained with 0.10 m.
34
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:61==
Based on these results, it was concluded that a cell size of 0.10 m represented the grid
independent solution. As described in subsection 3.2.2, a cell size of 0.05 m was addi-
tionally applied along the building façades, and the same resolution was therefore used
across the openings. Within the global flow field inside the building, the cell size was
maintained at 0.10 m for all fire sizes.
Furthermore, a control of the defined heat release rate, HRR, and mass loss rate, MLR,
was carried out for fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, which were reproduced cor-
rectly with a cell size of 0.10 m. As shown in appendix B on page 113, the simulated
values are consistent with the prescribed ones, with HRR deviating by less than 0.1%
from the defined value, while the calculated MLR of 0.29 kg/s, 0.59 kg/s, and 1.18 kg/s
are accurately reproduced in the simulations.
3.2.2
Validation of Wind model
The purpose of this section is to validate the wind model so that it can be coupled with the
fire model and thereby yield reliable results for the interaction between fire and wind. The
validation aims to obtain usable results for wind pressure and boundary layer modelling.
In the following, the pressure coefficient cp and the dimensionless wall distance y+ are
evaluated for a simplified FDS model.
3.2.2.1
Model set-up
In the computational wind model, a simple cubic building with dimensions of 10.4 × 10.4
× 10.4 m was inserted, as shown in figure 3.8. The computational domain extended from
x = −40.4 m to 50.0 m, y = −60.4 m to 50.0 m, and z = 0 m to 40.0 m, as illustrated in the
figure.
Figure 3.8: Mesh validation for wind.
Master in Fire Safety
35
==PAGE:62==
A constant wind speed was prescribed at the inlet boundary with values of 2 m/s, 8 m/s,
and 14 m/s, used solely in the sensitivity analysis to assess the influence of wind around
the building and the corresponding requirements for cell size.
To analyse the wind conditions around the building, measurement points were defined in
both vertical and horizontal planes relative to the wind direction, in order to record cp and
y+. These measurement points are indicated in figure 3.9 and figure 3.10.
Figure 3.9: Horizontal (in relation to wind
direction) measuring points.
Figure 3.10: Vertical (in relation to wind
direction) measuring points.
3.2.2.2
Qualitative Assessment
As an initial analysis of the airflow patterns around the building, a qualitative assessment
of flow field visualisations from FDS has been performed for different mesh sizes. In
the following, only results for a wind velocity of 8 m/s are shown. In figure 3.11 on the
facing page, the flow field visualisations for 8 m/s are presented, with the wind direction
coming from the top of the images. The different mesh sizes of 0.025 m, 0.05 m, 0.10 m
and 0.20 m refer to the innermost cell sizes adjacent to the surface of the building. The
airflows have been evaluated at a height of 2.0 m above the ground plane.
It is clearly evident from the figures that cell sizes of 0.025 m and 0.05 m provide a much
higher level of detail around the building within the boundary layer. Comparing 0.05 m
with 0.20 m shows that, with the 0.20 m mesh, the airflows deflect earlier at the building
corners and form larger recirculation zones, with the flow carried further away from the
building’s Boundaries. With the 0.05 m mesh, the flow remains closer to the building’s
sides, while the recirculation zones are smaller. This indicates that the boundary layer is
not sufficiently resolved at a cell size of 0.20 m, which alters the flow around the building.
Turbulent conditions are also observed around the building in all scenarios, which can
certainly occur; however, with 0.10 m and 0.20 m meshes some airflows are seen to be
carried up along the building in the opposite direction to the wind direction. This is not ex-
pected, and it indicates that a mesh with cell sizes of 0.10 m – 0.20 m provides insufficient
boundary-layer resolution, leading to imprecise values for the pressure coefficient cp. It
follows that the flow around the building may be error-prone due to incomplete resolution
of the boundary layer.

(b) 0.05 m
(c) 0.10 m

0.20 m) of the mesh shown 2 m above terrain.
3.2.2.3
Quantitative assessment
In figure 3.12 on the next page, the values of cp and y+ are given for the measurement
points in the vertical section aligned with the wind direction.
In accordance with subsection 2.3.2 on page 18, a y+ value between 30 – 100 provides
a sufficient resolution of the boundary layer around the building. From figure 3.12 on
the next page, it follows that with cell sizes of 0.025 m, the y+ values are approximately
within this interval, albeit with a few points just outside. As the cell size increases, the y+
values grow progressively, and the graph clearly shows that for 0.40 m Cell sizes, several
y+ values exceed 1,000. This indicates that a sufficient resolution of the boundary layer
around the building is achieved with 0.025 m cell sizes. It should, however, be noted that
the simulation time increases markedly as the cell size decreases.
Master in Fire Safety
37
==PAGE:64==
Figure 3.12: Pressure coefficient cp and y+-value in 13 different measure points on the
building and 5 different mesh-size with wind speed 8 m/s.
In the lower part of the graph, measurements of the cp value are shown, plotted as points
connected by curves to make it easier to compare the cell sizes. The graph shows that a
cell size of 0.40 m differs markedly from the curves for 0.20 m and 0.10 m as well as 0.05
and 0.025 m. The curve progression with 0.20 m and 0.10 m cell sizes also differs from that
of 0.05 m and 0.025 m, whereas the curves for 0.05 m and 0.025 m approximately follow
each other. This indicates that, for the pressure coefficient, a cell size of 0.05 m may be
sufficient to simulate the pressure conditions around the building. For further analysis of
the curve progression for 0.025 m and 0.05 m, refer to appendix C on page 131.
In figure 3.13 on the next page, the values of cp and y+ are given for the measurement
points in the horizontal section aligned with the wind direction.
According to figure 3.13 on the facing page, the same picture emerges for the y+ value
as in figure 3.12, although there are some measurement points for a cell size of 0.10 m
that yield a lower y+ value than a cell size of 0.05 m. This is typical for points located in
strongly turbulent conditions, i.e. around the building’s corners where the wind impinges.
In the lower part of the graph, there remains a tendency for turbulent conditions around the
building corner where the wind impinges. A clear tendency is observed that the curve pro-
gression for cell sizes of 0.025 m and 0.05 m follow each other, albeit without coinciding,
as was the tendency in figure 3.12. This indicates that the turbulent conditions around
the building’s corners in the horizontal section are more complex than at the building’s
corners in the vertical section.

Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:65==
Figure 3.13: Pressure coefficient cp and y+-value in 7 different measure points on the
building and 5 different mesh-size with wind speed 8 m/s.
3.2.2.4
Intermediate conclusion for Validation of Wind Model
The results showed that coarse meshes (≥0.10 m) under-resolved the boundary layer
and flow separation, which could introduce non-physical flow patterns. With a cell size of
0.05 m, the dominant aerodynamic features were reproduced with only minor deviations
compared with a cell size of 0.025 m, but at a substantially reduced computational cost.
A cell size of 0.05 m was therefore assessed to represent a robust compromise for further
studies, where the focus was on combined fire and wind conditions.
It followed from the above that an excessively coarse mesh could lead to systematic errors
in cp and thus in the pressure distribution around the building, which in turn might affect the
evaluation of the effectiveness of smoke ventilation. Conversely, an excessively fine mesh
would make the simulations computationally impractical. The choice of mesh resolution
therefore represented a balance between numerical accuracy and practical feasibility,
and within this balance, 0.05 m was found to be the most appropriate resolution for a
quantitative assessment of smoke ventilation effectiveness.
3.3
Verification of modeling tool (FDS)
In the following, the simulation tool FDS is verified with respect to the various elements
included in the simulation. Verification is regarded as a method by which the calculations
are assessed for their ability to reproduce the expected modelling results. In the following,
three sub-components are verified:
• Geometry
• Fire
• Wind
Master in Fire Safety
39
==PAGE:66==
This section evaluates whether FDS is suitable for modelling the physical elements in the
project as outlined above. FDS is a field-based fire modelling tool developed by NIST
that solves the Navier–Stokes equations and employs Large Eddy Simulation (LES) for
turbulence. The focus is on smoke and heat transport in fire-driven flows within buildings.
(Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024a,d)
FDS is subject to extensive verification and validation results, documented in the official
FDS manuals. The tool follows the framework of ASTM E1355 for the evaluation of de-
terministic fire models. These sources support that FDS can deliver reliable results when
used within its validated domain of applicability. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024a,d)
3.3.1
Verification regarding the geometry
To verify FDS as a useful program, the point of departure is Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024a),
in which Section 3.88 presents an analysis of a room with openings and varying fire sizes.
The purpose is to verify a model against exact values measured in a fire affected room.
In figure 3.14, the geometry used for the verification is shown.
Figure 3.14: FDS verification of geometry reproduced with permission from Kevin Mc-
Grattan et al. (2024a, National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST)).
The geometry used in the verification is, in principle, comparable to the model set-up in
the present project. It is therefore assumed that this verification can serve as a basis for
considering the model in the present project valid, provided that the FDS model limitations
are not exceeded. These are as follows.
FDS represents geometry on a Cartesian grid, and all objects snap to the cells. To avoid
numerical errors, the model has been geometrically optimised such that there are no
blind corners, narrow recesses, or details below the cell size (∆x). In addition, it has
been ensured that relevant openings span multiple cells across. Thin constructions are
modelled as one cell in the flow geometry. Openings are defined as “HOLE” or “VENT”
with the necessary margins and correct handling of overlap and perforation, as described
in Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b).
It is concluded from the above that FDS can be used as the simulation programme for the
selected geometry in the present master project.
40
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:67==
3.3.2
Verification regarding the fire
In appendix B on page 113, a comparison between the hand calculations and the CFD
model results shows that both the smoke layer height and the upper smoke layer tem-
perature are reproduced within comparable ranges. In the hand calculations, a Cv-factor
in the range 0.3 - 0.7 was applied, with the best agreement with the CFD simulations ob-
tained for values of 0.3 and 0.4. Figure 3.15 and figure 3.16 on the following page present
the smoke layer height and the upper smoke layer temperature, respectively, for both the
hand calculations and the CFD simulation.
Figure 3.15: Smoke Layer Height from hand calculation and CFD model with cell size of
0.10 m.
Compared with the hand calculations, the CFD model predicts smoke layer heights that
are 4.2% - 13.5% higher and upper smoke layer temperatures that are 2.9% - 4.4% higher.
These deviations nevertheless remain within the documented uncertainty of empirical
models (Johansson, 2016; Heskestad, 1984; The National Fire Protection Association,
2024).
It should also be noted that FDS is itself associated with some uncertainty in the simulation
of smoke and temperature distribution. This aspect has not been examined further, as
the purpose of the verification is solely to demonstrate that the smoke layer height and
temperature are at a comparable level with the hand calculations.
On the basis of this verification, it is concluded that FDS can be applied as a CFD model
for fire modelling.
Master in Fire Safety
41
==PAGE:68==
Figure 3.16: Upper layer temperature from hand calculation and CFD model with cell size
of 0.10 m.
3.3.3
Verification regarding the wind
To assess whether Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) could be applied to simulate wind
conditions, a verification study was conducted based on a simple reference setup. In
this verification, an empty rectangular domain without geometry or obstacles was es-
tablished, where an atmospheric wind profile was implemented according to the Monin–
Obukhov logarithmic wind profile for a neutrally stratified atmosphere, as described in
subsection 2.2.2 on page 13.
In FDS, the wind profile was defined with reference wind speeds of 8 m/s and 14 m/s mea-
sured at 10 m above ground level, and the profile was imposed as an inflow boundary at
the upstream outer boundary of the domain. The domain was used solely to verify that the
applied profile was reproduced correctly in the numerical model, without interference from
buildings or other objects. To confirm this, measurement points were placed at different
heights, and the time-averaged wind speeds from the FDS simulations were compared
with the analytical expression derived from Monin–Obukhov theory. A comparison of the
wind profiles is shown in figure 3.17 on the facing page.
The results demonstrated that FDS was able to reproduce the logarithmic wind profile
with reasonable accuracy. Deviations could partly be attributed to insufficient resolution
of the boundary layer, as discussed in subsection 3.2.2 on page 35, even though the
computational domain was empty.
This verification therefore indicated that FDS can be used to simulate atmospheric wind
profiles, since comparison with the analytical logarithmic profile confirmed that the model
is suitable for further investigations, such as wind effects on buildings.

erence wind speeds of 8 m/s and 14 m/s.

4
Results and discussion
In this section, the results from the completed fire engineering simulations are analysed
with the aim of assessing the buildings fire safety with respect to flashover and the pro-
tection of the load-bearing structures. The simulations are carried out using FDS, with
a focus on key parameters such as volume flow through openings, the development of
air temperature, smoke spread, and visibility. The results are processed and presented
both graphically and numerically to enable an assessment of whether they influence one
another, and to what extent they affect the effectiveness of thermal smoke ventilation.
The purpose of the calculations is to investigate the effectiveness of the thermal smoke
ventilation as a function of the placement of the replacement air openings in the buildings
façade under different conditions. For this purpose, three key parameters are systemat-
ically varied: the fire size, the wind velocity, and the angle between two replacement air
openings.
Together with the presentation of the results, these are continuously discussed. The re-
sults are presented on the basis of the most important graphs and numerical values. For
more detailed numerical values or supplementary graphs, references are generally made
to the Appendix, where these can be found. In evaluating the results, a specifically de-
fined set of measurement points has been used for air temperature, smoke layer height,
and visibility, as well as volume flow measurements through the openings. These mea-
surement points are shown in figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Measuring points for temperature, visibility and smoke layer height (left), re-
placement air openings and smoke ventilation openings (right) in the FDS model.
For volume flow measurements, readings were taken in all openings, covering positive,
negative, and net flow. For air temperature, smoke layer height, and visibility, measure-
ments have been taken on a grid assessed to capture a representative subset of the
measurement parameters in the building. In what follows, when an angle of 70°, 112° or
150° is given, this denotes the angle between the two replacement air openings relative
to the central point of the building, as shown in figure 4.1.
Master in Fire Safety
45
==PAGE:72==
4.1
Limitations
The analysis was based on an idealised model, where geometry, material properties, and
boundary conditions were fixed as described in chapter 3 on page 21. The validity of
the results is therefore limited to the investigated ranges of fire sizes, wind speeds, and
door angles. The results are indicative and cannot be directly transferred to multi-room,
complex, or otherwise different buildings.
Fire
The fire growth phase was shortened in the simulations to more rapidly achieve quasi-
steady conditions. Results were based on the final 50 seconds of each scenario, and the
growth phase was therefore assumed to have limited influence. Three discrete fire sizes
(5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW) were applied. These were considered representative of the
building’s use but cannot be assumed to cover all possible fire scenarios.
Furthermore, the study applied only a single value for heat of combustion and soot yield
corresponding to wood, meaning that the results cannot be directly transferred to other
materials with higher values, such as plastics.
Wind direction
Only one wind direction, perpendicular to the façade, was modelled, which was consistent
with the project’s objective, as wind direction was not included as a parameter variation.
The results can therefore not be directly generalised to other wind directions, where the
façade pressure distribution would differ.
Wind variation
A stationary wind profile without temporal variations or gusts was applied, unlike natural
wind conditions. The results therefore represent an idealised case and cannot readily be
transferred to situations with varying or turbulent wind conditions.
Building geometry and openings
The building geometry was idealised, and material properties were defined as homoge-
neous. Smoke ventilation openings were modelled as simple “holes” in the roof construc-
tion. Compared with how such openings are constructed in practice, this simplification
may alter the calculated flow through the openings. Replacement-air openings were like-
wise modelled as “holes”, but are not expected to have the same significance compared
with the smoke ventilation openings in the roof.
Mesh and numerical models
The CFD model was based on a grid that represented a compromise between resolution
and computational cost. Sensitivity studies for wind were carried out, but grid indepen-
dence was not systematically documented for all cases. Coarser cells outside the build-
ing may reduce the accuracy of the wind–building interaction. The model employed the
default FDS settings for turbulence, combustion, and radiation. No separate sensitivity
analyses were conducted for these aspects, and their influence on the results was not
evaluated in this project.
Terrain
An important factor is the representation of terrain roughness. In this study, roughness
was included via the parameter z0 in Monin–Obukhov Similarity Theory, ensuring consis-
tency in the wind profile. However, no separate analyses were conducted of how varia-
tions in roughness classes (e.g., open land, forest, or urban areas) might influence the
results. Furthermore, the actual surroundings of the building – such as terrain variations
and neighbouring buildings – were not modelled.
46
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:73==
Validation and verification
Validation of mesh resolution inside the building was carried out based on a 5 MW fire, as
this imposed the most stringent resolution requirements (D∗/∆x). The mesh resolution
outside the building was validated for wind speeds of 2 m/s, 8 m/s, and 14 m/s. In addition,
single reference verifications were performed for both fire and wind modelling using the
same fire size and wind speeds of 8 m/s and 14 m/s, which were also applied in the final
scenarios. Grid independence and consistency with analytical expressions were thus
demonstrated for selected reference cases, but cannot be considered fully generalisable
to all combinations.
Data processing
Data processing was based on moving averages over the final 50 seconds of the sim-
ulations, assuming quasi-steady conditions had been established. Measurement point
5, located directly above the fire, recorded locally elevated temperatures that were not
representative of the smoke layer, and this point was therefore evaluated separately.
Statistical models
The dataset consisted of a total of 27 CFD simulations, where three input parameters
(fire size, wind speed, and door angle) were each varied at three levels, representing a
full factorial design with one observation per combination. This provided a structured but
limited dataset, which influenced the choice of analysis methods.
Classical statistical models such as ANOVA, linear regression with interactions, or Gen-
eralized Additive Models (GAM) require either independent replicated measurements for
each combination (replication) or a larger number of observations to reliably estimate un-
certainty and residual distribution (Ross, 2014; Devore L. Jay, 2011)
These assumptions were not fulfilled in the present dataset, where each data point repre-
sented a single CFD simulation. Moreover, the variation within each simulation reflected
physical fluctuations in a transient solution rather than statistically independent repetitions.
For this reason, statistical inference models were not applied as the basis for evaluating
the effect of the three input parameters.
4.2
Airflow Dependence on the Angle between Replacement
Air Openings
To examine how the angle between the two replacement-air doors affects the internal
airflow, the air velocity 2 m above the floor is presented for three angles (70°, 112°, and
150°) in figure 4.2 on the next page, figure 4.3 on page 50, and figure 4.4 on page 53,
respectively. Each figure comprises nine panels arranged in a 3 × 3 grid, where columns
represent increasing wind speed (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s from left to right), while rows cor-
respond to increasing fire size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW from top to bottom).
The visualisations depict representative states of the flow field for all 27 scenarios; for
temporal evolution and variability, velocities at additional time instants are provided in
appendix D on page 147, which underpins the subsequent analysis.

For the configuration with a 70° angle between the two replacement-air openings, the
doors are positioned symmetrically relative to the building’s vertical centreline, both lo-
cated on the leeward side with respect to the wind direction. The airflow through the
replacement air openings is therefore primarily governed by the negative pressure gen-
erated on the leeward façade in combination with the fire induced thermal buoyancy.
48
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:75==
In addition, pressure variations caused by roof suction (negative pressure on the roof
surface) also influence the flow, as the suction gradually decreases towards the leeward
side depending on the wind velocity. Further documentation of this relationship is provided
in appendix D on page 147, where the volume flow through the smoke exhaust openings is
shown as a function of fire size, wind velocity, and the angle between the two replacement
air openings.
4.2.1.1
Wind speed of 0 m/s
As shown in figure 4.2 on the facing page, the flow field is generally calm and approxi-
mately symmetric. For a fire size of 5 MW, air velocities through the replacement-air open-
ings are typically in the range of 1.5 m/s - 3.0 m/s, with smaller regions reaching 3.0 m/s -
4.5 m/s. When the fire size increases to 10 MW and 20 MW, the additional heat release
intensifies the thermal buoyancy, creating a greater demand for replacement air and con-
sequently higher inflow velocities.
Average velocities through the openings rise to around 4.5 m/s, with local peaks up to
6 m/s at 20 MW. In addition, the area characterised by velocities above 1.5 m/s expands
significantly, indicating a more extensive horizontal air movement inside the building.
Thus, at a wind speed of 0 m/s, the airflow is governed solely by fire induced thermal
buoyancy, resulting in an approximately symmetric distribution about the building’s cen-
treline across all fire sizes.
4.2.1.2
Wind speed of 8 m/s
Compared with the scenario at 0 m/s, figure 4.2 on the preceding page shows that increas-
ing the wind speed to 8 m/s markedly intensifies the inflow through the two replacement
air openings. For a fire size of 5 MW, larger areas with velocities up to 4.5 m/s appear,
representing a clear increase relative to the case in the absence of wind. At 10 MW, ve-
locities through the openings rise further to 4.5 m/s - 6.0 m/s. However, when the fire size
is increased from 10 MW to 20 MW, the inflow velocity remains at a similar level, sug-
gesting that the flow is now primarily wind driven rather than pressure-driven by the fire.
Hence, increasing the wind speed from 0 m/s to 8 m/s reinforces the tendency for higher
replacement air demand with increasing fire size in order to maintain mass balance.
At fire sizes of 10 MW and 20 MW, a distinct asymmetry develops in the distribution of
the inflow. Door 1 (right-hand side) delivers a more stable and concentrated jet directly
into the compartment, while Door 2 (left-hand side) contributes with a more dispersed
and deflected stream. Given the symmetric placement of the two doors about the cen-
treline, a more uniform inflow distribution might have been expected. This discrepancy is
attributed to the dynamic wind effects, including local pressure variations and the recir-
culation zone forming on the leeward façade, both of which strongly influence the airflow
near the openings.
4.2.1.3
Wind speed of 14 m/s
As shown in figure 4.2 on the facing page, increasing the wind speed from 8 m/s to 14 m/s
results in higher inflow velocities through the replacement air openings. For a fire size of
5 MW, the velocity rises from 4.5 m/s to approximately 6 m/s, while at 10 MW it increases
from 4.5 m/s - 6.0 m/s to around 7.5 m/s. At 20 MW, a corresponding rise is observed from
6.0 m/s - 7.5 m/s.
It should be noted, however, that at a wind speed of 14 m/s a pronounced roof suction
develops, leading to higher outflow velocities through smoke exhaust openings OP3 and
OP6, which are located closest to the wind exposed façade (see figure 3.4 on page 25).
The suction diminishes across the roof, which may even cause inflow through OP1 and
OP4.
Master in Fire Safety
49
==PAGE:76==
As seen in appendix D on page 170, this phenomenon is particularly evident at 14 m/s,
where it partly explains the increased inflow velocity through the replacement air openings
at fire sizes of 5 MW and 10 MW. The effect is less pronounced at 20 MW, which may
indicate that the internal overpressure generated by the fire counteracts the roof suction.

For the configuration with a 112° angle between the two replacement air openings, the
doors are no longer placed symmetrically relative to the building’s vertical centreline. Door
1 retains its position on the leeward side, while Door 2 is moved to the side façade, aligned
parallel to the wind direction. The airflow through the replacement air openings is there-
fore primarily governed by the pressure difference between the two openings in combina-
tion with the fire-induced thermal buoyancy. In addition, roof suction may also affect the
flow, as it gradually decreases towards the leeward side depending on the wind velocity.
Further documentation of this relationship is provided in appendix D on page 147.
4.2.2.1
Wind speed of 0 m/s
At a 112° angle, a distinct circular airflow develops in the centre of the building, regardless
of whether the fire size is 5 MW, 10 MW, or 20 MW, as shown in figure 4.3 on the facing
page. This phenomenon arises from the horizontal inflow through Door 2 interacting with
the fire induced thermal buoyancy, which generates a rotating motion in the horizontal
plane. For a fire size of 5 MW, velocities through the replacement air openings are typically
in the range of 1.5 m/s - 4.5 m/s, increasing to at least 4.5 m/s at fire sizes of 10 MW and
20 MW. A similar trend to that observed at a 70° angle is evident: as the fire grows, more
heat is produced, intensifying the buoyancy and increasing the demand for replacement
air, which in turn drives larger inflows through the openings. Thus, at a wind speed of
0 m/s, the airflow is characterised by a centrally rotating pattern governed by the combined
effect of the horizontal inflow and thermal buoyancy, in contrast to the more symmetric
distribution observed at 70°.
4.2.2.2
Wind speed of 8 m/s
Compared with the scenario at 0 m/s, increasing the wind speed to 8 m/s strengthens the
rotating airflow in the compartment and raises local velocities with increasing fire size, as
shown in figure 4.3 on the preceding page. At 5 MW, internal velocities are in the range
of 1.5 m/s - 3.0 m/s, with localised areas up to 4.5 m/s. A similar distribution is observed at
10 MW, but with more frequent regions approaching 4.5 m/s, and at 20 MW these regions
expand further.
The rotating motion also influences the inflow through Door 1 (leeward side). At 5 MW,
the velocity through this door does not increase when the wind rises from 0 m/s to 8 m/s
but remains largely unchanged. This might be explained by the negative pressure on the
leeward side counteracting the fire-induced overpressure. Through Door 2, the velocity
rises from 1.5 m/s - 4.5 m/s to at least 6.0 m/s, with accelerations up to 7.5 m/s caused by
the interaction between the horizontal inflow and thermal buoyancy, which also affects the
inflow through Door 1.
When the fire size increases to 10 MW and 20 MW, the additional heat intensifies buoy-
ancy, thereby raising the demand for replacement air and drawing in greater volumes
through the openings. The velocity through Door 2 increases to about 7.5 m/s and 9.0 m/s,
respectively. For Door 1, a pulsating inflow of 1.5 m/s - 4.5 m/s is observed at 10 MW,
which transitions to a steadier 4.5 m/s - 6.0 m/s inflow at 20 MW. This behaviour is docu-
mented in appendix D on page 147.
4.2.2.3
Wind speed of 14 m/s
At a wind speed of 14 m/s, figure 4.3 on the preceding page shows that the inflow through
Door 2 becomes distinctly pulsating, with velocities generally ranging from 6.0 m/s to
10.5 m/s. Door 1 still exhibits a pulsating inflow of 3.0 m/s - 6.0 m/s, with peak values
up to 7.5 m/s. Further evidence of this behaviour is provided in appendix D on page 147.
With increasing fire size, the inflow velocities rise further compared to the case at 8.0 m/s.
Through Door 1, velocities are in the range of 3.0 m/s - 7.5 m/s at 10 MW and 20 MW,
though the flow becomes more turbulent at 20 MW than at 10 MW.
Master in Fire Safety
51
==PAGE:78==
The inflow through Door 1 is strongly influenced by the circulating airflow inside the com-
partment, generated by the horizontal stream through Door 2 interacting with the fire-
induced buoyancy. As the fire grows, this interaction intensifies. At 10 MW and 20 MW,
Door 2 exhibits stronger pulsations, with velocities of 7.5 m/s - 10.5 m/s and 7.5 m/s - 13.5 m/s,
respectively. In both cases, short-lived peaks of up to 15.0 m/s are observed. This indi-
cates that the interaction between the horizontal inflow through Door 2 and the buoyancy
generated by the fire plays a decisive role in controlling the airflow. Additional documen-
tation of this pulsating behaviour is provided in appendix D on page 147.
The strength of this interaction is considered to be largely governed by the building geom-
etry, particularly the distance between the fire centre and Doors 1 and 2. If the geometry
were twice as large, it cannot be assumed with certainty that the same interaction be-
tween the horizontal inflow through Doors 1 and 2 and the fire-induced buoyancy would
have occurred.
4.2.3
Angle – 150°
For the configuration with a 150° angle between the two replacement air openings, the
doors are no longer positioned symmetrically relative to the building’s vertical centreline.
Door 1 retains its position on the leeward side, while Door 2 is moved to the side façade,
closer to the windward wall where the wind impacts, and aligned parallel to the wind di-
rection. The airflow through the replacement air openings is therefore primarily governed
by the pressure difference between the two openings in combination with the fire-induced
thermal buoyancy. In addition, roof suction may also influence the flow, as it gradually de-
creases towards the leeward side depending on the wind velocity. Further documentation
of this relationship is provided in appendix D on page 147.
4.2.3.1
Wind speed of 0 m/s
As shown in figure 4.4 on the next page, the flow field inside the compartment is generally
calm, with inflow velocities through the replacement air openings in the range of 1.5 m/s -
3.0 m/s for a fire size of 5 MW. When the fire size increases to 10 MW and 20 MW, the addi-
tional heat intensifies the thermal buoyancy, which increases the demand for replacement
air. This is reflected in the higher inflow velocities observed through the openings, rising
to 3.0 m/s - 4.5 m/s at 10 MW and up to 4.5 m/s - 6.0 m/s at 20 MW. Hence, at a wind speed
of 0 m/s, the airflow is driven solely by fire induced buoyancy, which draws replacement
air through the two openings located on the leeward side.
4.2.3.2
Wind speed of 8 m/s
figure 4.4 on the facing page shows that increasing the wind speed to 8 m/s strongly
affects the airflow at Door 2. At a fire size of 5 MW, an outward flow develops as the
wind strikes the windward façade, creating an acceleration that generates strong suction
around the position of Door 2. The inflow through Door 1 impinges on the wall to the
right of the opening, which contributes to a circular airflow developing in the centre of the
compartment. Velocities through Door 1 are in the range of 3.0 m/s - 6.0 m/s, indicating a
pulsating inflow.
When the fire size increases to 10 MW, the inflow through Door 1 remains in the same
range of 3.0 m/s - 6.0 m/s, but the circular flow pattern becomes more pronounced. The
inflow through Door 2 becomes more strongly pulsating, with velocities of 3.0 m/s - 9.0 m/s,
where 9.0 m/s represents short peak values. At 20 MW, the velocity through Door 1 is
still in the range of 3.0 m/s - 6.0 m/s, though peaks up to 7.5 m/s may occur. For Door 2,
velocities also remain within the same range. Thus, the relatively strong suction acting
around Door 2 proves difficult to overcome, even as the fire size increases.

4.2.3.3
Wind speed of 14 m/s
As shown in figure 4.4 on the previous page, the velocity through Door 2 becomes more
pulsating at 14 m/s, independent of fire size. Further documentation of this behaviour is
provided in appendix D on page 147. With increasing fire size, the velocity through Door 2
rises, ranging from 3.0 m/s - 9.0 m/s at 5 MW, and reaching 6.0 m/s - 10.5 m/s at 10 MW
and 20 MW. It should be noted that Door 2 is located close to a smoke exhaust opening
situated near the wind exposed façade. This results in a relatively strong roof suction at
that opening, which contributes positively to the airflow entering through Door 2.
Regardless of fire size, Door 1 exhibits fluctuating inflow velocities generally in the range of
4.5 m/s - 7.5 m/s. These fluctuations become more pronounced as the circular flow pattern
inside the compartment strengthens, a process driven by the increased velocity through
Door 2 in combination with the fire-induced thermal buoyancy. This interaction between
the two inflows reinforces the unsteady behaviour at Door 1, highlighting the sensitivity of
the overall flow field to both external wind effects and buoyancy driven dynamics.
4.2.4
Intermediate conclusion of Airflow
The analysis demonstrates that fire size, wind speed, and the angle between the replace-
ment air openings all significantly influence the inflow velocities and internal flow patterns.
In general, increasing fire size and wind speed lead to higher velocities through the open-
ings. When one opening is aligned parallel to the wind direction and positioned near the
windward façade, the interaction with external wind effects produces pronounced changes
in the flow field, especially under strong wind conditions. The angle between the openings
further governs the distribution of inflow and the development of internal circulation.
Larger angles are associated with stronger pulsations, whose intensity depends on both
wind speed and fire size. Overall, the results highlight that variations in external wind
conditions induce marked differences in airflow through the openings, independent of fire
size. These effects have direct implications for the volume flow of replacement air and
are strongly influenced by the exact façade location of Door 2.
4.3
Volume flow through openings
To investigate how the inflow influences the compartment dynamics, the volume flows
were measured over time through Door 1 and Door 2 (providing replacement air) as well
as through the six roof openings OP1 - OP6 (exhausting the hot smoke gases) for each
of the 27 CFD simulations. The positions of Door 1, Door 2, and the six smoke exhaust
openings are shown in figure 4.1 on page 45. As illustrated, Door 2 is arranged such that
the angle between Door 1 and Door 2 is either 70°, 112°, or 150°relative to the building’s
centreline, while the position of Door 1 remains fixed.
For fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, the volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2
is shown over time in figure 4.5 on the facing page, figure 4.6 on page 56, and figure 4.7
on page 56. Each figure includes three curves corresponding to angles of 70°, 112°, and
150° between the doors relative to the building’s centreline. The reference wind speed at
10 m height is 0 m/s, 8 m/s, and 14 m/s during the intervals 0 s – 500 s, 501 s – 800 s, and
801 s – 1,100 s, respectively. The figures are intended to highlight trends in flow variation
with fire size, wind speed, and Door 2 placement, rather than to provide direct numerical
values. Enlarged versions are available in appendix D on page 166.
During the interval 0 s - 500 s, when the building is in the absence of wind, the flow through
Door 1 and Door 2 gradually increases as a result of the fire’s heat release, eventually
reaching a stable inflow with minimal fluctuations.
54
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:81==
Under these conditions, no significant differences are observed when varying the place-
ment of Door 2 in the façade, regardless of fire size. The only variation arises from the
increased volume flow associated with larger fire sizes. However, the relative increase
in volume flow is greater when the fire size rises from 5 MW to 10 MW than when it in-
creases from 10 MW to 20 MW. This indicates that, with growing fire size, a lower smoke
layer height may develop due to the limited capacity of buoyancy driven smoke ventila-
tion. Furthermore, this effect may also contribute to higher smoke layer temperatures, as
reflected in subsection 4.4.2.3 on page 88.
When the building is exposed to wind, pronounced fluctuations in the volume flow occur
through both Door 1 and Door 2, independent of the placement of Door 2 in the façade.
The curves also show that the fluctuation ranges vary with the position of Door 2, and
are further influenced by fire size and wind speed. Notably, at a fire size of 5 MW and
a Door 2 placement corresponding to a 150° angle relative to the building’s centreline,
relatively high negative flow values are observed at a wind speed of 14 m/s. A similar
effect is seen at 10 MW, but it diminishes at 20 MW. This behaviour arises because at a
150° angle, Door 2 is located in a region of relatively strong wind-induced suction. Air is
therefore drawn out through Door 2, as the fire-induced buoyancy cannot overcome the
suction. This effect is not observed to the same extent for Door 1, nor when the angle
between Door 1 and Door 2 is 70° or 112°, as seen in figure 4.5, figure 4.6 on the following
page, and figure 4.7 on the next page.

Based on figure 4.5 on the preceding page, figure 4.6, and figure 4.7, it can be observed
that the wind speed, in combination with the placement of Door 2 in the façade, influences
the volume flow through both Door 1 and Door 2. In addition, the fire size also affects the
amount of air drawn into the replacement air openings. However, from the figures it cannot
be directly demonstrated which of the parameters (the angle between the two doors, wind
speed, or fire size) has the dominant influence on the replacement air volume flow.
On this basis, a mean value of the flow is determined over a 50 s period, which is taken
to represent the output of each simulation for subsequent analysis. A 50 s interval was
chosen to reduce the effect of short-term fluctuations and numerical noise, while still being
sufficiently long to capture the characteristic behaviour of the flow by including one or more
dominant frequencies. This approach is therefore considered to provide a representative
measure of the volume flow.

figure 4.1 on page 45.

4.3.1
Ranking of Mean Volume Flow across Simulated Scenarios
In the following section, an initial analysis is conducted to examine how different physical
parameters influence the volume flow through the building’s replacement air openings.
The analysis is based on the results of the individual simulations, presented in the form
of bar charts. Each bar represents a unique scenario defined by a specific combination
of fire size, wind velocity, and the angle between the door openings.
The visualisation enables a comparison of the computed mean values of volume flow,
thereby providing insight into which combinations lead to increased or reduced inflow.
While the representation does not isolate the effect of individual parameters, it conveys
an overall picture of the variation in volume flow across scenarios. By ranking the mean
values from the lowest to the highest, the dominant trends across the 27 simulation com-
binations become evident.
Master in Fire Safety
57
==PAGE:84==
4.3.1.1
Opening Door 1
In figure 4.8, the ranked mean values of the volume flow through Door 1 are shown as
a bar chart, where each bar represents one simulation scenario as indicated below the
axis.
Figure 4.8: Mean volume flow through opening D1, serving as a replacement air opening,
for all 27 combinations of fire size, wind speed, and door angle. Bars are arranged in
ascending order to highlight the variation in flow across scenarios.
As shown in figure 4.8, the flow through Door 1 generally increases with growing fire size,
which is expected due to the intensified thermal buoyancy. The trend is, however, not
entirely consistent, as some simulations with a fire size of 5 MW produce higher flows
than certain simulations at 10 MW and 20 MW.
For example, in the case with a fire size of 5 MW, wind speed of 14 m/s, and a 70° angle
between Door 1 and Door 2, the mean flow reaches 23.7 m3/s, which exceeds many of
the simulations with fire sizes of 10 MW and 20 MW. Similarly, at a fire size of 10 MW, wind
speed of 14 m/s, and a 112° angle, the mean flow is 16.53 m3/s, while at 20 MW with the
same wind speed and angle the mean flow decreases to 15.79 m3/s. This indicates that
the increased buoyancy from the fire does not alone govern the flow, but that wind speed
and the angle between the two doors also play decisive roles.
It is also not evident from figure 4.8 that increasing wind speed necessarily enhances the
flow through Door 1. For a 20 MW fire and a 70° angle between Door 1 and Door 2, the
flow rises from 23.0 m3/s to 25.8 m3/s as the wind speed increases from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, but
then decreases again to 23.1 m3/s when the wind speed rises further to 14 m/s. Likewise,
at a fire size of 5 MW and an angle of 112°, the flow is 14.8 m3/s at 0 m/s, drops to 11.8 m3/s
at 8 m/s, and then increases again to 16.5 m3/s at 14 m/s. A similar tendency is observed
for a 10 MW fire at a 150° angle: the flow is 19.2 m3/s at 0 m/s but rises markedly to
23.2 m3/s when the wind speed increases to either 8 m/s or 14 m/s.
58
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:85==
It can further be observed from figure 4.8 on the facing page that the angle between
Door 1 and Door 2 also influences the magnitude of the flow through Door 1. This effect is
particularly evident at an angle of 112°, where the lowest volume flow values occur most
frequently. In contrast, angles of 70° and 150° generally yield the highest flow values,
depending on the fire size and wind speed. For instance, at a fire size of 20 MW and a
wind speed of 8 m/s, the flows are 25.8 m3/s, 17.4 m3/s, and 23.4 m3/s at door angles of
70°, 112°, and 150°, respectively.
4.3.1.2
Opening Door 2
The ranked mean values of the volume flow through Door 2 are shown as a bar chart
in figure 4.9, where each bar represents one simulation scenario as indicated below the
axis.
Figure 4.9: Mean volume flow through door 2, serving as a replacement air opening, for all
27 combinations of fire size, wind speed, and door angle. Bars are arranged in ascending
order to highlight the variation in flow across scenarios.
As shown in figure 4.9, the flow through Door 2 generally increases with growing fire
size, reflecting the stronger thermal buoyancy generated by larger fires. This trend is
clear and largely independent of wind speed and the angle between the two doors. In
particular, a fire size of 5 MW consistently produces the lowest flow values, regardless of
wind speed or door angle. The volume flow values for 5 MW are positioned at the lower
end of the distribution in the bar chart, suggesting that the buoyancy force from a 5 MW
fire alone is insufficient to sustain an effective inflow through Door 2. There are, however,
two scenarios that deviate from this general trend: at an angle of 112° and wind speeds
of 8 m/s and 14 m/s, where the resulting flows are significantly higher than in the other
5 MW cases. Nevertheless, these remain distinct exceptions and do not alter the overall
pattern.
It is also not evident from the figure that increasing wind speed necessarily enhances the
flow through Door 2. For example, at a fire size of 5 MW and a 150° angle, the volume
Master in Fire Safety
59
==PAGE:86==
flow decreases from 15.5 m3/s at 0 m/s to 6.4 m3/s at 8 m/s and further to 3.2 m3/s at
14 m/s. This indicates that, at low fire sizes, higher wind speeds intensify the suction
around the opening, thereby reducing the inflow. In contrast, at a fire size of 20 MW and
a 112° angle, the flow increases linearly from 22.1 m3/s at 0 m/s to 27.1 m3/s at 8 m/s and
31.5 m3/s at 14 m/s. A different tendency is observed at 10 MW and a 70° angle, where
the flow decreases from 19.4 m3/s at 0 m/s to 18.7 m3/s at 8 m/s, before rising again to
21.6 m3/s at 14 m/s, indicating a non linear relationship.
The angle between the two doors also exerts a significant influence on the flow through
Door 2. As shown in figure 4.9 on the previous page, a 150° angle generally results in
lower volume flows compared to 70° and 112°. For instance, at a fire size of 10 MW and
a wind speed of 14 m/s, the flows are 21.6 m3/s, 26.3 m3/s, and 2.0 m3/s at angles of 70°,
112°, and 150°, respectively, demonstrating a substantial reduction at 150°. Similarly, at
a fire size of 20 MW and a wind speed of 8 m/s, the flows are 23.4 m3/s, 27.1 m3/s, and
19.2 m3/s at the same angles, again highlighting the relative disadvantage of the 150°
configuration.
4.3.1.3
Intermediate conclusion of Mean Volume Flow Ranking
For both openings, it is found that increasing fire size generally leads to higher volume
flows. For Door 1, however, this tendency is not entirely consistent, as some simulations
with smaller fires produce higher flows than certain scenarios with larger fires. This sug-
gests that the influence of fire size must be understood in combination with the other two
parameters: wind speed and the angle between the replacement-air doors.
In some cases, the wind enhances the volume flow, while in others it reduces it. This
indicates that the wind effect depends not only on the wind speed itself but also on the
relative positioning of the doors, particularly their mutual angle.
The angle between the two doors is shown to have a marked effect on the flow. For
Door 1, lower values are typically observed at an angle of 112°, while angles of 70° and
150° more often yield higher flows. For Door 2, by contrast, an angle of 150° usually
results in the lowest flows, whereas 112° is more frequently associated with the highest
flows.
4.3.2
Analysis of Volume Flow by Parametric Grouping
In subsection 4.3.1 on page 57, the overall trends were identified based on all 27 simu-
lation scenarios, where central patterns were highlighted through a visual ranking of the
mean values.
In the following, a more systematic analysis is conducted by grouping the data according to
one independent parameter at a time: the angle between the two doors, the fire size, and
the wind speed. The purpose is to examine how each parameter, in isolation, influences
the volume flow through the replacement air doors. The results are presented as graphs in
which the mean values of volume flow are plotted as a function of the selected parameter,
while the other variables are held constant. For detailed numerical values, including mean,
standard deviation, range, minimum, and maximum of the volume flows through Door 1,
Door 2, and OP1 - OP6, reference is made to appendix D on page 180.
Each of subsection 4.3.2.1 on the facing page, subsection 4.3.2.2 on page 64, and sub-
section 4.3.2.3 on page 68 begins with a table summarising the mean values of volume
flow for the parameter under consideration. The analysis that follows focuses on the
graphical representations, with the tables providing the supporting reference data.

Door 1
In figure 4.10, the mean volume flow through Door 1 is shown as a function of the door
angle between Door 1 and Door 2 for varying fire sizes (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW) and wind
speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s).
Figure 4.10: Mean volume flow rate through Door 1, grouped by the angle between the
doors, for varying fire sizes and wind speeds.
Master in Fire Safety
61
==PAGE:88==
Across the scenarios, the volume flow through Door 1 generally decreases at an angle of
112° between the two replacement air openings, when fire size and wind speed are held
constant. At angles of 70° and 150°, the volume flow instead shows variation without a
clear trend. In some cases, the flow is highest at 150°, for example with a 10 MW fire
and a wind speed of 14 m/s, where the flow reaches approximately 23.2 m3/s compared
to 19.7 m3/s at 70°. In other scenarios, the opposite occurs, such as with a 20 MW fire
and a wind speed of 8 m/s, where the flow decreases from 25.8 m3/s at 70° to 23.4 m3/s
at 150°. Taken together with the overall trend in figure 4.10 on the previous page, these
examples show that the differences between 70° and 150° are moderate and variable,
whereas the reduction at 112° appears more consistent.
It can also be observed from figure 4.10 on the preceding page that in the absence of wind,
the volume flows through Door 1 are nearly identical across all three angles, though they
increase with growing fire size. This confirms the previously observed trend described in
section 4.2 on page 47: as the fire size increases, more heat is produced, which intensifies
the thermal buoyancy. This creates a greater demand for replacement air, thereby drawing
larger volumes through the openings to maintain mass balance.
Door 2
In figure 4.11, the mean volume flow through Door 2 is shown as a function of the door
angle between Door 1 and Door 2 for varying fire sizes (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW) and wind
speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s).
Figure 4.11: Mean volume flow rate through Door 2, grouped by door angle, for varying
fire sizes and wind speeds.
Across the scenarios, the volume flow through Door 2 generally reaches its highest values
at an angle of 112° between the two replacement air openings, when fire size and wind
speed are held constant, while a 150° angle results in a significant reduction.
62
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:89==
For example, at a fire size of 20 MW and a wind speed of 14 m/s, the volume flow is
31.5 m3/s at 112° but drops to 14.3 m3/s at 150°. Likewise, with a 10 MW fire and a wind
speed of 8 m/s, the flow through Door 2 is 21.8 m3/s at 112° compared to only 12.2 m3/s
at 150°.
The increased volume flow through Door 2 at 112° can be explained by the interaction be-
tween the horizontal inflow through the door and the fire-induced buoyancy, as described
in section 4.2 on page 47. This effect means that, as wind speed and fire size increase,
the volume flow through Door 2 rises, while it decreases through Door 1. The reduction
in flow through Door 2 at 150° is likely due to its proximity to the wind exposed façade,
where the wind causes stronger accelerations around the corner. This creates stronger
suction near the door’s position at 150°, as partly noted in section 4.2 on page 47.
In the absence of wind, the volume flows through Door 2 are nearly identical across all
three angles, though they increase with fire size to maintain mass balance.
It can further be observed from figure 4.11 on the preceding page that at a fire size of
5 MW, wind speed of 14 m/s, and a 112° angle, the mean flow through Door 2 is 11.4 m3/s,
compared to 23.8 m3/s through Door 1. A more balanced distribution of flows between
Door 1 and Door 2 would have been expected, since both are located on the leeward
side. The fact that Door 2 records lower values during the averaging period suggests that
the dynamic flow field and local pressure variations can lead to asymmetric distributions
of volume flow, even when the doors are placed symmetrically.
Sum of mean volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2
In figure 4.12, the total volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2 is shown as a function of
the door angle between the two replacement air openings.
Figure 4.12: Sum of mean volume flow rate through Door 1 and Door 2 grouped by door
angle, for varying fire sizes and wind speeds.
Master in Fire Safety
63
==PAGE:90==
Across the scenarios, figure 4.10 on page 61 and figure 4.11 on page 62 show that, at a
wind speed of 0 m/s, the curves are nearly horizontal, indicating that the angle between
the replacement air doors has little influence on the total volume flow when there is no
wind effect. Under these conditions, the airflow is driven solely by fire-induced buoyancy
to maintain mass balance.
At a wind speed of 8 m/s, a general tendency is observed in which the total volume flow is
highest at a 70° angle between the doors, followed by a decline as the angle increases. In
this case, a larger angle between the doors has a negative impact on the flow, as reflected
by the descending curves.
At 14 m/s, a clear trend emerges: the total flow increases slightly when the angle changes
from 70° to 112°, peaking at 112°, before dropping sharply at 150°. Thus, increasing the
angle from 70° to 112° has a positive effect, while increasing it further to 150° reduces
the flow. It should be noted that, for the scenario with a fire size of 5 MW, a wind speed
of 14 m/s, and a 70° angle, there is some uncertainty in the mean value for Door 2, as
mentioned earlier, but this does not alter the overall trend.
Overall, the analysis shows that the influence of the angle between the replacement air
openings is strongly conditioned by wind speed and, to some extent, by fire size. The
door angle cannot be evaluated in isolation but must be understood in conjunction with
these parameters, as the interaction between thermal buoyancy and wind effects largely
determines the resulting volume flows.

Door 1
Figure 4.13 on the facing page shows the mean volume flow through Door 2 as a function
of fire size at varying wind speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s) and door angles (70°, 112°, 150°).
64
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:91==
Figure 4.13: Mean volume flow rate through Door 1, grouped by fire size, for varying wind
speed and angle between replacement openings.
At a wind speed of 0 m/s, the figure shows that the curves for the volume flow through
Door 1 follow a slightly increasing trend depending on the angle between the replacement
air openings. At angles of 70° and 150°, the curves are nearly identical, whereas an
angle of 112° results in relatively lower volume flows as the fire size increases. For fire
sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, the volume flow through Door 1 varies by 0.7 m3/s,
1.2 m3/s, and 1.8 m3/s, respectively, depending on the angle, as shown in table 4.3 on
the preceding page. This indicates that the angle has only a limited influence on the flow
when the building is unaffected by wind, since the airflow is driven solely by fire induced
buoyancy to maintain mass balance.
When the wind speed increases from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, the influence of the angle on the
volume flow becomes more pronounced. The flow through Door 1 generally rises with
fire size, with variations of 8.4 m3/s, 7.7 m3/s, and 8.4 m3/s for 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW,
respectively, depending on the angle between the openings, see table 4.3 on the facing
page. The highest flows occur at angles of 70° or 150°, and the lowest at 112°. For
example, at a fire size of 10 MW, the flow is 15.5 m3/s at 112°, compared with 22.9 m3/s
at 70° and 23.2 m3/s at 150°.
At a wind speed of 14 m/s and an angle of 112°, the volume flow through Door 1 remains
nearly constant regardless of fire size, with only a slight decrease from 16.5 m3/s at 5 MW
to 15.8 m3/s at 20 MW. As noted in section 4.2 on page 47, the velocity through Door 1
under these conditions lies in the range 1.5 m/s - 4.5 m/s, which is reflected in the compa-
rable volume flows.
At an angle of 70°, a fire size of 5 MW and a wind speed of 14 m/s produces a relatively
high flow through Door 1. This is due to the averaging period capturing relatively high
values for Door 1 and low values for Door 2.
Master in Fire Safety
65
==PAGE:92==
This suggests that the dynamic flow field and local pressure variations can lead to an
asymmetric distribution of volume flow between the replacement air openings, even though
they are placed symmetrically. When the wind speed increases from 8 m/s to 14 m/s, the
tendency is mixed: at 5 MW the flow increases from 14.9 m3/s to 23.7 m3/s, while at 10 MW
it decreases from 22.9 m3/s to 19.7 m3/s, and at 20 MW it decreases from 25.8 m3/s to
23.1 m3/s.
Door 2
The mean volume flow through Door 2 is shown as a function of fire size at varying wind
speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s) and angles between openings D1 and D2 (70°, 112°, 150°)
in figure 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Mean volume flow rate through Door 2, grouped by fire size, for varying wind
speed and angle between replacement openings.
As shown in figure 4.14, the volume flow through Door 2 increases with fire size, similar
to what is observed for Door 1. In general, the flow rises from about 16 m3/s at 5 MW to
around 23 m3/s at 20 MW, depending on the angle. The differences between the angles
are 0.2 m3/s, 0.6 m3/s, and 0.9 m3/s for fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, respec-
tively, see table 4.3 on page 64. This indicates, as with Door 1, that the influence of
the angle is minimal in the absence of wind, and that the flow is primarily governed by
fire-induced buoyancy.
When the wind speed increases from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, the volume flow at 70° remains
comparable to the no wind case, with differences of only 0.3 m3/s, 0.8 m3/s, and 0.4 m3/s
for fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, respectively, see table 4.3 on page 64. The
highest flow occurs at 112°, followed by 70°, while 150° yields the lowest values. For
example, at a fire size of 10 MW, the flows are 21.2 m3/s, 18.7 m3/s, and 12.2 m3/s for
112°, 70°, and 150°, respectively. The differences become even more pronounced at
20 MW.
66
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:93==
Moreover, with wind, the flow through Door 2 at 150° decreases compared to the no wind
case. This suggests that at 112°, favourable pressure and flow conditions arise, while at
150° the flow is inhibited.
At a wind speed of 14 m/s, the pattern observed at 8 m/s is reinforced, with 112° producing
the highest flow, 150° the lowest, and 70° lying between these. This is illustrated at a fire
size of 10 MW, where the volume flows are 23.6 m3/s, 21.6 m3/s, and 2.0 m3/s at 112°, 70°,
and 150°, respectively. The data point for 5 MW at 70° has been omitted for the reasons
explained in subsection 4.3.2.1 on page 61, and the curve for 70° at 14 m/s therefore
largely follows the trend observed at 0 m/s, with increasing flow at higher fire sizes.
Sum of mean volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2
In figure 4.15, the total volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2 is shown as a function
of fire size. Together with table 4.3 on page 64, it can be seen that at a wind speed
of 0 m/s the total flow follows an increasing trend with fire size for all three door angles.
At fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, the combined volume flow through the two
doors ranges from 30.5 m3/s - 31.0 m3/s, 36.9 m3/s - 38.6 m3/s, and 43.4 m3/s - 46.0 m3/s,
respectively, depending on whether the angle is 70°, 112°, or 150°. This shows that, for
each fire size, the total flow is nearly comparable across the different angles when wind
effects are absent.
Figure 4.15: Sum of mean volume flow rate through Door 1 and Door 2 grouped by fire
size, for varying wind speeds and angle between replacement openings.
When wind speed increases from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, the influence of the door angle becomes
more apparent. At 70°, the highest total volume flow is achieved, followed by 112°, with the
lowest values occurring at 150°. For instance, at a fire size of 10 MW, the combined flow
is 41.6 m3/s, 37.4 m3/s, and 35.4 m3/s for 70°, 112°, and 150°, respectively, corresponding
to a difference of 6.2 m3/s.
Master in Fire Safety
67
==PAGE:94==
At fire sizes of 5 MW and 20 MW, the differences are 5.3 m3/s and 6.5 m3/s, indicating that
the variation depends more on wind speed in combination with door angle than on fire
size alone.
At 14 m/s, the pattern observed at 8 m/s is reinforced: the highest total flows occur at
112° or 70°, which are nearly identical, in the ranges 35.1 m3/s - 47.0 m3/s and 39.8 m3/s -
47.3 m3/s, respectively, as fire size increases. As at 8 m/s, 150° consistently produces the
lowest values, in the range 24.2 m3/s - 39.7 m3/s. However, a marked increase is observed
at 150°, where the total flow rises from 25.1 m3/s to 39.7 m3/s as fire size increases from
10 MW to 20 MW. This suggests that the buoyancy from a sufficiently large fire can offset
the wind induced suction near Door 2, thereby enhancing inflow.
It follows that when considering the total volume flow through both doors, the variations
between the angles appear less pronounced than in the separate analyses in subsec-
tion 4.3.2.1 on page 61. This may indicate that a reduction in flow through one door at a
given angle is compensated by a corresponding increase through the other.
It should also be noted that at 112° the horizontal inflow through Door 2 interacts with
the fire-induced buoyancy, counteracting the inflow through Door 1, as described in sec-
tion 4.2 on page 47. This interaction partly explains the observed tendencies for the total
volume flow through the two doors.
Overall, the results indicate that the interaction between wind speed and fire size largely
determines how the angle between the replacement air openings affects the volume flow.
The effect of angle is most pronounced when high wind speeds coincide with large fire
sizes, while at low wind speeds and small fire sizes, the effect is marginal.

Door 1
In figure 4.16 on the facing page, the mean volume flow through Door 1 is shown as a
function of wind speed for varying fire sizes (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW) and the angle between
the two replacement air openings (70°, 112°, 150°).

Figure 4.16: Mean volume flow rate through Door 1, grouped by wind speed, for varying
fire size and angle between replacement openings.
At a fire size of 5 MW, the general trend is that the highest volume flows through Door 1
occur at a wind speed of 14 m/s. This can be seen at 112°, where the flow decreases
from 14.8 m3/s at 0 m/s to 11.8 m3/s at 8 m/s, before rising again to 16.5 m3/s at 14 m/s.
The results indicate that wind direction, in combination with the placement of Door 2 in the
façade, may counteract the inflow at moderate wind speeds while amplifying it at higher
wind speeds. At 150°, a steady increasing tendency is observed regardless of wind speed.
At 70°, the change is limited between 0 m/s and 8 m/s, but a marked increase to 23.7 m3/s
occurs at 14 m/s. As explained in subsection 4.3.2.1 on page 61, this pronounced jump
is associated with specific flow dynamics, and the scenario with 5 MW, 14 m/s, and 70° is
therefore excluded from the overall assessment.
For a fire size of 10 MW, it is not consistent that the highest flows always occur at the
highest wind speeds, as this depends on the angle. At 70°, the flow rises from 19.1 m3/s
at 0 m/s to 22.9 m3/s at 8 m/s, after which it decreases to 19.7 m3/s at 14 m/s. At 112°,
the maximum flow is seen at 0 m/s with 18.0 m3/s, which decreases to 15.5 m3/s at 8 m/s
before increasing again to 16.5 m3/s at 14 m/s. At 150°, a general increase is observed
from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, after which the curve flattens out as wind speed increases further.
For a fire size of 20 MW, the same tendencies are observed as for 10 MW, except that the
overall level of the flows is higher due to the increased fire size.
At 112°, the results typically show a reduction in flow at a moderate wind speed of 8 m/s,
with a slight increase at 14 m/s for fire sizes of 5 MW and 10 MW. At 20 MW, however,
the flow continues to decrease as wind speed increases. In contrast, 150° consistently
produces the highest flows under stronger wind influence. An angle of 70° can also yield
relatively high values, but the flow is more sensitive to wind speed variations, and this
sensitivity becomes more pronounced as fire size increases.
