==PAGE:96==
Door 2
In figure 4.17, the mean volume flow through Door 2 is shown as a function of wind speed
for varying fire sizes (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW) and the angle between the two replacement
air doors (70°, 112°, 150°).
Figure 4.17: Mean volume flow rate through Door 2, grouped by wind speed, for varying
fire size and angle between replacement openings.
At 112°, a clear increase in the flow through Door 2 is observed with rising wind speed,
regardless of fire size. The flow curves are vertically offset due to the fire size, indicating
that larger fires result in higher absolute flows at all wind speeds. This behaviour can
be explained by the mechanism described in section 4.2 on page 47, where the interac-
tion between the horizontal wind forcing through Door 2 and the buoyancy driven plume
enhances the inflow.
In contrast, at 150°, the flow consistently decreases across the entire wind speed range,
independent of fire size. This suggests that the configuration systematically suppresses
inflow through Door 2. The behaviour is attributed to the intensified turbulence near the
corner of the wind exposed façade, which creates stronger suction around the opening,
as partly discussed in section 4.2 on page 47.
At 70°, the trend depends more strongly on fire size. For a 5 MW fire, the flow decreases
from 16.0 m3/s to 11.4 m3/s as wind speed increases from 8 m/s to 14 m/s, which can be
linked to the averaging period used for calculating the mean, as described in subsec-
tion 4.3.2.1 on page 61. For 10 MW, the flow instead rises from 18.7 m3/s to 21.6 m3/s in
the same interval, while showing only minor changes between 0 m/s and 8 m/s. At 20 MW,
the curve is nearly flat, with a total variation of just 0.9 m3/s across the wind speed range.
Overall, the results demonstrate that 112° consistently leads to increasing inflow with rising
wind speed, while 150° consistently results in decreasing inflow.
70
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:97==
At 70°, the effect of wind is more complex, as the flow becomes more sensitive to varia-
tions in wind speed and fire size. Consequently, no unambiguous trend can be identified
at 70°, in contrast to the clearer patterns observed at 112° and 150°.
Sum of mean volume flow through openings D1 and D2
In figure 4.18, the combined volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2 is shown as a function
of wind speed to evaluate the influence of wind on the total inflow through the doors. For
a 5 MW fire, the highest flow is generally observed at an angle of 112°, where the flow
increases from 30.5 m3/s at 0 m/s to 39.8 m3/s at 14 m/s. A similar trend is found at 70°,
where the flow rises from 30.9 m3/s to 35.1 m3/s over the same wind speed range. At both
70° and 112°, the increase is most pronounced between 8 m/s and 14 m/s. In contrast, at
150° the combined flow decreases steadily with increasing wind speed.
Figure 4.18: Sum of mean volume flow rate through Door 1 and Door 2 as function of
wind speed, for varying fire size and angle between replacement openings.
For a 10 MW fire at 70°, the flow increases slightly from 38.6 m3/s at 0 m/s to 41.6 m3/s
at 8 m/s, after which it stabilises around 41.2 m3/s at 14 m/s. At 112°, the flow rises from
36.9 m3/s at 0 m/s to 42.8 m3/s at 14 m/s, with the strongest increase occurring between
8 m/s and 14 m/s. At 150°, the flow decreases with wind speed, dropping from 38.1 m3/s
at 0 m/s to 25.1 m3/s at 14 m/s, with a marked change in slope between 8 m/s and 14 m/s.
At a fire size of 20 MW, the highest absolute values of total flow are observed across all
angles, reflecting the increased replacement-air demand at larger fire sizes to maintain
mass balance. At 70°, the flow rises slightly from 46.0 m3/s to 49.2 m3/s when wind speed
increases from 0 m/s to 8 m/s, but then decreases to 47.0 m3/s at 14 m/s. At 150°, the
flow consistently decreases from 45.0 m3/s to 39.7 m3/s with rising wind speed. However,
compared to the 5 MW and 10 MW cases, the slope of the curve is less steep at 20 MW,
indicating that the higher fire-induced buoyancy becomes the dominant driver of the flow.
Master in Fire Safety
71
==PAGE:98==
Overall, the results show that 112° generally yields the highest combined flows as wind
speed increases, while 150° consistently produces declining flows. At 70°, the flow is typ-
ically stable or slightly increasing between 0 m/s and 8 m/s, followed by a slight reduction
between 8 m/s and 14 m/s. The pronounced increase at 5 MW and 14 m/s is attributed to
the averaging interval, as discussed in subsection 4.3.2.1 on page 61.
4.3.3
Coefficient of Variation for Mean Volume Flow
To assess the stability of the measured airflow rates in the CFD simulations, the coefficient
of variation, CoV , is applied. The formula is given by (Ross, 2014):
CoV = σ
µ
(4.1)
where:
σ
The standard deviation
[m3/s]
µ
The mean value
[m3/s]
A CoV value below 0.1 is considered to indicate very precise measurements, while val-
ues in the range 0.1 - 0.3 are regarded as acceptable. If CoV exceeds 0.3, this should be
interpreted as high variability and potential uncertainty in the data. Accordingly, results
with CoV > 0.3 must be assessed with some conservatism according to Devore L. Jay
(2011). Reference is made to appendix D on page 180, which provides the detailed nu-
merical values of mean, standard deviation, range, minimum, and maximum volume flow
for each simulation case, including the calculated CoV values.
In figure 4.19, the coefficient of variation, CoV , for the volume flow through Door 1 and
Door 2 is presented for all combinations.
Figure 4.19: Coefficient of Variation (CoV ) for volume flow through Door 1 and Door 2 for
all combinations of fire size, wind speed, and door angle.
72
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:99==
As shown in figure 4.19 on the facing page, scenarios without wind influence yield CoV
values below 0.05 across all cases, confirming the trends described in section 4.2 and sub-
sections 4.3.2.1 to 4.3.2.3 on page 47, on page 61, on page 64 and on page 68, where
stable, buoyancy driven flows were observed in the absence of wind.
At moderate wind speeds (8 m/s), a more mixed stability pattern is evident. For example,
at an angle of 150°, CoV exceeds 1.0 for Door 2 at a fire size of 5 MW, but decreases
to about 0.50 and 0.37 for 10 MW and 20 MW, respectively. This indicates that, for small
fires combined with moderate wind speeds, the flow through Door 2 becomes unstable.
This finding is consistent with the flow field patterns discussed in section 4.2 on page 47
and the observed volume flows in subsections 4.3.2.1 to 4.3.2.3 on page 61, on page 64
and on page 68. In contrast, Door 1 shows CoV values in the range 0.12 - 0.16, reflecting
more stable flow conditions.
At high wind speed (14 m/s), this tendency is amplified for Door 2, where CoV reaches
4.70, 6.20, and 0.80 for fire sizes of 5 MW, 10 MW, and 20 MW, respectively, at a 150°
angle. For Door 1, CoV values of 0.40 are obtained at 5 MW and 10 MW, compared with
0.25 at 20 MW.
Another example is the scenario with a 5 MW fire, wind speed of 14 m/s, and an angle
of 70°, where CoV is 0.37 for Door 2 versus 0.20 for Door 1. This indicates unstable
flow through Door 2, which may explain why no balanced distribution of volume flow is
observed between Door 1 and Door 2, despite their symmetrical placement on the leeward
side of the building, as noted in subsections 4.3.2.1 to 4.3.2.3 on page 61, on page 64
and on page 68.
It follows from the above that the highest CoV values occur in scenarios where wind
speed and door angle are the dominant factors. This may explain the pronounced reduc-
tions and fluctuations in the mean volume flow observed in these cases, as discussed in
subsections 4.3.2.1 to 4.3.2.3 on page 61, on page 64 and on page 68.
4.3.4
Intermediate conclusion of Volume Flow Through Openings
The analysis demonstrates that the angle between the two replacement air openings can
have a significant influence on the total volume flow; however, this effect is strongly depen-
dent on both wind speed and fire size. At high wind speed (14 m/s), the most pronounced
differences between the angles are observed, with 112° consistently yielding the highest
flow and 150° the lowest. For moderate wind speed (8 m/s), the pattern is more complex,
and no single angle can be identified as universally optimal. In the absence of wind, the
angle generally has only a minor impact on the volume flow, as buoyancy forces driven
by the fire dominate the airflow.
Wind speed itself also plays a crucial role in shaping the volume flow, as it may either
enhance or suppress the influence of the angle between the openings. Without wind, the
differences between angles remain limited, whereas high wind speeds amplify the varia-
tions, particularly in combination with larger fire sizes. At moderate wind speed (8 m/s), a
more intricate flow pattern emerges in which the effect of the angle may either improve or
reduce the overall flow, depending on the fire size.
The analysis further reveals that fire size generally increases the volume flow due to in-
tensified thermal buoyancy, which increases the demand for replacement air and thereby
draws larger amounts of air through the openings to maintain mass balance. This effect
is most pronounced under windless conditions, whereas its significance diminishes with
increasing wind speed, at which point the interaction between wind and angle becomes
more decisive.
Master in Fire Safety
73
==PAGE:100==
These relationships are summarised in table 4.5, which identifies the dominant parameter
controlling the volume flow for each angle and wind speed. Here, fire size is denoted as
F, wind speed as W, and a combined effect of both as C. Under windless conditions, the
flow is unambiguously governed by buoyancy from the fire. For angles of 70° and 112°,
a combined pattern (C) emerges at wind speeds of 8 m/s and 14 m/s, where both fire
size and wind speed exert a substantial influence. In contrast, at 150°, wind speed (W)
systematically dominates under these conditions.

Based on the table, the main tendencies can be summarised as follows:
• At 0 m/s, the flow is consistently governed by fire size (F), regardless of the angle.
• At 8 m/s, a combination effect (C) is seen at 70° and 112°, while wind speed (W)
dominates at 150°.
• At 14 m/s, the same pattern is repeated: a combination effect (C) at 70° and 112°,
and wind speed (W) dominates at 150°.
Based on the analysis, none of the parameters (fire size, wind speed, or door angle) dom-
inates across all conditions. At low wind speeds, fire size is the primary driver, whereas
at high wind speeds (14 m/s), the outcome is governed by the interaction between wind
speed and door angle. For moderate wind speeds (8 m/s), all three jointly influence the
flow.
4.4
Air temperature
In the present section, the air temperature conditions for the 27 simulations are examined,
in order to analyse the influence of the placement of the openings, wind conditions, and
fire size on the efficiency of the smoke ventilation in the room.
Previously, all assumptions regarding the modelling were described, and the models were
validated through analyses of the fire size and the wind in the model. The present analysis
aims to identify trends, uncertainties, and any systematic deviations relative to a reference
state.
The data consist of 27 simulations, each with 9 air temperature measurements. Each
simulation is associated with three input parameters: fire size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW),
wind velocity (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s), and door angle (70°, 112°, 150°). In the following, an
excerpt from the simulations is presented for the different door angles.
74

to 4.22 on this page, on page 77 and on page 78, at door angles of 70°, 112° and 150°,
respectively, between the replacement air openings. In each figure, the rows indicate
increasing wind velocity, while the columns represent increasing fire size.

In this scenario, door 1 and door 2 are located on the leeward side of the building and are

4.4.1.2
Angle 112°
In this scenario, door 1 is located on the leeward side, and door 2 is located on the side
façade, parallel to the wind direction. On the rear façade of the building there will be a
negative pressure (suction) driven by the wind flowing over and along the sides of the
building, whereas along the side façades there will be turbulence generated by the wind’s
flow around the building corner, which can likewise induce suction.
In figure 4.21 on the facing page, it is generally observed that comparable air temperature
levels are achieved for the three fire sizes, irrespective of the wind velocity. However, at a
wind velocity of 14 m/s, lower air temperature levels are obtained than at 0 m/s and 8 m/s.
The air temperature range for a 5 MW fire size is 60◦C - 120◦C, with the 60◦C - 90◦C band
increasing when the wind velocity rises from 8 m/s to 14 m/s. For 10 MW and 20 MW, the
ranges are 120◦C - 180◦C and 180◦C - 300◦C, respectively. This relationship indicates
that the horizontal airflow through door 2, in combination with the fire’s thermal buoyancy,
changes with increasing air velocities, resulting in increased turbulence, which in turn
indicates greater mixing of the air.
The same air temperature ranges are observed when changing the angle from 70° to
112°, where the bands 90◦C - 120◦C and 210◦C - 240◦C increase at fire sizes of 10 MW
and 20 MW. This is due to greater mixing arising from changing the door angle from 70°
to 112°, in combination with increasing fire size, as previously discussed in section 4.2 on
page 47.
4.4.1.3
Angle 150°
In this scenario, door 1 is located on the leeward side, and door 2 is located on the side
façade, parallel to the wind direction. On the rear façade of the building there will be
negative pressure (suction) driven by the wind flowing over and along the sides of the
building, whereas along the side façades there will be turbulence generated by the wind
flowing around the building corner, which can likewise induce suction.
In figure 4.22 on page 78, it is observed that, at a fire size of 10 MW and 20 MW, compara-
ble air temperature levels of 120◦C - 180◦C and 180◦C - 300◦C are generally obtained as
wind velocities increase. However, for a 10 MW fire, local temperatures arise in the build-
ing centre in the range 210◦C - 300◦C. It is also observed that the area with 240◦C - 270◦C
increases with rising wind velocities for a 20 MW fire size.
It is noted that for a 5 MW fire size, comparable air temperature ranges of 60◦C - 120◦C
are obtained at a wind velocity of 0 m/s and 14 m/s, which increase to predominantly
90◦C - 150◦C at a wind velocity of 8 m/s. Likewise, it is observed that with increasing
wind velocities, cooler air is drawn through the replacement openings. This is seen in the
dark blue areas in the figure.

4.4.1.4
Intermediate conclusion - Qualitative analysis of the air temperature
distribution
Based on the qualitative description of the temperature distribution (subsections 4.4.1.1
to 4.4.1.3 on pages 75–76), the results indicate that the three fire sizes generally pro-
duce comparable temperature ranges regardless of wind speed, while the absolute levels
shift with increasing fire size (5 MW: 60◦C - 120◦C, 10 MW: 120◦C - 180◦C, 20 MW: 180◦C -
300◦C). Overall, lower temperature levels are observed at 14 m/s compared to 0 m/s and
8 m/s, suggesting that wind influence enhances mixing and thereby reduces the mean
temperature levels.
For the subsequent analyses, reference will be made to measurement point 8, located in
the upper left corner of the building. This point is least affected by local turbulence fields
and is therefore considered the most representative of the general thermal conditions.
4.4.2
Quantitative analysis of air temperature conditions
To clarify the effect of changing the parameter variation (fire size, wind velocity and door
angle), the air temperature conditions from measurement point 8 in the model are pre-
sented. This measurement point has been selected because it is located in a part of the
model where it is affected as little as possible by the three door placements. This as-
sessment is based on the qualitative analysis given in subsection 4.4.1 on page 75. The
measurement points are shown in figure 4.1 on page 45.
The air temperatures at the measurement points are processed as a running mean to
counteract larger fluctuations. The running mean spans the preceding 5 s. In figure 4.23,
the running mean is shown for measurement point 8 in the model. For the remaining
measurement points in the model, see appendix E on page 185.

angle may have an influence. However, this cannot be stated with certainty, since the
table includes only minimum and maximum temperatures, which are strongly affected by
fluctuations. Later in subsection 4.4.2.1, the mean air temperature within the time interval
is examined further.
It is difficult to state whether the door angle has a direct influence on the air temperature
conditions in the building based on figure 4.23 on page 79 and table 4.6 on the facing
page, as the fire size is likely the dominant factor. It is therefore considered necessary to
examine the data using supplementary methods, which is done in the following sections.
4.4.2.1
Mean air temperature across simulations
On the basis of the above, a mean value of the air temperature is determined over a time
period of 50 s, which will thereby represent the output for each simulation for the subse-
quent results processing. A period of 50 s has been chosen in order to reduce the effect
of short-term fluctuations and numerical noise, whilst at the same time being sufficiently
long to capture the air temperature characteristics by including one or more frequencies.
This is assessed to provide a representative description of the air temperature conditions
in the building.
That is, with a wind velocity of 0 m/s in the period 0 s - 500 s, an averaging is performed
over the seconds 450 s - 500 s. With a wind velocity of 8 m/s in the period 500 s - 800 s, an
averaging is performed over the seconds 750 s - 800 s. With a wind velocity of 14 m/s in
the period 800 s - 1100 s, an averaging is performed over the seconds 1050 s - 1100 s.
On figure 4.24, these mean values are presented for the respective simulations with dif-
ferent door angles, fire sizes, and wind velocities, as defined by the air temperature mea-
surement points shown in figure 4.1 on page 45. Curves have been plotted between the
individual measurement points to visualise any shifts in air temperature resulting from
changes in fire size, wind velocity, and door angle.
Figure 4.24: Mean temperature values for the last 50 s in each simulation.

With fire size held constant, figure 4.24 on the previous page indicates that wind veloc-
ity has limited influence, as the curve progressions track one another. For example, for a
5 MW fire size, the three respective wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s and 14 m/s) yield approx-
imately the same air temperature range of 75◦C - 125◦C. The same is observed for 10 MW
and 20 MW fire size, albeit with higher curves (i.e. higher air temperature ranges), as the
curves lie at higher air temperatures. There are clear peak values around temperature
measurement point 5, which is because this point is located in the centre of the fire in
the model. Consequently, this point is omitted from the results processing, as it deviates
substantially from the other measurement points and may not be representative of the air
temperature conditions in the building.
It is also evident from figure 4.24 on the preceding page that the curves with a door angle
of 70° deviate less from one another than for door angles of 112° and 150°, respectively.
This may be because the wind does not affect the internal airflow patterns in the building
at a 70° door angle in the same way as it does at 112° and 150°.
Generally, the air temperatures track one another when compared across changing wind
velocities. However, there is an indication that high wind velocities tend to yield slightly
lower air temperatures at some measurement points for certain door angles. For example,
at measurement points 1, 2, 3, and 4 with a door angle of 112° and fire sizes of 5 MW,
10 MW, and 20 MW, lower air temperatures are observed at a wind velocity of 14 m/s than
at 0 m/s and 8 m/s. This may be due to greater cooling of the air internally within the
building at these measurement points.
It again suggests that the fire size has a significant influence, as the curve progressions
shift upwards with increasing fire size. At the same time, when comparing the curve
progressions horizontally by door angle, they are approximately symmetric in amplitude.
However, the amplitude increases with increasing fire size, which likewise indicates that
the fire size strongly influences the air temperatures. Comparing the amplitudes along the
series between the different door angles shows that they do not deviate much from one
another. This indicates that the door angle likewise does not have a major influence on
the air temperature conditions. In figure 4.25, the manner in which this comparison was
made is illustrated.
Figure 4.25: Explanation of figure 4.24 on the previous page.
A mean-value curve has been drawn in figure 4.25, which reflects the mean air temper-
ature for all wind velocities within the respective fire sizes. These curve progressions
are plotted solely to illustrate the above qualitative analysis carried out on figure 4.24 on

off.
It is evident from the plotted curves in figure 4.25 on the facing page that the amplitude
increases with increasing fire size. This is seen, for example, by comparing the air temper-

amplitude at a 5 MW fire size is lower than at 20 MW. The temperature amplitude for a
door angle of 112° at a 5 MW fire size is less than 25◦C, whereas for the corresponding
situation at a 20 MW fire size it is 75◦C.
It is also evident from figure 4.25 on the preceding page that the amplitude tends to be
slightly skewed, being steeper at the beginning within the respective door angles and then
tapering in a gentler curve towards the rear end within the respective door angles. This
can be explained by the fact that the measurement points in the first part of the curve
progression are located at the replacement air openings, where the fire is drawn down
towards them, as opposed to the last part of the curve, which reflects the measurement
points in the model that are relatively unaffected by the replacement air openings. This
is also consistent with what can be seen in figures 4.20 to 4.22 on page 75, on page 77
and on page 78, as well as with the trends in figures 4.2 to 4.4 on page 48, on page 50
and on page 53.

a fire size of 5 MW and a door angle of 112°, the air temperatures at wind velocities of

the wind velocity does not have a major influence on the air temperature.

Using the same approach, the mean air temperature can be compared with the wind
velocity held at 8 m/s, and varying the door angle is analysed to have less significance
than the fire size. This is exemplified at a fire size of 10 MW and a wind velocity of 8 m/s.
For door angles of 112°, 70° and 150°, the mean air temperatures are 123.5◦C, 122.0◦C
and 132.7◦C, respectively.
However, there is a slight tendency for the temperatures generally to be higher at a door
angle of 150° at a wind velocity of 8 m/s. Nonetheless, it is difficult to say whether this is
a valid trend or merely a random coincidence.
It is clearly evident from the table that the air temperatures do not differ greatly when the
wind velocity or door angle is varied. However, it is unequivocally difficult to state whether
wind velocity and door angle influence the air temperatures, since the fluctuations are
relatively small and may be associated with other factors in the model. Conversely, it is
clear that the fire size has a dominant effect on the air temperatures in the building. To
better analyse the influence of wind velocity and door angle on the air temperatures, the
following section employs box plots to reveal possible trends.
4.4.2.2
Statistical distribution - Boxplots
To assess the different parameter variations relative to one another, box plots grouped by
fire size, wind velocity, and door angle have been used. The box plots include minimum
and maximum values, lower and upper quartiles, median, whiskers, and outliers. These
box plots are based on the averaged dataset for each temperature measurement point.
The box plots are presented in figures 4.27 to 4.29 on pages 85–87.
The box plot in figure 4.27 is grouped by fire size, and therefore includes the air temper-
atures for wind velocity and door angle within the box plot itself.
Figure 4.27: Boxplot grouped by fire size.
Master in Fire Safety
85
==PAGE:112==
From figure 4.27 on the preceding page, there is a clear and systematic increase in the air
temperature with increasing fire size. The minimum and maximum values, lower and up-
per quartiles, median, and mean all shift upwards as the fire size increases. For example,
the mean for a 5 MW fire size is around 100◦C, for 10 MW around 150◦C, and for 20 MW
around 250◦C. This is consistent with the previously applied method, where the mean
values for the respective measurement points were presented as curve progressions.
It is further observed that there is a clear increasing tendency in the spread between the
different fire sizes. For 5 MW, the difference between the minimum and maximum air tem-
perature is approximately 60◦C, for 10 MW approximately 75◦C, and for 20 MW approxi-
mately 125◦C, disregarding outliers. The spread thus increases markedly with increasing
fire size. This development is accompanied by a greater incidence of fluctuations in the
air temperatures at higher fire size, which is also seen as more extreme values in the
figure.
The box plot in figure 4.28 is grouped by wind velocity, and thus shows the air tempera-
tures for fire size and door angles within the box plot itself.
Figure 4.28: Boxplot grouped by wind speed.
From figure 4.28, the median and the mean lie relatively close for the three wind velocities.
A wind velocity of 14 m/s has a slightly lower mean of 160◦C than 165◦C and 170◦C at
0 m/s and 8 m/s, which can be explained by increased cooling due to higher air movement.
The median values generally lie around 150◦C - 155◦C, indicating that the wind velocity
has a limited influence compared with the fire size.
The temperature difference between minimum and maximum is not constant, but is about
240◦C for 0 m/s and 8 m/s, whereas 14 m/s has a larger range of about 295◦C. This reflects
that the spread is clearly greater at the highest wind velocity. The larger upper deviation
at 14 m/s may be due to altered airflow and increased mixing near the openings.
86
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:113==
The above indicates that wind velocity has a weak to moderate influence on the air tem-
perature conditions, but the effect is less pronounced than that observed with increasing
fire size. The box plot in figure 4.29 is grouped by door angle, and thus shows the air
temperatures for fire size and wind velocity within the box plot itself.
Figure 4.29: Boxplot grouped by door angle.
It can be observed in figure 4.29 that the median and mean lie relatively close across the
different door angles, albeit with a slightly higher median at a 150° door angle. There is
a tendency towards a small increase in air temperature from a 70° door angle to 150°,
though the effect is limited. The means lie around 150◦C, in the same manner as when
the air temperature was grouped by wind velocity in figure 4.28 on the preceding page.
It is also seen that the temperature range is roughly similar across wind velocities, at
approximately 130◦C.
From figure 4.29, the median and mean lie close for the three door angles, with a slight
tendency towards a higher median at 150°. Thus, there is a small increase in the median
from 150◦C at 70° to 155◦C at 150°, but the difference is limited. The means generally lie
around 165◦C at 70° and 150°, versus 160◦C at 112°, which roughly corresponds to what
is observed for wind velocity in figure 4.28 on the preceding page. The temperature differ-
ence between minimum and maximum varies somewhat more than previously indicated
in the text. For 70° the range is approximately 240◦C, for 112° approximately 260◦C, and
for 150° approximately 285◦C. It follows that the spread is overall uniform, albeit with a
tendency towards slightly greater variation at 150°.
Overall, the box plot analysis shows that fire size is the clearly dominant parameter for the
air temperature conditions in the building, as both the temperature level and the spread
increase markedly with increasing fire size. Wind velocity and door angle have only a
weak to moderate influence, though small differences are observed at high wind velocity
(14 m/s) and at a 150° door angle, where the spread is slightly larger.

4.4.2.3
Air temperature distribution - Heatmaps
To compare the air temperatures in another way, a heat map has been constructed for
the different simulation scenarios, grouped by door angle. These heat maps, shown in
figure 4.30, are based on the mean air temperature over the last 50 s in each simulation
scenario. Each cell thus shows the mean air temperature for a given combination of fire
size and wind velocity.
Figure 4.30: Heatmap of the temperature in different measuring points.
At a door angle of 70°, a clear increase in air temperature with fire size is observed,
while the variation with wind velocity is limited. For example, at 5 MW the air temperature
changes only slightly between 0 m/s (93.0◦C) and 14 m/s (95.2◦C).
At a door angle of 112°, a more general decrease in air temperature is observed with
increasing wind velocity for all fire sizes. For 10 MW, the air temperature falls from 165.1◦C
at 0 m/s to 132.6◦C at 14 m/s, and for 5 MW the decrease is from 99.5◦C to 73.8◦C.
At a door angle of 150°, the development of air temperature with wind velocity is more
non-uniform. For 20 MW, the air temperature rises from 235.7◦C at 0 m/s to 270.1◦C at
14 m/s, whereas 5 MW shows a smaller decrease from 106.5◦C at 8 m/s to 90.4◦C at
14 m/s. This variation may be due to turbulence at the opening facing the wind, as also
noted under section 4.3 on page 54.
The highest air temperatures in the entire figure are observed at 150°, 20 MW and 14 m/s.
This is not consistent with the box plot analysis, where 14 m/s generally showed lower air
temperatures. The difference can be explained by the fact that the box plot analysis is
based on the mean of the last 50 s at the individual measurement points, whereas the heat
map shows the mean across the 9 measurement points within each simulation scenario.
Figure 4.30 also clearly shows that the influence of the wind on the air temperature does
not follow a linear pattern, but depends on both the fire size and the door angle.
In figure 4.31 on the next page, a deviation analysis is performed, in which the scenarios
with a door angle of 70° and 112° are compared with a door angle of 150°, which is
considered the reference model based on design practice. The figure is structured such
that the 70° and 112° door angles are shown on the left with percentage deviations, while
the air temperatures for a 150° door angle are reproduced on the right (from figure 4.30).
For 70°, the deviations range between approximately -3.7% and +9.0%, which is gener-
ally below 10.0% and can therefore be regarded as acceptable within the air temperature
conditions present in the building. Both positive and negative values are observed, de-
pending on the combination of fire size and wind velocity.
88
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:115==
Figure 4.31: Percentage deviation from the reference with a 150° door angle.
For 112°, the deviations are generally higher than for 70°, with values ranging from -15.3%
to +19.4%. Particularly at 14 m/s, large positive deviations are observed for 5 MW (18.4%)
and 10 MW (19.4%), while negative deviations also occur at low wind velocity, e.g. -15.3%
for 10 MW at 0 m/s. Overall, the analysis shows that the evolution of the air temperature
in the building depends on the fire size, but that both wind velocity and door angle can
influence the deviation in complex, situation dependent patterns.
The heat map analysis (figure 4.30 on the preceding page) shows that the air temperature
increases markedly with fire size for all door angles, while the influence of wind velocity
varies. For 70° the wind effect is limited. For 112° a clear decrease in air temperature
is observed with increasing wind velocity. For 150° the pattern is more non-uniform, with
the highest air temperature at 20 MW and 14 m/s.
The deviation analysis (figure 4.31) confirms that 70° generally lies within ±10% of the
reference (150°), whereas 112° more often deviates further, especially at high wind veloci-
ties. Both positive and negative deviations occur, which underscores that the combination
of fire size, wind velocity, and door angle affects the air temperature in a complex manner.
Although the percentage deviations may appear large, the actual differences in air tem-
perature typically amount to only 30◦C - 50◦C. Relative to a flashover temperature of ap-
proximately 500◦C, this is a relatively limited change, especially at fire sizes of 10 MW
and 20 MW.
4.4.2.4
Coefficient of variation for air temperature
To visualise a sensitivity analysis of the different simulation scenarios, an analysis of the
coefficient of variation has been performed for selected measurement points. The coef-
ficient of variation is a means of describing the stability between the different simulation
scenarios. The coefficient of variation is calculated as the standard deviation divided by
the mean. Refer to equation (4.1) on page 72 for the expression of this method.
The mean used in this analysis remains the mean over the last 50 s in the respective
simulation scenarios. In figure 4.32 on the following page, the coefficient of variation is
shown for all simulation scenarios for measurement point 8. For all other measurement
points, refer to appendix E on page 185. It is generally observed across all measurement
points that air temperatures are stable in this sensitivity analysis.
As the evaluation basis, two thresholds are applied: values below 10% are designated
very stable, while values between 10% and 30% are designated acceptably stable. It is
clearly evident that all scenarios lie below 10%, indicating very stable temperature condi-
tions across door angles, wind velocities, and fire sizes.
Master in Fire Safety
89
==PAGE:116==
Figure 4.32: Coefficient of variation for temperature measuring point 8.
The differences between the door angles 70°, 112° and 150° are small, and no angle
stands out markedly with respect to stability. The low CoV in all scenarios indicates that
the air temperature measurements are consistent, and that the simulations yield robust
results.
4.4.3
Intermediate conclusion of the temperature
The qualitative and quantitative analysis shows that the evolution of the air temperature
in the building is largely determined by the fire size, whereas both wind velocity and door
angle generally have a substantially smaller influence. This is clearly evident from the box
plot analysis, where both air temperature and spread increase markedly with increasing
fire size, while the differences between wind velocities and door angles are relatively
small. The heat map and the deviation analysis confirm this trend, but at the same time
show that the combination of high wind velocity and certain door angles can produce local
increases or decreases in air temperature, especially at 112° and 150°. However, for most
scenarios the deviations are moderate, and for 70° they generally lie within ±10% of the
reference angle 150°. It should be emphasised that, although the percentage deviations
may appear large, the actual differences in air temperature in [◦C] are relatively small
compared with the overall temperature level.
The analysis of the coefficient of variation (CoV) indicates very stable air temperature
conditions across all scenarios, with values consistently below 10% for all combinations
of parameters (except at measurement point 5, which is strongly influenced by the fire
plume). This suggests that the results are consistent, and that the CFD simulations are
robust. Overall, the analysis indicates that the air temperature distribution in the building
is only to a limited extent affected by wind effects and door geometry, whereas the fire
size dominates the thermal field. This is consistent with the expected behaviour under
natural thermal smoke ventilation, where thermal buoyancy is the primary driving force
for flow and heat distribution.
90
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:117==
4.5
Smoke Layer Height and Visibility
Smoke layer height and visibility are both essential parameters in relation to life safety and
evacuation. Within the scope of this study, however, they are considered secondary to
volume flow and temperature, as they are more sensitive to local variations and short-term
fluctuations. The detailed quantitative results are provided in appendix F on page 199 and
appendix G on page 219.
4.5.1
Smoke Layer Height
In the analysed building, however, there is pronounced wind influence, which creates
large variations in the positioning of the smoke layer within the building. This makes the
interpretation of the individual measurement points uncertain with respect to a robust as-
sessment of the smoke layer height in the building. The results clearly bear the character
of snapshots.
Figure 4.33 shows an example of the smoke layer height at measurement point 8 for
different fire sizes, wind velocities, and door angles.
Figure 4.33: Smoke Layer Height at measuring point 8 for each fire size (5 MW, 10 MW,
20 MW) for the different wind speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s) and the different door angles
(70°, 112°, 150°).
The dataset shows pronounced fluctuations in the smoke layer height. Typically in the
range of about 2.5 m – 7 m above the floor, depending on fire size, door angle, and wind
velocity. The amplitude of the fluctuations increases with wind velocity, and the door angle
affects stability. A door opening at 112° appears overall the most robust, whereas 150°
produces the largest variations under wind, with 70° lying between these two.
The fire size affects the level. From 5 MW to 10 MW and 20 MW, the mean smoke layer
height decreases and becomes more wind sensitive, which naturally relates to increased
soot production and turbulence. Critical evacuation situations typically arise at large fire
Master in Fire Safety
91
==PAGE:118==
sizes combined with high wind and a door angle of around 150°. Locally, however, short-
lived dips can occur under other combinations, which further underscores the variability.
Figure 4.34 shows the mean values for the 27 scenarios and confirms these tendencies.
Increasing fire size and wind reduce the mean smoke layer height, while 112° generally
lies slightly higher than the other angles.
Figure 4.34: Mean Smoke Layer Height values for the last 50 s in each simulation.
Due to the considerable wind dependence and the large fluctuations, the smoke layer
height is assessed to be a less suitable primary indicator in the present project. The
focus is therefore directed towards visibility, which is assessed to be more representative
for describing the smoke gases in the building. It is, however, specified that smoke layer
height and visibility are primarily associated with evacuation scenarios.
4.5.2
Visibility
Visibility is used as the primary measurement parameter to describe the evacuation sit-
uation. The measurements are taken at the same points as for air temperature, and
the parameters are systematically varied, fire size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW), wind velocity
(0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s), and door angle (70°, 112°, 150°), to capture both local and global
effects in the building.
Measurements of visibility have been carried out at the same measurement points as for
air temperature. In figure 4.35 on the facing page, an example of the visibility at measure-
ment point 8 is shown for different fire sizes, wind velocities, and door angles.
Figure 4.36 on the next page shows the mean value of the visibility for the 27 simulation
scenarios that have been carried out.
92
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:119==
Figure 4.35: Visibility at measuring point 8 for each fire size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW) for
the different wind speeds (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s) and the different door angles (70°, 112°,
150°).
Figure 4.36: Mean Visibility values for the last 50 s in each simulation.
Master in Fire Safety
93
==PAGE:120==
The results from the simulation study indicate unambiguously that wind velocity is the
dominant parameter for visibility, whereas the door angle has a secondary, but not neg-
ligible effect, and the fire size amplifies the influence of the wind. Without wind, smaller
fires can maintain a level of visibility that allows visual orientation over longer distances.
Already at 8 m/s the levels are reduced markedly, and at 14 m/s the differences between
fire sizes become dominant, since a high fire size increases soot production and thereby
reduces visibility. Combinations of high fire size and high wind are identified as the most
critical scenarios, which is also reflected in the mean values over the last 50 s of each
simulation.
Across measurement points and fire sizes, a door angle of 112° generally appears most
robust, whereas 150° tends to be the most unfavourable. A 70° door angle exhibits greater
local variability, particularly under wind effects, where the flow can create zones with
changing visibility. At 14 m/s, the differences between door angles are slightly reduced,
primarily because the wind dominates and increases the spread in the data (larger fluctu-
ations). These tendencies are supported by the overall review in appendix F on page 199,
where 112° consistently emerges as the most stable angle, 150° yields lower and more
variable values, and 70° is more dependent on local flow patterns and the location of the
measurement point.
The time series demonstrate that, under wind, visibility is not only shifted to lower lev-
els but also becomes more erratic. At 8 m/s clear drops and periods of low visibility are
observed. At 14 m/s the effect intensifies, and the largest fire sizes yield the lowest levels.
A consolidated view of the 27 scenarios shows that the mean values cluster by wind
velocity, and that the door angle particularly affects the outcome at moderate fire sizes.
Without wind the means are higher, 8 m/s and 14 m/s clearly pull them down. For 20 MW
fire size the levels are generally low, and differences between angles are reduced because
wind and fire size dominate. For 5 MW and 10 MW, by contrast, the door angle can be
used for a degree of robustness.
The amplitude of the fluctuations grows with wind velocity, which both affects the mini-
mum values and increases the uncertainty of any snapshot. A simple averaging over 50 s
is therefore useful for comparison, but may be incomplete if the averaging window cap-
tures an unfavourable period. Overall, the door angle can improve conditions (112° is the
most robust), but it cannot neutralise the dominance of the wind, particularly at 14 m/s.
This should be reflected in sizing, operational strategies, and risk assessments, where
scenarios with high wind and large fire size are prioritised as critical.
4.5.3
Intermediate conclusion of Smoke Layer Height and Visibility
Substantial variations are observed between individual measuring points, implying that a
full evaluation should be conducted on a point-by-point basis with respect to door place-
ment, fire size, and wind velocity. Such an analysis lies beyond the scope of this project.
The findings nevertheless indicate that the interaction between wind and door angle is
of considerable importance, with the magnitude of the effect depending on the spatial
position of the measuring point.
In scenarios where life safety is the primary concern, the placement of openings relative to
the building geometry and prevailing wind conditions becomes critical. This is decisive for
smoke spread and, consequently, the available conditions for safe evacuation in naturally
ventilated fire scenarios driven by buoyancy and wind induced flow.
94
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:121==
5
Conclusion
In the present study, the effectiveness of thermal smoke ventilation was investigated as
a function of the placement of replacement-air openings in the façade of a simple square
building, under varying conditions. Three key parameters were systematically varied: fire
size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW), wind speed (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s), and the angular separation
between two replacement-air openings relative to the centre of the building (70°, 112°,
150°). This resulted in 27 unique simulation scenarios carried out in the Fire Dynamics
Simulator, FDS, integrating both wind effects and fire development.
The analysis demonstrated that the interaction between wind speed, fire size, and door
angle determined the inflow and internal flow structures. In the absence of wind, the flow
was governed by buoyancy from the fire, whereas wind effects significantly altered the
flow pattern, particularly when one door was placed on the windward side, parallel to the
wind direction. In such cases, pulsations and redistribution of the inflow between openings
were observed. A larger angular separation consistently produced stronger pulsations,
whose intensity increased with wind speed and fire size. The door aligned with the wind
direction thus became the governing factor for inflow characteristics.
The total volume flow confirmed these observations. In the absence of wind, the angle
had only limited influence, with fire size being the dominant factor. At 8 m/s and 14 m/s,
the outcome was controlled by the combined effect of fire size and wind speed, except
for the 150° case, where the wind consistently dominated. This produced a complex
pattern: at 14 m/s, 112° systematically yielded the highest total volume flow, 150° the
lowest, while 70° lay in between. Variability given by the coefficient of variation, CoV was
especially high at 150°, reflecting unstable, wind-dominated inflow. Overall, wind could
both increase and reduce the volume flow depending on angle and fire size; no single
parameter dominated under all conditions.
The compartment temperature was primarily governed by fire size: approximately 60–
120 ◦C at 5 MW, 120–180 ◦C at 10 MW, and 180–300 ◦C at 20 MW. In the absence of wind,
this hierarchy was preserved, while 14 m/s generally reduced mean temperatures due to
enhanced mixing. Boxplot analysis confirmed that fire size was the dominant parameter,
with wind speed and angle having only weak to moderate influence. Deviation analysis
showed that 70° typically lay within ±10 % of 150°, whereas 112° deviated more strongly
at high wind, both positively and negatively. This indicated a complex interplay between
fire size, wind, and angle, but did not alter the finding that fire size was the governing
factor for temperature levels.
Wind speed was the primary driver: increasing wind reduced both smoke layer height and
visibility significantly, regardless of angle and fire size. The door angle was the secondary
factor, determining robustness: 112° generally provided the most stable conditions, 150°
the most critical, while 70° was advantageous at moderate wind but unstable at high wind.
Fire size was most influential in the absence of wind, determining layer height and visibility;
under moderate wind, it interacted with angle, while at high wind, wind and angle jointly
dominated. The effects varied across measurement points, underlining that a complete
evaluation must always be specific to the building geometry and the placement of the
replacement-air openings.
Master in Fire Safety
95
==PAGE:122==
No optimal angle was identified. While 112° performed best in terms of volume flow and
robustness of smoke layer height and visibility, 150° consistently proved least favourable
and most unstable. An angle of 70° could be beneficial at moderate wind speeds but
lost robustness under stronger winds. The results therefore do not support the hypoth-
esis that a large angle (≥135°) inherently ensures wind-independent performance of
replacement-air openings. For the building geometry, fire sizes, and wind configurations
examined in this study, the hypothesis was refuted. Accordingly, the design should be
based on project-specific assessments of wind conditions to determine the placement of
replacement-air openings.
96
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:123==
6
Outlook
The present study has provided new insights into how fire size, wind velocity, and the
placement of the replacement air openings affect the effectiveness of natural smoke ven-
tilation. However, the model is idealised, and there are several areas that could be ex-
panded to further nuance the results in future studies.
General building regulations
It should be investigated whether there is evidence underpinning the requirements for the
placement of the replacement air openings in the various legal frameworks and design
standards across different countries.
Building Geometry
The analyses were carried out with a square building; future studies should examine how
other configurations e.g., T-shaped, V-shaped, rectangular, triangular, or circular buildings
may influence the effectiveness of natural smoke ventilation with respect to the placement
of the replacement air openings.
In future studies, it would likewise be relevant to examine how the building’s proportions—
length, width, and height—may influence the external wind environment and, conse-
quently, the pressure conditions around the Replacement air openings as well as the
Smoke exhaust openings.
Placement of Replacement Air Openings
The results have shown that the mutual angle between the replacement air openings
affects the overall efficiency of the smoke ventilation system. Therefore, future studies
should include more combinations of angles between the replacement air openings. In
addition, scenarios with more than two openings in the façade should be examined to
determine whether greater robustness can be achieved by distributing the opening area
across multiple openings.
It should additionally be examined whether obstructions in the replacement air openings
influence the resulting Volume flow through them.
Smoke Ventilation Openings
Smoke exhaust openings were modelled as simple square openings, but in practice they
will be equipped with deflectors or similar design features to minimise wind effects. Future
studies should therefore consider a more realistic design of the smoke exhaust openings
to elucidate the influence on the system’s overall efficiency. In addition, the mesh resolu-
tion around the smoke exhaust openings may be crucial for correctly resolving the airflow
through them, which should be verified with a thorough grid-independence analysis.
Wind Conditions and Terrain Effects
In the present study, only a single Wind direction has been used; therefore, future studies
should include multiple Wind directions to assess the significance of varying pressure
distributions around the building with respect to the placement of the replacement air
openings.
Furthermore, terrain roughness, landscape conditions, and neighbouring buildings should
also be included in future studies, as these factors can markedly affect the Flow around
the analysis building and thus the air inflow through the replacement air openings as well
as the smoke exhaust openings.
Master in Fire Safety
97
==PAGE:124==
It would be advantageous to examine the wind validation in greater depth, including the
application of the MOST method (Monin–Obukhov Similarity Theory), to investigate how
the stability classes may influence the results. It would also be beneficial to make the
design of smoke ventilation systems more performance-driven and site-specific, based
on local wind conditions.
Mesh Validation and Full Scale Experiments
To strengthen the validity of future results, the computational mesh should be explicitly
validated with respect to the modelling of the wind environment around the analysis build-
ing, so that correct boundary layer resolution is ensured and grid independence can be
documented. The validation should be benchmarked against existing measurement data
from a full-scale experiment for a comparable building geometry.
The above could also be supplemented with scale-model tests for a building, including
fire and wind effects, which could help verify the CFD-model and increase the reliability
of future studies.
Alternative Simulation Tools
The possibility of employing other CFD codes such as ANSYS Fluent, Siemens Star-
CCM+, and OpenFOAM should be investigated, as these codes allow a more controllable
handling of computational mesh generation than is the case with FDS. Using an alterna-
tive CFD code could reduce uncertainties related to grid dependence and simultaneously
reduce computational time, which would support a more extensive parametric variation.
Big Data for Statistical Data Processing
It is considered relevant to conduct additional simulation scenarios in order to develop
larger statistical models for the data analysis. In this way, it may be easier to discern
relationships and trends associated with parameter variation. Moreover, there is a need
for more researchers to engage with the subject so as to elucidate trends and test the
hypotheses.
Life Safety and Sustainability
It should be assessed how the parameters influence the safety level for evacuees and
the fire resistance of the load-bearing structures. It should also be examined what the
economic implications would be if material consumption can presumably be reduced as
a result of better-designed smoke ventilation systems based on local conditions.
