A
Appendix - Literature study
Master Thesis’ from DTU
Efficiency of thermal fire ventilation when affected by exterior wind (Svensgaard and
Brøndum, 2024)
The thesis explores the efficiency of natural smoke and heat exhaust ventilation systems
(NSHEVS), particularly in how they respond to exterior wind factors. NSHEVS are vital in
fire safety as they restrict fire and smoke spread and ensure adequate time for building
evacuation. However, they are traditionally designed using prescriptive standards that do
not fully account for environmental factors like wind. This research investigates how wind
speed and direction influence the effectiveness of these ventilation systems.
The study utilizes simulations conducted in the Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS), analyzing
temperature changes and smoke layer height within the building. Two distinct models are
employed: one with accurately modeled exhaust openings in the roof and another with
standardized roof holes, common in NSHEVS simulations. Both models are subjected
to various wind speeds (2, 5, and 10 m/s) from different geographic orientations (north,
south, east, and west). Additionally, the placement of air inlets is altered to document
its effect on ventilation performance. But this is not necessarily a focus they have in the
report.
Results show that both wind speed and direction significantly impact the efficiency of
NSHEVS. Specifically, increasing wind causes turbulence and vortex formation, which
disrupt the upward movement of hot gases and smoke, impacting smoke clearance.
Influence on aerodynamic free area for natural smoke and heat exhaust ventilators
in horizontal openings (Andreassen and Carstensen, 2023)
The thesis investigates the impact of limited spacing between skylights on natural smoke
and heat exhaust ventilation systems (NSHEVS) efficiency. Skylights in these systems are
evaluated under EN 12101-2 standards, where the coefficient of discharge—a measure
of efficiency—is determined through wind tunnel testing. This coefficient indicates the
skylight’s effectiveness by comparing its geometric opening area to its aerodynamic free
area, with the lowest values defining performance under varying conditions .
Danish design standards, particularly (Dansk Brand- og Sikringsinstitut, 2021), specify a
correction factor for area when neighboring skylights are placed close together. How-
ever, these guidelines lack clarity on the impact of reduced spacing on overall NSHEVS
performance. This thesis aims to fill this knowledge gap by evaluating how the proximity
of skylights affects efficiency, especially in the presence of wind. Research has shown
that external side wind generally produces the lowest discharge coefficients, making it a
crucial factor in assessing overall system efficiency. Close skylights influence airflow and
create variable pressure zones, potentially hindering ventilation performance.
Computational fluid dynamics (CFD) simulations conducted in Fire Dynamics Simulator
(FDS) were used to model and evaluate different skylight configurations under wind con-
ditions. The results suggest that reduced spacing between skylights typically decreases
NSHEVS efficiency.
In some cases, however, strategic spacing can improve perfor-
mance. For instance, with wind coming from specific directions, one skylight might shelter
another, improving airflow and increasing system efficiency.
Master in Fire Safety
103
==PAGE:130==
Naturlig brandventilation med dobbeltrettet strømninger i horisontale åbninger (Nielsen,
2023)
This thesis explores the dynamics of natural smoke ventilation through horizontal open-
ings without dedicated replacement air openings, focusing on fire safety applications.
Simulations conducted in Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) compare the smoke layer tem-
perature, smoke height, radiation, and mass flow in ventilation systems with and without
replacement air vents. The study considers three fire sizes — 5 MW, 20 MW, and 35
MW — alongside two roof vent opening sizes (10 m² and 24 m²) in a hypothetical building
setup.
Key findings highlight that, under sufficient roof opening areas relative to the heat release
rate, systems without replacement air vents achieve comparable results to systems with
vents. However, this leads to a higher smoke temperature and a lower smoke layer height.
Notably, airflow through horizontal vent openings shifts between inflow and outflow, dic-
tated by pressure differentials and temperature gradients.
The research underscores the importance of design considerations for smoke ventila-
tion systems in rooms with limited openings, where double-directional airflow might be
achieved by optimizing vent area and placement. This study aligns with existing literature
on mass flow and smoke behavior, enhancing understanding of alternative smoke control
methods in building fire safety.
Wind effect on fire- and smoke spread (Hektoen, 2021)
The thesis examines how wind conditions influence fire safety and smoke spread in build-
ings. The primary goal is to determine if wind should be considered a risk factor in fire
safety engineering (FSE) assessments, given that many building regulations do not re-
quire wind considerations in such evaluations.
The thesis discusses how wind impacts fire characteristics, such as flame spread and
smoke propagation, and presents mechanisms of smoke spread, including buoyancy,
stack effect, and wind forces. Fire behavior can intensify as wind adds oxygen, decreases
moisture in combustibles, and transports smoke, potentially reducing evacuation time be-
fore conditions become life-threatening. The study finds that wind-driven smoke moves
differently, affecting visibility and egress safety. Additionally, pressure differences caused
by wind can impede smoke management systems, making evacuation challenging in sce-
narios with negative pressure zones within buildings.
The shopping center case study illustrates the implications of including wind in fire safety
models. Without considering wind, simulations underestimated smoke spread and evac-
uation difficulty. With wind, the case study showed decreased evacuation time, indicating
that occupants had less time to evacuate safely. Moreover, wind direction and velocity
affected smoke infiltration rates and emergency routes’ viability, underscoring the need
for updated fire safety designs that account for wind.
The thesis concludes that wind is indeed a critical factor in fire risk assessments and
should be considered explicitly. The author recommends integrating wind effects in fire
modeling to improve safety margins, particularly in high-rise buildings and large, complex
structures like shopping centers. When it’s challenging to include wind data, an added
safety margin in design can account for potential uncertainties in wind influence.
104
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:131==
Intermediate conclusion
For all the master projects, the primary focus is on exhaust openings. According to Nielsen
(2023), a relatively efficient thermal smoke ventilation can be achieved simply by increas-
ing the free opening area of the exhaust openings. However, Andreassen and Carstensen
(2023) notes that this should be done with caution, as their research suggests that the ef-
ficiency of thermal smoke ventilation decreases if the exhaust openings are placed too
closely together. Both Nielsen (2023) and Andreassen and Carstensen (2023) give less
consideration to the impact of wind on the openings, whereas Svensgaard and Brøndum
(2024) focuses more on how wind affects the exhaust openings with regard to different
wind directions on the building. Svensgaard and Brøndum (2024) indicates that wind in-
fluences the efficiency of thermal smoke ventilation, as increasing wind speed generates
turbulence.
According to Hektoen (2021), wind has a significant effect on smoke dispersion in the
building, which is why it is recommended to include a safety margin in fire dimensioning.
Scientific articles
Effects of Wind on Natural Fire Vents (Brandforsk, 1995)
The paper addresses how wind impacts the performance of natural fire vents. The primary
focus of this study is understanding how wind interacts with these vents, as wind can
both hinder and enhance the ventilation process depending on factors like wind speed,
direction, and vent placement.
Wind can significantly influence how effectively these vents operate. When wind flows
against the vent openings, it can create backpressure, forcing smoke and heat back into
the building rather than allowing them to escape. Conversely, wind blowing in the same
direction as vent openings may help extract smoke more efficiently, enhancing ventilation.
The study includes experiments and simulations that measure the effects of different wind
speeds and directions on natural fire vent performance. Findings indicate that higher wind
speeds generally worsen vent performance if they oppose the flow of escaping smoke,
while they improve performance if aligned with it. The study also emphasizes that vent
design modifications can mitigate some negative effects of adverse wind conditions.
Air inlets play a critical role by supplying fresh air that helps displace smoke and hot
gases through fire vents. The study examines how wind affects both air inlets and outlets.
Specifically, it notes that wind can influence the pressure at air inlets, either facilitating or
impeding the flow of air into the building. This pressure difference is essential for effective
ventilation, as it drives the air through the structure, helping clear smoke-filled areas.
The study concludes that replacement air openings placed opposite each other affect the
efficiency of thermal fire ventilation, as a jet stream will be created through the building if
both are open.
Wind Effects On Heat And Smoke Control Of Industrial Buildings In Case Of A Fire
(Kramer and Gerhardt, 1990)
The paper examines how wind influences smoke extraction and heat control systems
in industrial buildings during a fire. The study highlights the critical role of natural and
mechanical venting systems, particularly focusing on roof vents, which are essential for
smoke and heat removal. It explores how wind interactions impact these systems, em-
phasizing the need for precise design to maintain effective venting and ensure safety in
fire events.
Master in Fire Safety
105
==PAGE:132==
The authors delve into the relationship between smoke production and combustion gas
flow. Using data from various studies, they present equations that relate the combustion
gas flow to factors such as fire plume circumference and height, as well as energy output.
The authors address challenges in air intake management, especially under high-wind
conditions. Large air intake openings are recommended to ensure sufficient air inflow
during a fire. However, windward-facing openings can create jets of air that disrupt the
smoke-free layer by causing secondary airflow and mixing fresh air with smoke. To miti-
gate this, smoke skirts can be installed to separate smoke and fresh air flows, ensuring a
stable smoke-free zone and more efficient smoke control.
The study concludes with practical recommendations for designing effective venting sys-
tems in industrial buildings. For instance, high-efficiency roof vents are crucial, and vent
placement near the roof edges should generally be avoided. The internal airflow dy-
namics within buildings should also be considered, as vent interference can significantly
impact smoke extraction effectiveness. In summary, the paper emphasizes that success-
ful smoke control in industrial buildings requires a detailed understanding of wind effects,
vent design, and strategic placement to optimize heat and smoke extraction during fires.
An Experimental Fire in Compartment with Dual Vent on Opposite Walls (Kumar and
Naveen, 2007)
The study investigates the effect of ventilation on fire behavior in enclosed spaces, specif-
ically in a compartment with dual vents on opposite walls. Traditional research has pri-
marily examined single-vent configurations. This study addresses the gap by analyzing
fire dynamics in a dual-vent compartment to understand how cross-ventilation affects fire
growth, burning rates, and temperature distribution.
The results showed that cross-ventilation significantly impacts fire behavior, with distinct
variations in burning rates and temperature profiles. In larger fires, the rate of burning
and heat release was higher in cross-ventilation conditions than in single-vent scenarios.
This difference arises because cross-ventilation allows more air to enter, enhancing fuel
combustion and leading to higher temperatures and heat release rates. For smaller fires,
however, the temperature in single-vent conditions was slightly higher. This may be due
to weaker plumes that do not fully reach the ceiling in cross-ventilation, allowing gases to
exit before accumulating significant heat.
The study observed that the gas flow patterns varied between ventilation conditions. In
single-vent conditions, gases primarily flowed toward the vent, creating a horizontal heat
layer. In contrast, cross-ventilation encouraged vertical and multidirectional gas move-
ment, resulting in a more complex convection current.
This study demonstrates that cross-ventilation significantly alters the fire dynamics in a
compartment by increasing the rate of air entrainment and enhancing fuel combustion.
The findings suggest that for larger fires, cross-ventilation promotes higher temperatures
and more intense combustion, likely due to improved mixing of air with fuel vapors. For
smaller fires, however, cross-ventilation may lead to lower compartment temperatures
because of plume dilution.
In general, cross-ventilation in buildings should be carefully considered, as one conse-
quence of cross-ventilation can be complete mixing, leading to the introduction of a large
amount of oxygen to the fire. This can result in higher temperatures and increased smoke
production.
106
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:133==
Effects of wind, buoyancy and thermal expansion on a room fire with natural ven-
tilation (Chow et al., 2014)
The study investigates the effects of wind, buoyancy, and thermal expansion on room
fires in naturally ventilated buildings, where fire dynamics are critical due to sustainability-
focused designs favoring natural ventilation. The research models the room as a single
thermal zone with two openings, assessing airflow patterns under various heat release
rates. Three primary airflow modes emerge, depending on heat release intensity: wind-
dominant, buoyancy-dominant, and thermal expansion-dominant.
At low heat release rates, thermal expansion’s effect on airflow is minimal, with wind ac-
tion and buoyancy as primary forces. Wind generally induces inward airflow through the
upper opening, while buoyancy pulls air out of the upper opening and into the lower.
In cases of medium heat release rates, all three forces—wind, buoyancy, and thermal
expansion—significantly influence air movement. For high heat release rates, thermal
expansion prevails over buoyancy, pushing air outward from both openings, regardless
of wind direction.
Simulations show that at lower heat levels, wind dominates, maintaining a stable flow
in and out of openings. With intermediate fires, both thermal expansion and buoyancy
interact with wind, creating zones where one or both forces momentarily counterbalance,
stabilizing temperature fluctuations. At extreme heat levels, thermal expansion pressure
outweighs others, enforcing a unidirectional outward flow.
This research expands on previous simplified models, emphasizing the critical role of
thermal expansion in larger fires within naturally ventilated rooms. The study concludes
that neglecting thermal expansion can lead to inaccurate predictions, especially for high-
temperature fires.
The study identifies that at low heat release rates, wind can drive air into the upper opening
while air exits through the lower one. However, at higher heat release rates, thermal
expansion becomes more dominant, sometimes even overshadowing the effect of wind
and directing airflow out of both openings.
Numerical Study on Fire characteristics in Force-Ventilated Compartment with dif-
ferent air inlet locations (Zhang et al., 2017)
The study explores fire characteristics within a force-ventilated compartment, focusing
on how different air inlet configurations impact flame behavior, oxygen levels, soot con-
centration, and smoke velocity. This research uses computational fluid dynamics (CFD)
simulations, specifically the Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS), to examine how variations in
air inlet location influence fire behavior.
The compartment studied has dimensions of 3,6 m (length) x 3,4 m (width) x 2,05 m
(height) and contains two air inlets and one exhaust vent. Simulations were conducted
with air inlets at two elevations: 0,25 m and 1,65 m from the floor. Both one-inlet and two-
inlet scenarios were modeled. In the one-inlet cases, flames tended to lean toward the
open inlet due to the strong airflow on that side, which increased fire plume entrainment.
Conversely, in two-inlet scenarios, flames did not lean toward any inlet but instead inclined
towards the exhaust vent.
The study found that oxygen concentration was generally higher in one-inlet configura-
tions compared to two-inlet setups, as the latter allowed for greater oxygen mixing and
consumption. In terms of soot concentration, simulations showed that compartments with
Master in Fire Safety
107
==PAGE:134==
higher air inlet elevation produced more uniform soot distribution, indicating better air mix-
ing. Additionally, in one-inlet scenarios, the velocity of smoke was greater on the side with
the air inlet, contributing to the inclined flame behavior observed.
The findings underscore that air inlet placement significantly influences fire behavior in
force-ventilated environments. The research highlights the complex interactions between
airflow and fire dynamics, particularly how ventilation setups can alter flame behavior, gas
concentrations, and smoke movement.
Wind and Fire Coupled Modelling - Part I Literature review (Wegrzyński and Lipecki,
2018)
This paper reviews the coupling of wind and fire dynamics, emphasizing the importance of
Computational Wind Engineering (CWE) in fire safety and fire dynamics modeling. Wind
significantly influences fire behavior, affecting flame spread, smoke dispersal, and overall
fire intensity in various environments, including buildings, and tunnels.
The paper discusses key applications of wind-fire modeling, such as indoor flows, natural
smoke ventilation, and the design of smoke exhaust systems for large structures. For ex-
ample, it notes that high wind velocities can obstruct smoke vents or create reversed flows,
while carefully designed smoke control systems can leverage wind to improve smoke ex-
traction. The authors explore design guidelines for natural smoke ventilators, highlighting
factors like wind velocity and vent placement that impact system performance.
Wind and Fire Coupled Modelling - Part II Good practice guidelines (Wegrzyński
et al., 2018)
The paper addresses the critical role of wind in fire safety engineering, specifically in how
airflow influences fire spread, smoke dispersion, and overall fire dynamics. Wind can
significantly alter fire behavior, accelerating fire spread rates, extending smoke dispersion.
The paper is structured as a guideline to implement CWE practices in fire scenarios,
focusing on urban environments, natural ventilation, and smoke control. It outlines how air
inlet openings and other architectural features impact ventilation systems, smoke exhaust
efficiency, and structural response to wind-driven fire scenarios.
Various turbulence models are discussed, including RANS (Reynolds-Averaged Navier-
Stokes) and LES (Large Eddy Simulation), with LES generally providing better accuracy
in simulating turbulent flows around structures. Additionally, the paper recommends us-
ing detailed CFD modeling for specific structural elements, such as vents, with elements
over 5 cm represented in the model to accurately simulate discharge characteristics. The
introduction of wind boundary conditions also requires careful parameterization, using
historical or real-time wind data to accurately represent wind profiles in simulations.
This paper aims to improve FSE practices by adopting CWE methodologies, contributing
to better-informed designs for fire prevention, enhanced safety measures in fire-prone
areas, and more accurate simulations of wind-driven fire scenarios.
Effect of Ambient Wind Speed on Pressure Distribution and Smoke Movement in
Single and Multiple (Zhou et al., 2019)
This paper examines the impact of ambient wind speed on pressure distribution and
smoke movement in single and multi-compartment fire scenarios. Using numerical sim-
ulations, the authors analyze how variations in wind speed, air inlet, and smoke outlet
locations influence smoke spread, exhaust efficiency, and pressure dynamics in building
108
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:135==
fires. The study considers the effects of different configurations for air inlets and out-
lets positioned on windward, leeward, sideward walls, and ceilings, across various wind
conditions.
The findings indicate that wind-driven pressure significantly affects smoke movement
and exhaust, especially as wind speed increases. For instance, under certain condi-
tions (windward-leeward and windward-sideward), higher wind speeds increase outlet
flow velocities, enhancing smoke extraction. However, the configuration plays a crucial
role: in windward-leeward (WL) conditions, pressure differentials across openings rise
parabolically with wind speed, and larger smoke outlets improve exhaust performance.
Conversely, larger air inlets reduce the pressure differential, which may hinder smoke
control. The smoke exhaust rate also varies by the position of the outlet, with ceiling
outlets showing the best smoke removal performance in most scenarios.
The study notes that at wind speeds above 2 m/s, wind-driven pressure generally domi-
nates over fire-driven thermal pressure, which changes the dynamics of smoke stratifica-
tion and exhaust efficiency. In corridors, smoke stratification becomes less prominent as
wind speed rises, reducing visible layers of smoke. For multiple compartments, smoke
spread through adjoining rooms decreases as wind speed increases, and higher inlet and
outlet areas can improve exhaust performance and pressure stability.
The authors suggest that positioning and sizing of inlets and outlets can be optimized for
better smoke management in real-world building fire scenarios influenced by environmen-
tal wind. Ceiling outlets, in particular, show the highest efficiency for smoke removal, while
windward inlets paired with ceiling outlets offer optimal conditions for natural ventilation.
Fire smoke dispersion inside and outside of a warehouse (Wegrzyński et al., 2023)
The paper investigates the impact of wind on smoke dispersion and exhaust system per-
formance during a warehouse fire, using Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) simula-
tions. It analyzes the effects of various wind velocities and directions on smoke flow within
and around the building. The simulations cover three main conditions: no wind, moderate
wind (5 m/s), and strong wind (10 m/s), examining 12 wind angles at 30° increments.
The warehouse uses Natural Smoke and Heat Exhaust Ventilators (NSHEV) with inlets
located on its lower façades and ventilators on the roof. In moderate to strong wind condi-
tions, system performance fluctuates due to changes in pressure across these inlets and
outlets, ranging from 74% to 158% of the no-wind performance. The study finds that wind
can enhance or hinder the exhaust flow depending on the wind angle and surrounding
building configurations. For instance, certain wind directions cause increased exhaust
flow, while others can hinder it by generating pressure differences that obstruct airflow or
lead to smoke accumulation.
Intermediate conclusion
All the references above generally address fire ventilation and wind effects, with some
focusing on the impact of wind on smoke movement within the fire-affected space and
others on how wind on the building affects the efficiency of fire ventilation.
However, none of the references specifically consider the impact of wind on the placement
of replacement air openings.
Master in Fire Safety
109
==PAGE:136==
Danish guidelines
Brandventilationsanlæg - DBI retningslinje 027 (Dansk Brand- og Sikringsinstitut, 2021)
The standard provides guidelines for designing and implementing smoke ventilation sys-
tems according to Danish regulations, covering both exhaust and replacement air open-
ings. For replacement air openings, it specifies that they should be placed on at least
two facades that are offset by at least 135 degrees from each other to counteract adverse
wind effects. The area of these replacement air openings must be distributed so that each
facade has at least 40 percent of the required area.
This distribution across two facades is intended to minimize the risk of achieving a higher
negative pressure on the replacement air openings than the negative pressure on the roof
smoke ventilation openings.
International regulations
DS 12101-4 - Smoke and heat control systems - Part 4: Installed SHEVS systems
for smoke and heat ventilation (Dansk Standard, 2009)
The standard provides guidelines on the design and implementation of smoke and heat
exhaust ventilation systems (SHEVS), with a specific focus on air inlet openings and wind
management. Air inlets are essential in SHEVS to replace smoke-filled air with fresh air
during fire events, improving evacuation safety and firefighting effectiveness. Air inlets
can be designed as natural or powered, depending on the system and building require-
ments. Natural air inlets, often positioned near the floor to maintain a clear smoke-free
layer above, allow fresh air to enter due to thermal buoyancy.
For natural systems with wall-mounted air inlets, it is critical to address wind effects that
could impact smoke control. To mitigate this, the guidelines recommend using air inlets
on at least two different building sides, especially in locations subject to variable wind
conditions. When wind speeds exceed 1 m/s, wind direction monitoring systems activate
to close the inlets on the windward side, thereby only allowing airflow through the inlets
on the opposite side. This selective inlet operation reduces the impact of wind on smoke
movement and helps maintain the efficiency and integrity of the smoke layer in the build-
ing. This setup prevents the wind from pushing smoke back into the building or disrupting
the intended smoke movement towards the exhaust vents.
The guideline specifies that the proper sizing, positioning, and operation of air inlets are
crucial for SHEVS to perform as designed. The airflow through the inlets must match the
needs of the exhaust system, ensuring that the supply of fresh air can replace the volume
of smoke vented.
Wind direction monitoring is particularly relevant for natural SHEVS, where external fac-
tors like wind could compromise the system’s effectiveness. By adapting air inlet operation
based on wind conditions, SHEVS can provide reliable smoke control under various en-
vironmental scenarios. This design approach emphasizes safety, system efficiency, and
regulatory compliance, highlighting the importance of planning and maintaining these sys-
tems for optimal performance in real-world fire conditions.
NFPA 92 - Standard for Smoke Control Systems (The National Fire Protection Associ-
ation, 2024)
NFPA 92 specifies requirements for air inlet openings in smoke control systems, particu-
larly for makeup air that replaces the air exhausted to control smoke movement.
110
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:137==
These openings must be located and designed to minimize interference with smoke con-
trol and to ensure proper airflow balance.
The standard provides guidelines on the size and capacity of inlet openings to ensure
adequate airflow. Sufficient makeup air is critical to prevent negative pressure zones that
could disrupt smoke movement and compromise the smoke control system.
NFPA 92 considers external wind pressure as it can significantly impact the performance
of smoke control systems, particularly in high-rise buildings. High winds can affect airflow
patterns, potentially causing unintended air movement that disrupts smoke containment
and extraction.
The standard suggests that designers account for prevailing wind directions and velocities
in the system design. This may include the placement of air inlet openings, the use of
pressure control techniques, or adjustments to fan capacities.
In some cases, the standard allows for shielding or barriers to mitigate wind effects on air
inlet openings, especially in areas exposed to strong winds.
According to the standard, there are no specific requirements for the placement of re-
placement air openings, leaving it up to the responsible designer of the SHEVS system
to address the considerations mentioned above.
Røykkontroll av bygninger (Norges byggforskningsinstitutt, 2024)
The document, a guideline on smoke control in buildings, outlines key principles for fire
ventilation to improve safety during fires. The guideline specify minimum dimensions for
the air inlets, depending on the type of building and expected smoke production. Properly
placed and sized, these vents help keep the smoke layer high, minimizing exposure to
toxic gases.
The document covers additional considerations, such as placing fire vents over high-risk
areas and conducting regular maintenance. In some cases, smoke compartments with
barriers can be employed to control smoke spread, enhancing occupant safety in large
rooms or open spaces. Specific advice is provided for stairwells, garages, basements,
and lofts, including recommendations on ventilation and pressurization to prevent smoke
infiltration into evacuation routes.
Intermediate conclusion
All the aforementioned standards include considerations for replacement air in fire venti-
lation systems and protection against adverse wind effects. Dansk Brand- og Sikringsin-
stitut (2021) specifies explicit requirements to address the risk of adverse wind influence,
whereas Dansk Standard (2009), The National Fire Protection Association (2024) and
Norges byggforskningsinstitutt (2024) provide broader frameworks, allowing the designer
of the fire ventilation system flexibility to dimension and validate the system accordingly.

B
Appendix - Validation of the Fire Model
In this appendix, an investigation is carried out to determine the cell size required to obtain
grid-independent simulation results for the developed FDS model. The evaluation con-
siders smoke layer height, temperature in the upper smoke layer, and volumetric flows
through the roof openings and the replacement air openings.
Geometry
The building described insubsection 3.1.1 on page 21 is applied, including the thermal
boundary conditions for the enclosing surfaces such as walls and roof. For the validation,
the two replacement air openings are positioned at an angle of 150° relative to the build-
ing’s centreline, as illustrated in figure B.1. The figure also shows the six roof openings
used for the venting of hot gases from the compartment. Each opening is modelled as a
square with an area of 2.56 m2 (1.6 m × 1.6 m).
Figure B.1: Model of building used for mesh validation
Grid Independence Study
The fire simulations do not include mechanical ventilation or external wind effects, implying
that the flow field is governed by a thermally driven buoyant plume. The level of detail in
resolving this plume is quantified by the non-dimensional expression D∗/δx, also referred
to as the plume resolution index, where D∗represents the characteristic fire diameter as
defined in equation (B.1) (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b):

Several methodologies have been proposed in the literature for determining an optimal cell
size, although they are generally regarded only as approximate guidelines (Kevin McGrat-
tan et al., 2024b,c). It must therefore be documented that further mesh refinement does
not lead to noticeable differences in the results, thereby establishing grid independence
McGrattan (2007); McGrattan et al. (2003). According to McGrattan (2007); McGrattan
et al. (2003), experience has shown that a ratio D∗/δx in the range of 5 - 10 usually pro-

Master in Fire Safety

The plume resolution index primarily quantifies the resolution of the fire plume, as it de-
pends solely on the fire heat release rate and constants such as air properties and grav-
itational acceleration (equation (B.1) on page 113). Therefore, additional quantities such
as temperature, air velocity, and mass flow in the global flow field must also be assessed
to demonstrate grid independence.
Finally, since the FDS pressure solver is based on Fast Fourier Transforms (FFTs) in the
y and z directions, the number of cells in these dimensions should ideally follow the form
2l3m5n to maximize efficiency. However, as the pressure solver typically consumes less
than 10 % of the total CPU time, this condition has negligible impact on overall runtime.
For this reason, the mesh division in this study does not adhere strictly to this form, as
it influences only computational speed and not the numerical stability or accuracy of the
results. (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b,c)

flame heights and temperatures (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024c). For a horizontal burner,
Q∗is defined as (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b):

Smoke layer height
As described in chapter 4 on page 45, the smoke layer height is measured in nine ver-
tical columns positioned as shown in figure 3.5 on page 26. In FDS, the smoke layer
height approximates the interface between the hot smoke and the cooler clear air. The
method, described in (Kevin McGrattan et al., 2024b), is an approximate approach that
enables comparison with, for example, two-zone models in accordance with (Karlsson
and Quintiere, 2022).
Figure B.2 on the next page shows the smoke layer height at measurement point S3 for
the three different cell sizes of 0.10 m, 0.20 m, and 0.40 m as a function of time.
116
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:143==
Figure B.2: Smoke layer height at measurement point S3 as a function of time for the
three different cell sizes of 0.10 m, 0.20 m, and 0.40 m.
It can be observed from figure B.2 that stationary conditions are reached after approx-
imately 300 s of simulation. However, temporal fluctuations occur once the stationary
state has been reached, caused by the natural variability in the flow combined with ther-
mal buoyancy, which in turn affects the smoke layer height. With a cell size of 0.40 m,
fewer fluctuations are observed compared to a cell size of 0.10 m. This indicates that a
finer resolution of the computational mesh resolves turbulent structures in greater detail,
resulting in larger fluctuations of the smoke layer height compared to a coarser resolution.
For illustration, the smoke layer height is presented only for measurement point S3 in
figure B.2. The remaining measurement points are omitted since similar behaviour is
expected throughout the computational domain.
A mean value of the smoke layer height is determined for the period 450 s – 500 s to allow
comparison of the three mesh resolutions. This provides a stable value indicating where
the smoke layer stabilises in the steady state.
Table B.5 on the following page summarises the time-averaged smoke layer height (SLH)
at measurement points S1–S9 over 450 s – 500 s for cell sizes of 0.10 m, 0.20 m, and
0.40 m, corresponding to Mesh 1, Mesh 2, and Mesh 3.
From table B.5 on the next page, it can be seen that comparable smoke layer heights
are obtained at the nine measurement points regardless of cell size, with differences of
approximately 0.03 m – 0.50 m. This should also be considered relative to the fact that a
cell size of up to 0.40 m is applied. The largest differences are observed when changing
from 0.40 m to 0.10 m. A relatively large deviation is observed at S5, which is located in
the fire centre, where a 0.40 m cell size underestimates the smoke layer height compared
to 0.10 m and 0.20 m.

As shown in figure B.3 on the facing page, the smoke layer height is over- or underes-

by approximately 6 %.
In conclusion, a 0.40 m cell size leads to larger deviations and both over- and underestima-
tion of smoke layer height compared to 0.10 m and 0.20 m. Consequently, the precision
of the model is reduced at 0.40 m. As comparable values are obtained with 0.10 m and
0.20 m cell sizes, it is concluded that 0.10 m represents the grid-independent solution for
the smoke layer height.

solution.

123
==PAGE:150==
When airflow passes through the roof openings or doors, it is constricted due to the vena
contracta effect which corresponds to well-documented jet contraction and flow separation
phenomena in computational fluid dynamics Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024b); Yuen and
Yeoh (2008). It is found that the calculated volume flow decreases as the mesh resolution
becomes finer. This suggests that a 0.10 m cell size is better able to resolve the detailed
flow patterns that develop through the openings.
Based on the above, it is concluded that a cell size of 0.10 m provides acceptable results
for volume flow through the openings in the present study. It should be noted, however,
that subsection 3.2.2 on page 35 demonstrated the need for a cell size of at least 0.05 m
along the external boundaries of the building to capture the flow around the structure.
Consequently, a cell size of 0.05 m is applied locally around the openings.
Fire Characteristics
The design fire is validated using the heat release rate (HRR) and the mass loss rate
(MLR), which represent the most fundamental fire characteristics. HRR describes the
energy released per unit of time, whereas MLR expresses the corresponding rate of fuel
consumption. Karlsson and Quintiere (2022)
Heat Release Rate
The design fire is validated by comparing the simulated heat release rate, HRR, with the
prescribed fire size defined in subsection 3.1.5 on page 28. In the following, the HRR is
shown on the y-axis and time on the x-axis in figure B.8.
Figure B.8: Simulated heat release rate, HRR, for the design fire of 5,000 kW, 10,000 kW
and 20,000 kW.
As shown in figure B.8, the maximum fire size is reached after approximately 10 s for the
three design fires, after which the values stabilise at mean levels of 5,001 kW, 10,003 kW,
and 20,006 kW over the period 20 s – 1,100 s. This corresponds to deviations of only
124
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:151==
0.02 %, 0.03 % and 0.03 % from the prescribed design fire sizes. The simulations there-
fore reproduce fire sizes that are essentially identical to the intended design values. The
figure also demonstrates that the fires are fuel-controlled, as the HRR is sustained through-
out the simulations. It is further noted that the 20,000 kW fire exhibits larger fluctuations
compared to the 5,000 kW and 10,000 kW fires, which is attributed to the greater dynam-
ical variability introduced by increasing fire size.
The simulated HRR is additionally presented for three wind conditions of 0 m/s, 8 m/s, and
14 m/s over the periods 0 s – 500 s, 501 s – 800 s, and 801 s – 1,100 s, respectively. The
mean values for these periods are summarised in table B.11.

Verification regarding the fire
To verify the FDS model, a hand calculation of the height of the smoke layer and the corre-
sponding average temperature of the smoke layer has been carried out. The calculation
follows the iterative procedure described in Karlsson and Quintiere (2022), where an ini-
tial estimate of the smoke layer height is made, followed by calculations of the pressure
difference in the lower openings, the temperature and density of the smoke layer, and
the mass flow through the opening of the roof. The procedure is repeated until a mass
balance between the plume flow and the exhaust flow is achieved. The calculation re-
sults in a single theoretical temperature for the smoke layer and a theoretical smoke layer
height, which are later compared to the nine measurement points from the CFD model.
The results from the CFD model with a cell size of 0.1 m are applied, as it was found in
subsection 3.2.1 on page 34 that this resolution yields grid-independent results
The calculation procedure is as follows (Karlsson and Quintiere, 2022):

As can be seen from the above calculation procedure, the contraction coefficient, Cv, for
the replacement air openings and the smoke ventilation openings is included in steps 3
Master in Fire Safety
127
==PAGE:154==
and 6, respectively. The Cv-factor has a constant value that depends on the geometry of
the opening, including whether it is equipped with upstands or deflectors. For openings
that have not been tested, such as doors and gates used for replacement air, the Cv-factor
can normally be set to 0.7 in accordance with Wegrzyński and Krajewski (2017).
A study referenced in Wegrzyński and Krajewski (2017) reports Cv factors ranging from
0.55 to 0.65, depending on the manufacturer, for a square opening with an upstand height
of 0.32 m. The Cv factor of a roof opening is strongly influenced by the height of the
upstand and the presence of deflectors. An upstand increases aerodynamic efficiency by
separating the external airflow and enhancing the buoyancy-driven exhaust of hot gases,
which results in a higher Cv factor. Deflectors shield the vent from adverse wind effects
and guide the flow upwards, thereby reducing turbulence and backflow (Wegrzyński and
Krajewski, 2017; Dansk Standard, 2009).
In the constructed FDS model applied in the present study, exhaust openings are repre-
sented as square openings without upstands or deflectors, and no experimental data are
available for these openings. Consequently, the actual contraction coefficient is unknown.
It must therefore be assumed that the Cv factor is lower than those reported in the study
referenced in Wegrzyński and Krajewski (2017). In the above calculation procedure, a Cv
factor range of 0.3–0.7 is applied for roof openings. Table B.13 presents the theoretical
values of the upper smoke layer temperature, Tg, the smoke layer height above the floor,
z, and the internal surface area in contact with hot smoke gases, Aw, as a function of the
Cv factor.

floor, z, and internal surface area in contact with hot smoke gases, Aw, as a function of
the Cv factor.

In figure B.10 on the facing page, the theoretical smoke layer height is shown for a Cv
factor in the range 0.3–0.7 for the roof smoke ventilation openings, compared with the
values obtained at the nine measurement points from the developed CFD model.
The simulated smoke layer height varies between 3.5 m–4.9 m, with a mean value of
3.9 m. The best agreement is obtained for Cv = 0.3–0.4, where the theoretical values
of 3.4 m and 4.1 m correspond to differences of 0.5 m (13.5 %) and 0.2 m (4.2 %), re-
spectively, relative to the mean value from the CFD model. As highlighted in Heskestad
(1984); The National Fire Protection Association (2024), empirical plume models may be
associated with an uncertainty of 10 %–20 %, and the results are therefore considered
to demonstrate satisfactory agreement for Cv = 0.3–0.4. At higher Cv factors (> 0.4),
however, a systematic shift towards larger smoke layer heights is observed, leading to in-
creased divergence between the hand calculations and the CFD model. If measurement
point 5 is disregarded, since it is located at the centre of the fire and may not necessar-
ily represent the mean smoke layer height, the mean smoke layer height from the CFD
model can be determined as 3.8 m, which does not alter the observation regarding the
influence of the Cv-factor.

Master in Fire Safety
129
==PAGE:156==
across the nine measurement points, ranging from a minimum of 70.1 ◦C to a maximum
of 121.4 ◦C. The highest value occurs at measurement point 5, which is located at the
centre of the fire and may therefore be directly influenced by the fire plume. This value
is consequently excluded from the determination of the mean, as it is considered not to
be representative of the average smoke layer temperature. The mean temperature is
thus calculated as 74.1 ◦C, compared with 79.3 ◦C if the value at measurement point 5 is
included.
It can be seen from figure B.11 on the previous page that the theoretical value of the
upper gas layer temperature ranges from 69.7 ◦C–72.0 ◦C depending on the Cv factor.
The best agreement is obtained for Cv = 0.3–0.4, where the theoretical values of 72.0 ◦C
and 70.9 ◦C are close to the mean upper smoke layer temperature of 74.1 ◦C from the CFD
model. This corresponds to a difference of 2.9 %–4.4 % between the hand calculation and
the CFD model for Cv = 0.3–0.4. If measurement point 5 is included in the mean smoke
layer temperature, the difference increases to 10.2 %–11.9 %. In this context, Johansson
(2016) notes that empirical models may be associated with an uncertainty of 5 %–20 %.
Intermediate Sonclusion of Verification Regarding the Fire
Overall, the comparison between the hand calculations and the CFD model shows that
both the smoke layer height and the upper layer temperature are reproduced within ac-
ceptable margins. The best agreement is found for Cv = 0.3–0.4, where deviations be-
tween theory and simulation are within 3 %–4 % for the temperature and around 4 %–13 %
for the smoke layer height. These differences are consistent with the documented uncer-
tainties of empirical plume correlations, which typically range from 5 %–20 % (Johansson,
2016; Heskestad, 1984; The National Fire Protection Association, 2024). Although the
CFD model itself is subject to uncertainties in smoke and heat transport, the verification
demonstrates that FDS provides results at a comparable level to the hand calculations
and can therefore be applied with confidence for this type of analysis.

Appendix - Validation of Wind model
In this section, validation of the wind model is examined. An initial validation is performed
with respect to the solar altitude, including the input parameters used for this purpose.
Subsequently, a validation of the pressure coefficient cp and the boundary layer parameter
y+ is presented.
Determination of solar altitude
In the following, the formulas used to derive the solar altitude over the year, assuming a
northern latitude of 54°, are set out. For Denmark this lies in the interval 54° – 58°.
First, the declination is calculated throughout the year using the following formula (Duffie
and Beckman, 2013):
δ = 23.45 sin
(
360284 + n
365
)
(C.1)
where:
n
Day number
[-]
Subsequently, the solar altitude can be calculated for the various declinations over the
year using the following formula (Duffie and Beckman, 2013):
cos θz = cos ϕ cos δ cos ω + sin ϕ sin δ
(C.2)
where:
θz
Zenith angle
[◦]
ϕ
Latitude
[◦]
ω
Hour angle
[◦]
It holds that αs = 90◦−θz
⇒
sin αs = cos θz, hence equation C.2 can be rewritten as:
sin αs = cos ϕ cos δ cos ω + sin ϕ sin δ
(C.3)
According to Duffie and Beckman (2013), the hour angle can be determined by the fol-
lowing relation:
ω = 15◦· (soltid −12)
(C.4)
from which it follows that the largest contribution to equation C.3 will occur at 12:00, as
the hour angle is 0° at that time, which implies that cos ω = 1.
In figure C.1 on the following page, the solar altitude for Denmark over the year is shown,
and it is evident that the solar altitude over the year in Denmark never exceeds 60°.
It is therefore concluded that, for construction in Denmark, “Moderate” Daytime insolation
applies, implying that, given the specified wind velocities, the conditions are unstable at
low wind velocity (in the present project 0 m/s) and neutral at higher wind velocities (in the
present project 8 m/s and 14 m/s).

Quantitative analysis of cp and y+ for 2 m/s
Figure C.6 on the next page - figure C.11 on page 137
Pressure coefficient
The figures reproduce the expected wind pressure profile for a building, with positive
stagnation pressure at the front, followed by an increasing pressure at the roof surface
and thereafter a gradual decrease in pressure across the roof towards the rear edge. This
is also in agreement with figure 2.4 on page 15.
0.025 m and 0.05 m lie very close across the entire profile, with only small differences
around the region (x/L ≈0.55 −0.65). The 0.10 m and 0.20 m curves are less steep and
are slightly shifted at the “valley” and the “recovery” towards the end, whereas the 0.40 m
curve deviates markedly with a premature inflection and an altered amplitude progression.
The same is observed in the horizontal cross-section, where 0.025 m and 0.05 m track
each other closely, with minor deviations near the corner regions. With coarser meshes
(≥0.10 m), more pronounced smoothing and a larger displacement of local points are
seen.
At 2 m/s, 0.05 m cells are sufficient to describe the pressure field around the building
in both cross-sections. 0.025 m adds only a minor improvement in the level of detail,
whereas ≥0.10 m yields systematic deviations.
Near-wall grid resolution
y+ increases with distance from the wall, as expected. Cell sizes of 0.025 m at the wall
lie predominantly above y+ = 30 and, for several points, below y+ = 100, although higher
values occur near the front and the corners.
0.05 m cell sizes at the wall typically yield y+ > 100, and in high-pressure regions they
are significantly higher. For cell sizes of 0.10 m - 0.40 m, y+ is consistently above 100.
In horizontal cross-sections, the same tendency is observed. Some points with a 0.025 m
cell size at the wall near leeward zones can approach y+ ≈30, whereas the 0.05 m cells
often lie ≥100. The corners exhibit the highest y+, which is consistent with increased
friction velocity in these zones.
According to Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024a), the first cell should lie in the logarithmic
layer with a practical target of y+ ≈100 (often expressed as 30 – 100 as a conservative
interval). Based on the points in the 0.025 m layer, the figures indicate that values are
often within or just above the 30 – 100 interval at 2 m/s, whereas y+ at 0.05 m is typically
above 100.
The measurement points show y+ at fixed offsets (not necessarily exactly at the first cell
centre), and therefore the final assessment should be based on the first cell y+. The figure
nevertheless provides a solid basis for showing that the optimal spacing at 2 m/s should
be ≈0.02 m – 0.03 m to remain within the interval y+ = 30 – 100 over most of the surfaces.
0.05 m will locally exceed y+ = 100 in pressure loaded zones.
0.025 m largely fulfils the objective of log-layer resolution at 2 m/s. 0.05 m is usable for wall
functions (typically y+ = 100), but in regions of high pressure it will exceed the conservative
interval y+ = 30 – 100. Coarser cell discretisations (≥0.10 m) yield y+ well above 100 and
are therefore unsuitable for wind simulation.
Intermediate conclusion
At 2 m/s, a 0.05 m mesh size is sufficient for cp-validation, whereas 0.025 m provides the
most robust y+ in the logarithmic layer. However, for this low wind velocity, 0.05 m cells
are considered sufficient.

Near-wall grid resolution
y+ increases with distance from the wall (0.025 m ⇒0.40 m), as expected. The 0.025 m
layer cells lie, in most of the cross-sections, around y+ = 100 and above y+ = 30. Locally at
the front and the corners, values close to or slightly above 100 are observed. The 0.05 m
layer cells lie predominantly above y+ = 100 and remain clearly above y+ = 30 across the
entire field. For cell sizes of 0.10 m - 0.40 m, the y+ values are progressively higher, which
is expected and illustrates that the first cell spacing must not be too large if one aims for
conservative y+ values.
According to Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024a), the first cell should be placed in the logarith-
mic layer with a practical target of y+ ≈100 (often expressed conservatively as 30 – 100).
The figures suggest that if the first cell centre lies around 0.025 m, one will, over large ar-
eas, be within or just above the 30 – 100 interval. With 0.05 m cells, the y+ value will often
lie above 100 in heavily loaded zones, especially at the front and the corners of the cube.
0.025 m is generally favourably positioned with respect to the logarithmic layer at 8 m/s.
0.05 m is usable for wall functions, but locally exceeds the y+ = 30 −100 interval. Coarser
first cell spacings (≥0.10 m) are assessed as unsuitable.
Intermediate conclusion
0.05 m reproduces approximately the same curves as 0.025 m in both cross-sections and
can be used for pressure validation. 0.10 m - 0.40 m show increasing deviations and are
assessed as unsuitable for wind simulation.
To keep the first-cell y+ within a conservative 30 – 100 interval, ≈0.025 m is recommended
in the most heavily loaded zones, whereas 0.05 m is acceptable if the target is the loga-
rithmic layer y+ = 100 and computational time is prioritised.

==PAGE:168==
Quantitative analysis of cp and y+ for 14 m/s
Figure C.18 on the facing page – figure C.23 on page 145
Pressure coefficient
All curves reproduce the expected wind profile over the building: stagnation at the front,
a drop around x/L ≈0.35 −0.40, and gradual recovery towards the rear edge of the cube.
The 0.025 m and 0.05 m curves lie very close across the entire profile, with only minor
differences in the reattachment region (x/L ≈0.55 −0.65). The 0.10 m and 0.20 m curves
are less steep and are slightly shifted at the local minimum and maximum. The 0.40 m
curve deviates the most, exhibiting early smoothing and a premature inflection.
The same is observed in the horizontal cross-section, where 0.025 m and 0.05 m track
each other closely both on the stagnation plateau and over the roof. Coarser meshes
(≥0.10 m) smooth the extremes and alter the slope around the corner region.
At 14 m/s, 0.05 m cells are sufficient to describe the pressure field in both cross-sections.
0.025 m primarily adds a marginal gain in detail, whereas cell sizes ≥0.10 m produce
visible, systematic deviations.
Near-wall grid resolution
y+ increases with distance from the wall (0.025 m →0.40 m) and is overall higher than
at 8 m/s, consistent with uτ (and hence y+) growing approximately proportionally with
the wind velocity for the same mesh. The 0.025 m curve lies, over large parts of the
cross-sections, around or slightly above y+ = 100 and clearly above y+ = 30. The highest
values are seen at the front and the corners of the building. The 0.05 m curve lies predom-
inantly above 100 and locally approaches y+ ≈200 - 300 in high-load zones, whereas the
0.10 m – 0.40 m curves are correspondingly higher.
According to Kevin McGrattan et al. (2024a), the practice is to place the first cell within
the logarithmic layer, with a practical target of y+ ≈100 (conservatively often stated as
30 – 100). The figures indicate that if the first cell centre lies near 0.025 m, one will often
hit around 100 at 14 m/s, and in hot spots exceed 100. With 0.05 m cell sizes, y+ will
typically be > 100 and can locally approach ≈300.
0.025 m generally provides a first cell placement close to the desired logarithmic-layer
target of y+ ≈100 at 14 m/s, although values at the front and the corners of the cube
can exceed 100. 0.05 m is often on the high side relative to a y+ = 30 −100 interval, but
may be acceptable if the objective is a broader logarithmic-layer range (≈30 −300) and
computational time is a priority. Cell sizes of ≥0.10 m are assessed as unsuitable if one
wishes to maintain conservative y+ control in the most heavily loaded zones.
Intermediate conclusion
0.05 m reproduces the curves almost identically to 0.025 m in both cross-sections, whereas
cell sizes of 0.10 m – 0.40 m yield progressively larger deviations compared with lower
wind velocities.
y+ is observed to be higher than at 8 m/s for the same cell sizes. If a y+ value in the range
30 – 100 is desired at the first cell, local refinement to 0.02 – 0.03 m should be applied
at the front and corners of the building. With a target of y+ = 100, 0.025 m is generally
appropriate, whereas 0.05 m may be acceptable if small exceedances ≥100 are tolerated
at critical points.

Appendix - Volume Flow
In the following appendix, results are presented that are applied in the main report sec-
tion 4.3 on page 54 with respect to evaluating which of the three parameters (fire size,
wind velocity, and the angle between the two replacement air openings) has the greatest
influence on the volume flow through these openings.

Tabels for mean volume flow
The subsequent table D.4 on the next page–table D.7 on page 184 summarise the re-
sults of the simulations based on statistical key figures for the volume flow through the
replacement-air openings and the smoke ventilation openings in the model. Table D.4 on
the next page presents the flow through Door 1 and Door 2, while table D.5 on page 182–
table D.7 on page 184 show the outflow through the smoke ventilation openings OP1–
OP6. For each opening, the following statistical measures of the volume flow are reported:
• Mean value, ¯x
• Standard deviation, s
• Variance, s2
• Minimum value, xmin
• Maximum value, xmax
• Coefficient of variation, CoV
In addition, table D.7 on page 184 includes the sum of the mean values for OP1–OP6,
denoted as ¯xsum, representing the total exhaust for each simulation.

E
Appendix - Temperature
This section provides additional data for air temperature that are not included in section 4.4
on page 74. The air temperature is measured at 9 points in the building. The following
figures present the measured air temperature in the building, disaggregated by individual
measurement points, with variations in fire size, wind velocity, and door angle. Subse-
quently, the same measurement points are presented using a coefficient of variation.
Temperature - plots
At measurement points 1–2, without wind and with a fire size of 5 MW, the air temperature
is approximately 70 – 90 ◦C, at 10 MW it is about 110 – 140 ◦C, and at 20 MW about 180 –
220 ◦C. Increasing the wind velocity to 8 m/s changes the level moderately, whereas at
14 m/s it changes more markedly, with larger decreases or a greater spread in air temper-
ature, especially at 5 – 10 MW. This suggests increased inflow of cooler air that undergoes
mixing with the hot smoke.
At measurement points 3 – 4, the air temperature is higher compared to measurement
points 1 – 2, particularly at 20 MW, where the air temperature is approximately 200 – 250 ◦C.
With increasing wind velocity, a greater degree of turbulence is observed in the air tem-
perature, at high wind velocity, the air temperature depends somewhat on the door angle,
which can rapidly affect the air temperature via airflows of cooler air.
At measurement point 5, markedly higher air temperatures are generally observed than
at the other points, which can be explained by the point being located within the plume. In
addition, the point exhibits a pronounced peak in the start-up phase, explained by the fire
in the simulation igniting instantaneously, whereas the smoke ventilation ramps up more
slowly.
In the quasi-steady phase at measurement point 5, air temperatures of 140 – 170 ◦C are
observed for a fire size of 10 MW, and 230 – 300 ◦C for a fire size of 20 MW.
With increasing wind velocity, neither an increase nor a decrease in air temperature is
observed at this measurement point, suggesting that the plume likely entrains a constant
amount of fresh air independently of wind velocity.
At measurement points 6 – 7, the air temperature remains relatively unaffected, in the
range 200 – 240 ◦C for a 20 MW fire size and 130 – 160 ◦C for a 10 MW fire size.
However, with increasing wind velocity, a slight increase in air temperature is observed,
which may be caused by the wind advecting the hot smoke toward this end of the building,
where these measurement points are located.
At measurement points 8 – 9, the lowest and most stable air temperatures are observed
for a fire size of 5 MW and 10 MW, typically in the range 70 – 120 ◦C. For a 20 MW fire
size, a significant increase in air temperature is seen, at approximately 170 – 210 ◦C.
The common feature of measurement points 8 - 9 and compared to the other measure-
ment points is that they are less sensitive to changes in the angle of the door.
The size of the fire is the clearly dominant parameter when air temperatures are analysed
across all measurement points. It is evident that with increasing fire size, significantly
higher air temperatures are obtained at the individual measurement points.
Master in Fire Safety
185
==PAGE:212==
The air temperatures are highest near the fire, which is centred in the building, conse-
quently, the air temperatures at measurement points 3 – 4 and 6 – 7 are higher than at
measurement points 1 – 2 and 8 – 9.
At 20 MW, greater variation and higher peak air temperatures are observed, directly at-
tributable to the high fire size, which also gives rise to a greater radiative contribution.
At low wind velocities, rapid stratification and moderately stable air temperatures are ob-
served, and with increasing wind velocity, the overall influence diminishes, however the
largest effect occurs at measurement points located in the immediate vicinity of openings,
as these are affected by cooler air or airflows that carry hot smoke around.
At high wind velocity, more fluctuations are observed in air temperatures; this is consistent
with the turbulence generated by higher wind velocities in the airflows of the building.
The angle of the door has a secondary impact, similar to the wind, when assessed relative
to the size of the fire. However, there is a clear effect that the measurement points along
the boundary with openings are more affected by the angle of the door than those located
farther away.
A door angle of 112° most often yields the highest air temperatures at the building centre,
which may be associated with a balanced air exchange in the building. A door angle of 70°
produces slightly lower air temperatures in the centre, which can be explained by cooler
inflows within the building. A door angle of 150° generally results in a more pronounced
deviation in air temperatures, which may be due to stronger airflows through the building.
In general, the differences between the door angles are smaller than the differences be-
tween the fire sizes, and the influence of the door angle becomes more evident when the
wind is present.
Fire size is the dominant factor for the air temperatures, as an increase leads to a higher
mean air temperature and greater variation in the air temperatures. Wind raises the air
temperature at specific measurement points, depending on the door angle and the turbu-
lence present in the building. However, it can be difficult to determine the specific effect
of wind and door angle, as they are interdependent.

Coefficient of Variation - plots
The attached bar charts show the coefficient of variation (CoV) for the air temperature
measurements at the 9 points (Temp 1 – Temp 9). CoV is defined as the standard deviation
divided by the mean value and is therefore dimensionless. A low CoV indicates a stable air
temperature level (small relative fluctuations), whereas a high CoV indicates substantial
fluctuations, typically caused by turbulence, changing stratification, or periodic mixing of
cold air.
In each figure, the x-axis is grouped by door angle (70°, 112°, 150°), wind velocity (0 m/s,
8 m/s, 14 m/s), and fire size (5 MW, 10 MW, 20 MW). The horizontal dashed lines mark a
“low variability” threshold (≈0,10) and a “high variability” threshold (≈0,30).
As shown in figure E.10 on page 193, most of the simulations exhibit a coefficient of vari-
ation (CoV) below 0,12 – 0,14, which indicates generally relatively stable air temperature
levels.
However, there is a tendency for the highest CoV values to occur with increasing wind
velocity, which is also consistent with increasing turbulence. In addition, maximum values
are observed at measurement point 5, which is in the centre of the fire, and thus does not
produce representative air temperature conditions.
However, no values are observed to significantly exceed 0,30, and thus the air tempera-
ture conditions are considered generally stable within the building.
For temperature measurements 1 and 2, low to moderate CoV values are observed for a
large share of the simulation scenarios. Some cases with high wind velocity are slightly
higher, particularly at 112°, which can be explained by greater flushing with cooler airflows.
For temperature measurements 3 and 4, a consistently low CoV value is observed. These
Master in Fire Safety
191
==PAGE:218==
remain fairly stable and, therefore, it can be inferred that they lie in zones where the air
temperatures are relatively stable.
Temperature measurement 5 exhibits the greatest variability, as it is located in the centre
of the fire. Clear peak values are observed at high wind velocity, perhaps especially at a
door angle of 112°. This is also expected given the unstable interplay between the inflow
of cold air into the plume and the hot smoke at the top of the plume.
Predominantly low CoV values are observed for temperature measurements 6 and 7.
Again, at high wind velocities, somewhat greater instability can be observed, albeit more
uniform than at the measurement points at the other end of the building. This can be
explained by the occurrence of more turbulent airflows at the other end. The lowest CoV
values are seen at measurement points 8 and 9, which are located farthest from the wind
effects at the openings.
Wind velocity is observed to affect the CoV value in that increasing the wind increases
CoV. The tendency is that low wind velocities produce stable stratification in the building
and limited turbulence. With increasing wind, fluctuations occur particularly in the door
zones and in the centre. At high wind velocity, there are clearly more outliers and the wind
is dominant for variability.
112° yields the highest CoV values, particularly for temperature measurement 5. This
suggests that the geometry provides effective throughflow through the openings at this
door angle, creating more unstable stratification caused by turbulence. 70° and 150°
produce a more generally even level of CoV values, albeit with a few outliers, which can
be explained by cooler pulsating airflows.
CoV does not increase systematically with increasing fire size. On the contrary, lower
relative variability is often observed at 20 MW, even though the absolute air temperature
fluctuations may be larger. This is because the mean air temperature is higher, so the
relative fluctuations become smaller.

F
Appendix - Visibility
In this section, the data for visibility are analysed. Visibility is not treated as in-depth as
the volume flow and the air temperature, since it is difficult to identify trends due to the
large, non-quasi-steady fluctuations. The following figures present visibility grouped by
fire sizes in the respective subsections.
Figure F.1, F.2 and figure F.3 on the following page show the visibility in the building,
averaged over 50 s for the quasi-steady period at the end of each simulation. The graphs
are grouped by fire size and include varying door angle and wind velocity.
Figure F.1: Visibility for the different measuring points with a fire size of 5 MW and varying
door angle and wind speed.
It is clear that wind dominates visibility. When the wind velocity increases from 0 m/s
to 8 m/s, visibility decreases by approximately 10–15 m. A smaller decrease in visibility
occurs as the wind velocity rises from 8 m/s to 14 m/s, with a drop of approximately 3–6 m.
This trend is observed for all fire sizes at nearly all points.
At a fire size of 5 MW and without wind, relatively high visibility is observed with a few
dips (caused by the fire). At 8 m/s, visibility becomes more fluctuating, especially at a
door angle of 112° and 70°, whereas at 150° a generally more stable but lower visibility is
seen. At 14 m/s, a more stable visibility is observed independent of the door angle, albeit
at a lower level compared with lower wind velocity.
A door angle of 112° yields the most stable visibility in general, although it is difficult to
determine which door angles are optimal, as this also depends on the wind direction. It
can, however, be concluded that the door angle is sensitive to wind.
Master in Fire Safety
199
==PAGE:226==
Figure F.2: Visibility for the different measuring points with a fire size of 10 MW and varying
door angle and wind speed.
Figure F.3: Visibility for the different measuring points with a fire size of 20 MW and varying
door angle and wind speed.

Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:227==
5 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in visibility for a fire size of
5 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door angles (70°, 112°, 150°)
and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows the temporal development
for different combinations of these conditions, from which a number of important trends
regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
Overall, visibility is high and relatively stable without wind, after which it drops markedly
when wind is applied and decreases further at 14 m/s. The door angle affects both level
and stability, the 150° curve often lies lowest in wind, whereas 112°, and at times 70°,
yields higher and more stable visibility.
150° (short-dashed): During the wind periods (8 m/s and 14 m/s), this curve often lies
lowest, particularly evident in VIS 1–4 and 6–9. This can be interpreted as the door posi-
tion in the 150° configuration more strongly channelling smoke towards the measurement
points or more effectively reducing the smoke exhaust. The curve is also characterised
by pronounced short-lived dips, indicating unstable airflows and turbulence pockets.
112° (solid line): This door angle consistently delivers the most stable time series and,
in many figures, lies higher than 150°, often also on a par with or slightly above 70°.
Particular in VIS 1, 3, 4, 6, 7, 8, and 9, 112° emerges as a robust compromise with less
spread in level and fewer deep dips under wind effects.
70° (dashed): The 70° curve can, in some figures (e.g. VIS 2, 3, 4 and 8), lie among the
best at 8 m/s, but it is often more fluctuating, at times even high in one wind phase and
low in the next. This suggests that 70° may be sensitive to small variations in smoke/air
distribution and wind incidence, that is, good performance in periods but less predictability.
0 m/s (before Wind effects): In most VIS plots (1, 2, 3, 4, 6, 7, 8, 9), the curves lie close to
30 m with only small, short-lived dips. This suggests that smoke ventilation and/or natural
stratification provide a relatively clear atmosphere at the measurement points as long as
the external driving forces are small. A clear exception is VIS 5, where visibility is very
low early on (down to a few metres) and only later rises, this points to a local recirculation
zone or a geometry near the fire source where smoke accumulates until the flow field
reorganises.
8 m/s: Immediately after the transition to the wind, a rapid drop in visibility is observed at
all measurement points. The time series are characterised by large fluctuations during
the first 50–150 s, after which the curves settle at a lower but more stable level. At 8 m/s,
typical values lie in the range 15–25 m, though lower in VIS 2 and VIS 5, where dips
decrease toward 10–15 m occur. This indicates that the wind drives the smoke to the
measurement points and partially counteracts the desired smoke ventilation.
14 m/s: The higher wind further worsens the conditions. In many plots, visibility increases
to around 8–15 m, in the most exposed time series, periods below 10 m are observed. At
the same time, the difference between the door angles becomes more apparent. 150°
often produce the lowest levels, while 112° and 70° typically lie higher.
VIS 1–4: These four points exhibit a broadly similar pattern: high visibility without wind,
followed by a rapid drop at 8 m/s and a further decline at 14 m/s. In all four, 150° is
consistently the weakest configuration during the wind periods, while 112° and 70° peak,
although with varying relative ranking.
VIS 5: Quite distinct. At 0 m/s, visibility is very low in the early phase, after which it
temporarily improves. When the wind increases to 8 m/s, a brief redistribution is seen,
Master in Fire Safety
201
==PAGE:228==
but at 14 m/s the level falls again and ends among the lowest in the entire set. This
indicates that the location of VIS 5 lies in or near a smoke-laden zone, which is strongly
influenced by both the fire plume and the incoming wind.
VIS 6–9: Represent the behaviour in VIS 1–4, but with a tendency for 112° to be the best
more consistently in both wind phases. In several of these points, the curves converge
towards the end of the 14 m/s period to a narrow interval of about 8–12 m, which may
indicate that the system reaches a more steady but relatively smoke dense operating
state under strong wind.
Wind is the dominant driver of reduced visibility. Without wind, the levels are high (≈30 m)
at most points. Already at 8 m/s, visibility is markedly reduced, and at 14 m/s several points
drop to around 8–12 m with frequent dips.
The door angle matters. 150° often appears as the most unfavourable scenario under
wind, whereas 112° generally yields higher and more stable levels. 70° can be competi-
tive, but is more fluctuating and location sensitive.

wind speed.

10 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in visibility (VIS) for a fire
size of 10 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door angles (70°,
112°, 150°) and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows the temporal
development for different combinations of these conditions, from which a number of im-
portant trends regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
150°: Again, most often the weakest configuration under the wind. This is evident in
VIS 1, 3, 4, 6, 7, 8 and 9, where the 150° curve lies lowest in both the 8 m/s and 14 m/s
phases and exhibits the deepest short-lived dips. The interpretation is that this orientation
more strongly channels smoke over the measurement points or effectively reduces smoke
exhaust.
112°: At 10 MW, it emerges as the most robust and stable angle in the majority of points.
It consistently resides above 150°, and is often on a par with or above 70°. Particularly in
VIS 2, 112° stands out by maintaining clearly higher visibility than the other two angles at
both 8 m/s and 14 m/s.
70°: In several figures, it delivers competitive levels and in some cases the best but is
more fluctuating. This is most pronounced in VIS 5, where 70°, after the onset of wind to
8 m/s, remains significantly higher than both 112° and 150° for an extended period before
gradually declining.
Without wind (0 m/s), many measurement points remain close to 30 m, but compared to
5 MW earlier and deeper dips are observed, especially in VIS 1 and VIS 2, as early as 400–
500 s. This reflects that the stronger fire plume generates more pronounced pulsation and
recirculation, which periodically brings smoke pockets down to the measurement level.
At 8 m/s, visibility drops rapidly and markedly in all figures, typically to 15–22 m at the
“better” points and down to around 8–12 m at the more exposed ones (e.g. VIS 3, VIS 4
and VIS 6). The transition is accompanied by strong transient fluctuations during the first
50–150 s after the onset of the wind.
At 14 m/s, the level decreases further and stabilises at many points around 5–10 m, with
short dips below 5 m in the most exposed time series. This indicates that the combination
of strong wind and a 10 MW fire size locally overrides the smoke ventilation at times.
VIS 1: At 0 m/s, early dips are observed (from ≈300 s), after which 8 m/s reduces visibility
to 12–20 m with distinct pulsations. 14 m/s stabilises around 6–10 m. 150° is consistently
lowest, 70° and 112° are close, with a slight advantage to 112° towards the end. VIS 2:
Pronounced separation between door angles under wind. At 8 m/s and 14 m/s, 112° lies
clearly higher (often 15–22 m), whereas 70°, and especially 150°, drop to 3–10 m with
frequent dips.
VIS 3: After the onset of wind, a large drop is observed, at 8 m/s, 150° lies lowest (down
towards 5–10 m), while 70°/112° maintain 8–15 m. At 14 m/s, all are forced lower, with
150° again lowest (4–6 m). VIS 4: All three door angles converge at 8 m/s to approximately
10–15 m, and at 14 m/s to approximately 5–8 m. Small angle differences, but 112° is
marginally best and more stable.
VIS 5: Quite distinct. Initially (0 m/s) visibility is very low with slow improvement and large
fluctuations. At 8 m/s, 70° surprisingly maintains a higher level (periodically 10–20 m),
while 112° and 150° quickly drop to 3–8 m. At 14 m/s, all three decline and stabilise at a
low level (typically 3–7 m). This point indicates a local recirculation zone, where the wind
Master in Fire Safety
207
==PAGE:234==
direction and the angle of the door can change the situation between ’usable’ and ’very
poor’ visibility.
VIS 6: Close to the VIS 4 pattern. At 8 m/s, all three drop to 10–18 m; 14 m/s yields
5–10 m. 70° often lies slightly higher in the 8 m/s phase, but the differences are small.
VIS 7: Rapid drop at 8 m/s and again at 14 m/s. 150° is consistently the lowest, 70° can
temporarily yield slightly higher values after the onset of wind, but towards the end 112°
is the most stable.
VIS 8: At 8 m/s, a clear level shift to ≈12–18 m is observed, 150° is lowest, 70° is most
often highest immediately after the onset of wind, after which the curves gradually con-
verge. At 14 m/s, all end up around 6–10 m. VIS 9: Clear separation at 8 m/s: 112° lies
highest (often 15–22 m), 70° between 12–18 m, and 150° around 10–15 m. At 14 m/s, all
fall to ≈8–12 m, with 150° still the lowest.
Fire size increases sensitivity to wind. Compared with 5 MW, earlier dips are observed
even without wind, and larger declines when the wind increases. At 14 m/s, most points lie
down at 5–10 m, indicating that smoke ventilation cannot locally maintain a “clear” layer.
112° is generally the most robust choice. Particularly in VIS 2 and VIS 9, 112° maintains
significantly higher visibility under both wind levels. This suggests that this door orientation
more often supports effective smoke exhaust and limits smoke ingress. 150° should be
avoided in wind. It consistently yields the lowest visibility with more frequent deep dips
across points, which can be critical for orientation and evacuation. 70° can be favourable
locally but is unstable. In VIS 5, 70° is the only setting that, for an extended period after
8 m/s, provides usable levels, but this reverses later and at other points.
Figure F.13: Visibility for measuring point 1 with fire size 10 MW and varying door angle
and wind speed.
