20 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in visibility (VIS) for a fire
size of 20 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door angles (70°,
112°, 150°) and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows the temporal
development for different combinations of these conditions, from which a number of im-
portant trends regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
150° (short-dashed) is again the most unfavourable setting under wind. It consistently lies
lowest in the majority of points (particularly evident in VIS 1–4 and VIS 7–9) and exhibits
the deepest short-lived dips when the flow field reorganises during wind changes.
112° (solid line) is consistently the most robust angle: higher mean values and lower
variance than 150°, and often also better than 70°. At several points (e.g. VIS 2, 3, 8, 9),
112° maintains a 1–3 m better steady level than the other two angles at both 8 m/s and
14 m/s.
70° (dashed) can deliver the best values for short periods (e.g. early 0 m/s in VIS 6 and
parts of VIS 5), but is the most fluctuating. It appears sensitive to small changes in recir-
culation, and the advantage disappears most often as the wind increases.
Without wind (0 m/s), an earlier and faster drop from ≈30 m is observed than in both
5 MW and 10 MW. Already around 150–250 s, many curves begin to leave the “ceiling”
and stabilise in the 15–25 m range, with a few points briefly lower. Clear oscillations
appear in the otherwise calm phase, consistent with a stronger fire plume periodically
drawing smoke pockets down to the measurement level.
At 8 m/s, a pronounced level shift occurs in all figures. After 1–2 minutes of transient
fluctuations, visibility typically drops to 6–12 m; in more exposed points, longer periods at
3–8 m occur.
At 14 m/s, the system is pressed further, most measurement points lie in the stationary
tail around 3–6 m, with short dips even lower. Differences between door angles persist,
but the dominance of wind causes all three curves to end relatively close together and at
a low level.
VIS 1: An early drop already at 0 m/s, the transition to 8 m/s brings the level down to ≈6–
10 m, and 14 m/s to ≈4–6 m. 150° lowest, 112° consistently highest towards the end.
VIS 2: Pronounced separation between door angles under wind. 112° typically lies 2–5 m
above 70° and 150°. At 14 m/s it ends at ≈4–6 m for 112° and lower for 150°.
VIS 3: 150° drops early, already at 0 m/s. At 8 m/s, 150° often lies at ≈5–8 m, whereas
70° and 112° hold ≈7–12 m. At 14 m/s, all are forced into the low tail (≈3–6 m), with 150°
consistently lowest. VIS 4: Uniform pattern, gradual decline at 0 m/s to ≈12–18 m, then
≈6–8 m at 8 m/s and ≈4–6 m at 14 m/s. Small differences between door angles, 112°
marginally better.
VIS 5: Continues to stand out. At 0 m/s, large fluctuations are observed and at times a
higher level for 70° than for 112° and 150°. After the onset of wind, all three decline, but
70° remains 1–3 m higher than the others for periods before the curves converge at a low
level (3–5 m). The point indicates a local recirculation zone that responds differently to
door angle and wind.
VIS 6: 70° remains near ≈30 m for a long time in the calm phase, while 112° and 150°
already decline, an example of the angle sensitivity mentioned earlier. In wind, all three
end up around ≈5–8 m, 112° appears to be the most stable. VIS 7: Pattern similar to VIS
Master in Fire Safety
213
==PAGE:240==
4 and 6, a clear drop at 0 m/s, a further decline at 8 m/s, and a low tail at 14 m/s. 150°
lowest, 112° and 70° close, with small advantages to 112° in the later part.
VIS 8: From ≈250 s, visibility in the calm phase declines to ≈16–22 m. 8 m/s yields ≈8–
12 m, 14 m/s ≈4–6 m. 112° is consistently the highest. VIS 9: Similar to VIS 8. Clear
separation at 8 m/s (112° highest, 150° lowest), at 14 m/s all converge at a low level, but
112° retains a slight edge.
The system is dominated by the wind and under pressure, even without wind, many points
lie below 20–25 m after a short time, at 8 m/s the majority drop to 6–12 m, and at 14 m/s
typically 3–6 m. This indicates that, in the scenario 20 MW, the ventilation of the smoke is
locally overridden by the plume and the wind.
112° remains the most robust choice, consistently delivers the highest and most stable
levels under wind, although the difference from the other angles diminishes at 14 m/s.
150° should be avoided in the wind, consistently lowest and most fluctuating, with frequent
dips below 5 m during transition periods. 70° can help in specific zones (e.g. VIS 5, early
0 m/s in VIS 6), but is generally unstable and loses its advantage at high wind.

G
Appendix - Smoke Layer Height
In this section, the data for smoke layer height are analysed. Smoke layer height is not
treated as in-depth as the volume flow and the air temperature, since it is difficult to iden-
tify trends due to large, non-quasi-steady fluctuations. The following figures present the
smoke layer height grouped by fire sizes in the respective subsections.
Figure G.1, G.2 and G.3 show the smoke layer height in the building, averaged over 50 s
for the quasi-steady period at the end of each simulation. The graphs are grouped by fire
size and include varying door angle and wind velocity.
Figure G.1: Smoke Layer Height for the different measuring points with a fire size of 5 MW
and varying door angle and wind speed.
Fire size has the greatest effect on the smoke layer height, which decreases with increas-
ing fire size. With increasing fire size, larger fluctuations in smoke layer height are also
observed, which naturally reflects greater soot production and increased turbulence in the
building.
A door angle of 112° emerges as the most robust angle, as the smoke layer height there
generally lies higher compared with the other angles, 70° and 150°. This tendency is most
evident at low fire sizes (5 MW and 10 MW), while at 20 MW it is the fire size that domi-
nates. Low wind velocities yield the highest and most stable smoke layer heights, while
with increasing wind velocity the smoke layer height in the room decreases. However,
it is observed that with increasing wind velocity, the smoke layer height first declines at
door angles of 70° and 150°, whereas at 112° it maintains a higher level in the room.

==PAGE:246==

10 MW and varying door angle and wind speed.

20 MW and varying door angle and wind speed.

5 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in smoke layer height
(SLH) for a fire size of 5 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door
angles (70°, 112°, 150°) and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows
the temporal development for different combinations of these conditions, from which a
number of important trends regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
All graphs show a clear initial decline in the smoke layer height immediately after the
fire starts. This is because the fire rapidly produces smoke that accumulates beneath
the ceiling and displaces the clear height. Thereafter, the smoke layer height stabilises
at a level that depends on the specific conditions. In general, it lies in the range 3–5 m
for large portions of the period, indicating a degree of stability, although with substantial
fluctuations.
The door angle has a marked influence on both the level and the stability of the smoke
layer.
70° (dashed line): The smoke layer height generally lies lower compared with the other
angles. This suggests that a narrower opening yields less effective smoke exhaust, as it
restricts the flow.
112° (solid line): This angle provides the most stable and relatively high smoke layer
heights, especially at lower wind velocities. It can thus be assessed that a 112° opening
represents an optimal balance between air supply and smoke exhaust.
150° (dash-dotted line): The results often show larger variations and periodic drops in
smoke layer height. The wider opening allows for stronger influence of wind conditions,
which in some cases destabilises the smoke flow.
The influence of wind is clearly evident in the time intervals where the velocity is 8 m/s
and 14 m/s.
0 m/s: The smoke layer stabilises more quickly and with relatively small fluctuations. This
shows that smoke ventilation operates most predictably in calm conditions.
8 m/s: Emerging fluctuations are observed and a clear decrease in the mean smoke layer
height for certain door angles. The wind begins to interact with the smoke flow and induces
instability.
14 m/s: Fluctuations become much more pronounced and the smoke layer can sometimes
drop to levels around 2–3 m. This underscores that high wind velocities have a negative
effect on system efficiency, especially with extreme openings (150°).
SLH 1 and 2 show relatively stable profiles with a clear effect of wind velocity. SLH 5
stands out with large fluctuations, probably due to its location within the fire. SLH 6–
9 exhibit more uniform time series, yet still with a tendency towards lower smoke layer
heights at 14 m/s, as the points are located in a zone less affected than SLH 1–4.
Based on the figures, it can be concluded that both the door angle and wind velocity play
a crucial role in the efficiency of smoke ventilation. The most favourable combination
appears to be a door opening angle of around 112° at low wind velocities, where the
smoke layer height remains high and relatively stable.

10 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in smoke layer height
(SLH) for a fire size of 10 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door
angles (70°, 112°, 150°) and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows
the temporal development for different combinations of these conditions, from which a
number of important trends regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
In all figures, the expected initial decline in smoke layer height is observed immediately
after ignition, after which the smoke layer stabilises at a lower level. Compared to the
5 MW case, the “baseline” for 10 MW generally lies lower and with larger fluctuations,
typically around 3–4 m over large portions of the period. This is consistent with higher
soot production and increased turbulence caused by a stronger fire size.
The influence of wind is clear, at 0 m/s, obscuration is lower and stabilisation faster, at
8 m/s, fluctuations increase, at 14 m/s, markedly higher dynamics are seen, with periodic
dips in the smoke layer height in some locations and periodic “purging” in others, depend-
ing on measurement position and door angle.
112° (solid line) stands out in many figures as the angle that produces the highest and most
persistent smoke layer height, particularly from 8 m/s onwards. In SLH 1–2 and SLH 7–9,
the smoke layer is visibly lifted when the wind increases, and the 112° curve consistently
lies above 70° and 150°. This indicates that 112° provides a favourable balance between
supply air and exhaust, so the cross-draught from the wind assists the smoke ventilation
without “short-circuiting” the flow pattern.
70° (dotted line) generally produces a lower smoke layer height than 112°, especially
under wind. A narrower opening limits the volume flow through the opening and increases
226
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:253==
flow resistance, resulting in lower effective exhaust. A notable exception occurs near
the fire (e.g. SLH 5 and partly SLH 6), where 70° often lies higher than 112° in the 0–
8 m/s periods. Here, a smaller opening can locally limit wind driven inflow and mixing
and turbulence in the measurement zone, so the smoke layer appears “cleaner” precisely
there.
150° (dash-dotted line) yields, in many figures, the lowest mean smoke layer height and
the largest fluctuations under wind effects. A very large opening appears to make the sys-
tem more vulnerable to wind driven recirculation and shifting flow directions, this causes
periodic drops in layer height (e.g. several dips towards 3 m or lower in SLH 1–2 and SLH
7–9 in the 14 m/s interval), but also brief “purges” where the layer height increases. In
general, performance becomes more volatile.
0 m/s: The smoke layer drops quickly from the ceiling and stabilises with relatively small
fluctuations. The door angle ordering is most often 112° ≥70° > 150°, but the differences
are moderate and strongly position dependent.
8 m/s: In several measurement points (SLH 1–2, SLH 7–9), the 112° curve is gradually
lifted to around 4,5–6 m with distinct pulsations, while 70° and 150° typically lie 0,5–1,5 m
lower. In the more central points (SLH 3–4), the curves converge around 3,5–4 m, sug-
gesting that the net wind effect there is smaller, either because the space is more decou-
pled from the opening’s cross draught, or because local turbulence “eats” the gain.
14 m/s: The effect becomes further polarised. Where the flow “couples correctly” (SLH
1–2, SLH 7–9), 112° remains the clear winner with a higher mean height and occasional
peaks above 6 m, whereas 70° can dip towards 2,5–3 m. Closer to the fire (SLH 5), 14 m/s
often produces strong, rapid variations, and the ranking can change. 112° can there
become the lowest due to strong mixing and plug-holing tendencies in the near field of
the opening.
SLH 1–2: 112° benefits most from wind; the smoke layer height is lifted markedly at
8 m/s and 14 m/s. 70° and 150° follow below. SLH 3–4: The curves are closer together,
112° is only slightly better than 70° and 150°. Wind mainly increases fluctuations, not
necessarily the mean height. SLH 5: Atypical ranking, 70° is often highest at 0–8 m/s,
while 112° can be lowest in strong wind. This points to a local flow in which a large
opening actually increases turbulence precisely there. SLH 6: The transitional picture
between SLH 5 and the central points, 70° starts high but converges. SLH 7–9: 112° is
clearly the best, especially at 14 m/s, where the smoke layer height is maintained highest
despite the greater dynamics.
At 10 MW, the smoke layer height generally lies 0,3–0,7 m lower and is more sensitive to
the wind. In contrast to the 5 MW case, a positive wind effect is observed in several loca-
tions for 112°, where the increased wind actually raises the smoke layer height (because
of better ventilation) in large parts of the space, while 70°, and especially 150°, become
more unstable. However, near the measurement point (SLH 5), however, the picture is
the opposite, here a smaller door angle (70°) can, at times, yield the highest local smoke
layer height.
112° is the most robust overall, particularly under wind and yields the highest mean smoke
layer height in the majority of measurement points. 70° performs best locally near the
opening in weak wind, but loses ground in high wind and in remote areas. 150° produces
the greatest variability and, most often, the lowest layer height under wind effects. Large
openings make the system more vulnerable to wind driven recirculation.

20 MW plots
The graphs shown in the present section illustrate the variation in smoke layer height
(SLH) for a fire size of 20 MW under different conditions. The parameters varied are door
angles (70°, 112°, 150°) and wind velocities (0 m/s, 8 m/s, 14 m/s). Each figure shows
the temporal development for different combinations of these conditions, from which a
number of important trends regarding the efficiency of smoke ventilation can be inferred.
Compared with 5 MW and 10 MW, the smoke layer height drops faster and stabilises at a
lower level. For large parts of the time series, a mean level around 2,5–4 m is observed,
depending on measurement position, wind, and door angle, and with larger fluctuations
than at the lower fire sizes. This is consistent with a 20 MW fire size producing more
smoke and generating stronger turbulence, which more readily disrupts stratification.
At 0 m/s, a roughly stable layer is established, whereas 8 m/s, and especially 14 m/s,
markedly increases the dynamics. Periodic “purges” occur, where the smoke layer height
is briefly lifted, but there are also frequent dips towards critical heights, particularly for
unfavourable combinations of a large opening and high wind.
112° yields, across many figures, the highest and most robust smoke layer height, es-
pecially when wind is present. The angle appears to balance inflow and exhaust, so the
cross draught from the wind assists the smoke ventilation without inducing pronounced
“short-circuiting”. At measurement points that lie in the part of the room which “couples”
well to the cross draught (e.g. SLH 1–2 and SLH 7–9), 112° generally lies above 70° and
150° at both 8 m/s and 14 m/s.
70° typically yields lower smoke layer heights than 112° under wind, because the narrower
opening limits the volume flow through the façade. An important exception is near the
232
Master in Fire Safety
==PAGE:259==
opening (e.g. SLH 5 and partly SLH 6), where 70° can at times lie higher than 112° at
0–8 m/s. Here, the smaller opening can damp wind driven inflow and turbulence in the
near field, so the smoke layer height locally appears favourable, but the effect diminishes
or reverses at 14 m/s.
150° is often the most volatile solution. The large opening makes the system more vulner-
able to wind driven recirculation and shifting flow directions, observed as large fluctuations
and frequent dips in smoke layer height at 8–14 m/s. In some locations, 150° can yield
short periods of effective “purging”, but overall the mean height is typically lower than with
112°.
0 m/s: The smoke layer drops quickly from the ceiling and stabilises with relatively small
fluctuations. The ranking is often 112° ≥70° > 150°, but the differences are moderate
and strongly measurement position dependent.
8 m/s: The wind amplifies the effect of the door angle. In zones well coupled to the cross
draught, 112° lifts the smoke layer (often 0,5–1 m above 70° and 150°), whereas 70° and
150° remain lower and more unsteady.
14 m/s: The dynamics increase further. In more distant zones (SLH 1–2, SLH 7–9), 112°
typically maintains the smoke layer height best, while 70° and 150° experience frequent
dips. In the near field at the opening (SLH 5), strong wind can cause pronounced mixing,
so even 112° temporarily becomes lowest, here the ranking is more variable.
SLH 1–2 (upstream/further from the opening): 112° utilises the wind best and yields the
highest mean smoke layer height at 8–14 m/s; 150° fluctuates the most.
SLH 3–4 (more central): The curves lie closer together, and wind does not necessarily
raise the mean height, it primarily increases fluctuations. 112° still has a slight advantage.
SLH 5 (in the Fire): Atypical ranking. At 0–8 m/s, 70° is often highest, whereas 14 m/s
can reverse the picture and force all angles low due to strong mixing and a tendency to
“short-circuiting”.
SLH 6: Transitional between the near field and the mid-zone, 70° starts relatively high but
converges towards the other angles under wind.
SLH 7–9 (more distant zones): 112° is clearly best under wind, 150° shows the largest
fluctuations, especially in the 14 m/s period.
It is clearly observed that with increasing fire size, the mean smoke layer height decreases.
There is also a clear tendency for a door angle of 150° to exhibit greater wind sensitivity,
as the fluctuations are larger. In general, a door angle of 112° yields the most acceptable
smoke layer height.
112° is the most robust angle overall, especially in zones that are well coupled to the cross
draught (SLH 1–2, SLH 7–9). 70° can be advantageous locally near the opening in low
wind, but loses ground at high wind and in more distant zones. 150° yields the greatest
variability and most frequently low layer heights under win, the large opening promotes
recirculation and “short-circuiting”. 14 m/s is critical: several measurement points show
periodic dips towards ≈2–3 m, which may be below the desired clear heights for life safety.
